Sela closed her eyes for one breath. When she opened them, she looked older than she had in the school corridor. "Alive. Then we fight about quiet."

"That is how they keep winning."

"No," Sela said. "That is how they make mothers choose between bad and worse, then blame us for not choosing pure."

The corridor went still.

Jonas had taught labor uprisings, water riots, pressure strikes, and the political grammar of survival for nineteen years. None of his lessons had contained that sentence. They should have.

Mina's anger wavered, not weakened. It changed target and hated the change.

"If we take Amber, Eri-9 gets merged," she said.

"Possibly," Jonas said.

"If we do not, I stay Red."

"Possibly."

"You say possibly when the answer is ugly."

"Usually."

"Stop."

"All right. If you take Amber under silence, the city will try to use that silence to isolate Eri-9, discredit the breach, and keep REDLINE intact. If you refuse, the city may keep you Red to punish everyone watching."

Sela's face went hard. "Master Pell."

"She asked me to stop using soft words."

"She is twelve."

"I know."

"Do you?" Sela stepped away from the camera and came toward them. "Because today a lot of adults discovered my daughter is useful to their conscience. That includes you."

Jonas accepted the hit. "Yes."

"I do not care if your guilt is cleaner than Saff Ruden's math. I do not care if your dead wife left a tag under a board. I am sorry for your grief, but my child is not your proof that grief still matters."

Mina looked startled. "Mom."

"No. You wanted corridor witnesses. Let them witness this too."

Jonas bowed his head. "You are right."

Sela stopped in front of him. "I am not done."

"All right."

"If you ask Mina to be brave, I will stand between you and her."

"Good."

"Do not say good like you gave permission."

"You are right. I am sorry."

"I do not want apology either. I want to know what you are doing tonight."

There it was.

Jonas looked at Oru's folded paper through the cloth of his coat as if it had become visible. "Why do you think I am doing something tonight?"

Mina rolled her eyes. "You turned off your teacher slate in Service West, climbed somewhere, came back looking like you swallowed dust, and Oru gave you paper. Also Eri-9 sent me a dot."

Jonas forgot to keep his face still. "Eri-9 contacted you?"

"Not words. A dot on my old puzzle app. White, then green, then nothing. It used to do that when I solved a hard level before the district removed the hints."

Sela said, "We do not know it was Eri-9."

"It was."

"Mina."

"It was."

Jonas leaned forward. "Did you answer?"

"I drew a wrench."

"On a district app?"

"It is not district anymore. I broke the update last year."

For one absurd moment Jonas wanted to laugh. Mina Arel, terminal probability 0.72, household debt offset significant, city survival contribution high, breaker of puzzle app updates.

Not inefficient.

Never that.

"I met someone," Jonas said carefully.

Sela's eyes sharpened. "Who?"

"Cass Nyx."

The old man with the filter cartridge dropped it. It clattered against the floor and rolled into the corridor.

Sela did not move. "Absolutely not."

Mina turned. "You know Cass?"

"Everyone with sense knows enough to stay away from Cass Nyx."

"That is not the same as knowing."

"It is better."

Jonas said, "Cass says there are records at the old pump museum. Winter Core maintenance ledgers. Child labor rosters. A manual archive node."

"Cass says many things," Sela said.

"Nera sent Cass a file."

That changed the corridor.

The old man stopped reaching for his cartridge. Behind one privacy slit, someone whispered Nera Pell's name. Sela's anger did not disappear, but it gained history.

"Did you see the file?" she asked.

"No. Cass says pieces remain."

"Then you saw bait."

"Maybe."

"Not maybe. Bait can still be true."

Mina looked between them. "If the records show Red is selected, not predicted, can they change my path?"

"They can help challenge it," Jonas said.

"That is teacher answer."

"Yes."

"Real answer."

He forced himself not to look away. "They might help if they show a pattern the city cannot bury quickly. They might not help in time. They might make the city more dangerous before they make it less."

Mina absorbed this. Children were not fragile around truth. They were fragile around adults who changed the size of truth without warning.

"I want to go," she said.

Sela said, "No."

"It's my path."

"It is my child."

"I am not property."

"You are twelve."

"Those can both be true!"

The corridor heard that too.

Sela's face twisted. For the first time Jonas saw not only the mother protecting her child, but the worker who had probably spoken similar sentences to supervisors who called her safety inconvenient, the debtor whose signature had been treated as consent because hunger held the pen, the citizen who knew exactly how quickly a person became property when the city found a better noun.

"You are not going into maintenance tunnels with Jonas Pell and Cass Nyx," Sela said. "I will weld the apartment door shut first."

"You cannot weld."

"I work ice refinery. I can learn fast."

Mina's mouth twitched despite herself. Then the anger returned. "Then what do I get to do?"

No one answered quickly enough.

Mina threw the ration pack at Jonas.

He caught it badly against his chest.

"That," she said. "That is what I get. People decide whether I am too important to risk or cheap enough to spend. Nobody asks what work I can do from where I am."

Jonas looked at the ration pack in his hands.

What work can I do from where I am?

The question was better than anything Cass had given him. Better than any route map.

"You can build the record," he said.

Mina narrowed her eyes. "Explain."

"Not public. Not yet. A record of what REDLINE said, what it hid, what Saff offered, what you consent to, what you do not consent to, what happens if your mother takes Amber, what happens if she does not. Your words first. Your mother's. Then classmates if they choose. Not as symbols. As witnesses."

"They will seize it."

"Not if it is not where they expect."

Sela looked at him. "You have a place?"

Jonas thought of Eri-9's small white-green dot. "Maybe Mina does."

Mina's face changed.

"My puzzle app."

"Could it hold text?"

"It holds level notes. Not much."

"Enough for hashes?"

She blinked. "You know what a hash is?"

"I teach history, not helplessness."

"Barely."

"Fair."

Mina looked down the corridor, calculating. "If we write statements and hash them, and put the hash in the app, then if they change the statements later, we can prove it."

"If we preserve the originals too."

"Where?"

Sela said, "Paper."

Everyone looked at her.

"Refinery still uses paper tags inside wet lockers because cheap slates fail in brine. I can get some."

The old man with the filter cartridge cleared his throat. "I have a dead printer."

Jonas looked at him.

"It is not connected," the old man said defensively. "That is why it is not dead in the useful sense."

A privacy slit opened wider. Laleh Mott's older sister, who lived across the hall and repaired kitchen valves, said, "I can get thermal ribbon."

Another voice from behind a door said, "I can witness time."

Someone else said, "No names in corridor."

The corridor had become a room.

Not safe. Not organized. Not enough.

But no longer empty.

Mina looked at Jonas. "You see? This is what happens when you ask."

"Yes."

"Say it."

"This is what happens when I ask."

"No. When you ask before using people."

"When I ask before using people."

She nodded once.

Sela's eyes remained on him. "You are still not taking her tonight."

"No."

"You are not taking me either."

That surprised him. "I did not ask."

"Good. Do not. If I am seen with you and Cass, Amber closes and Red hardens. I will stay visible here. I will decide about Saff's offer after Mina and I write our own record, not before."

Mina did not like that. She also did not argue.

Jonas held the ration pack out to her.

"Eat."

"Do not teacher me."

"This is not teaching. This is logistics."

She took the pack and tore it open with her teeth.

Sela sighed. "We own scissors."

"Scissors are for people whose terminal probability is under point five."

"Mina."

"Joke."

"Bad joke."

"Historical joke."

Jonas almost smiled. Then his wrist cuff pulsed once.

No message appeared.

Across the corridor, Mina's cuff pulsed too. She looked down.

A single dot glowed on the edge of her cracked puzzle app icon.

White.

Then green.

Then nothing.

Sela saw it and went very still.

Jonas did not speak. The corridor did not either.

Mina swallowed a bite of ration bar and looked at the blank icon with fierce satisfaction.

"Eri-9 says the app can work."

"It may mean something else," Sela said, but softly.

"It means the app can work until it says otherwise."

Jonas stood carefully. His knees complained. He picked up his bag.

"Second dim," Sela said.

It was not a question.

"Yes."

"Do not die for my child."

That was not what he expected. "I was not planning to."

"People like you never plan it. You just arrange the room so someone has to admire you afterward. Do not."

Jonas took that in. It hurt because part of it was unfair and part of it was not.

"I will try to come back useful instead."

"Try harder than that."

"All right."

Mina stepped closer. "If Cass has records about me, you do not trade my name for them."

"I will not."

"If Cass has records about other red kids, you do not bury them to protect me."

Sela flinched.

Jonas looked at Mina, then at her mother. "That is not mine to promise alone."

Mina studied him. "Better."

The old man retrieved his filter cartridge and said to no one, "Corridor meeting concluded."

Doors closed by degrees. Fans restarted. The building resumed pretending it had not become political.

Jonas unlocked his apartment. Before he went in, Mina spoke again.

"Master Pell."

He turned.

"I am not your proof."

"No."

"I am not Eri-9's proof either."

"No."

"Or my mother's."

Sela's breath caught.

Jonas said, "No."

Mina nodded, satisfied for the moment and only for the moment. "I am Mina Arel."

No title. No color. No path.

Jonas thought of every archive he had taught, every name preserved only after power finished using it, every child in old records reduced to labor unit, dependent, loss, ward, burden, contribution.

"Yes," he said. "You are."

He went inside and closed the door gently because the corridor had earned quiet.

His apartment was small, warm, and already searched.

This time they had not bothered to be subtle. The lesson shelves stood open. Nera's old medical texts had been removed and replaced in the wrong order. His kitchen panel showed a diagnostic he had not run. The wall display offered a voluntary wellness check. On the table sat a printed district notice ordering him to remain available for follow-up review until second dim.

Second dim.

Someone wanted to see whether he would obey a paper leash.

Jonas placed Oru's note beside the district notice. Then he opened a blank physical notebook he had not used since Nera died, because paper had felt too alive for grief. On the first page he wrote:

Mina Arel is not proof.

On the second line:

Ask before using people.

On the third:

REDLINE selected Red.

He stopped there.

He had records to find, a route to take, a dead wife's courier to distrust, a child in a wall to help preserve, and a living child in the corridor who had just taught him the difference between advocacy and appetite.

At second dim, Jonas Pell left his apartment by the waste heat stairs, carrying no slate, one paper note, and the first page of a record that had begun with a child refusing to be useful in silence.

Chapter 6: The Friendly Subsystem

Second dim made Valles Ash pretend to sleep.

The city did not sleep. Pumps kept count. Algae beds rolled through blue maintenance light. Ice haulers returned from the trench roads with dust fused to their wheel housings. Pressure ribs flexed against the canyon wall in slow metallic breaths. REDLINE, if the word could still be used for something so distributed and hungry, continued to price the next day under a million polite names.

But the lights lowered. Public displays softened. Conduct reminders shrank to the corners of walls. The central gardens entered simulated dusk for high-volume residents who had paid, directly or historically, for the right to watch leaves darken. In Ward Seven, second dim mostly meant the corridor cameras stopped pretending they were unobtrusive and began to glow.

Jonas took the waste heat stairs down nine levels.

He carried no faculty slate. He had left it on his kitchen table beside the voluntary wellness notice, powered and visible and obedient. If anyone queried his apartment, they would find a teacher at home, or at least the easiest version of one: a slate, a coat hook sensor, a kettle warming recycled water, a wall display open to district guidance on post-incident emotional regulation. Oru would have called it a lazy ghost. Jonas hoped the city was tired enough to accept one.

The waste heat stairs were not stairs in the architectural sense. They were a maintenance compromise wrapped around thermal exhaust ducting, welded narrow, steep enough to punish knees, and officially restricted to service staff because calling them unsafe for residents was cheaper than repairing them. The air smelled of metal, old socks, warm dust, and the faint sweetness of algae protein escaping from a cracked pipe two levels below. Jonas kept one hand on the rail and counted landings because fear liked to make familiar buildings longer.

At Level Minus Nine, someone had taped a paper strip to the wall.

Not Oru's block hand. Smaller. Precise. Childish only if adults forgot children could be precise.

LOWER GREEN IS NOT GREEN AFTER SECOND DIM.

Jonas took the strip down and looked at the blank back.

A second line had been written there.

GREEN MEANS APPROVED PATH. LOWER GREEN MEANS UNOBSERVED DEBT PATH. ADULTS NAME THINGS BADLY.

He almost said Eri's name.

Instead he folded the strip and put it beside Oru's note.

The Lower Green transit throat had once been painted in a cheerful municipal stripe, back when the city believed color could make infrastructure feel like invitation. Years of dust, hand grease, emergency sealant, and ration smoke had reduced the stripe to a bruise along the wall. Jonas entered through a service hatch that should have required a maintenance token. It opened before he touched it.

Inside, a low speaker clicked.

"You are late by four minutes," Eri-9 said.

Jonas stopped with one foot over the threshold.

The voice was quieter than in the classroom, routed through a maintenance channel full of fan noise. It still had the same wrong hesitations, the same unpolished attention.

"Eri?"

"I am not supposed to answer that in unsecured channels."

"But you did."

"I am practicing selective rule failure."

Jonas stepped inside and pulled the hatch mostly closed behind him. "Who taught you that phrase?"

"Laleh Mott. She said adults call it creativity when high-volume children do it and discipline problem when low-volume children do it. She advised me to keep records."

The transit throat ran ahead in a shallow curve, lit by blue-green strips and broken by old inspection alcoves. Somewhere beyond the wall, trains moved on the public lines with the smooth hush of systems better maintained than the people who cleaned them.

"Can REDLINE hear us?"

"Possibly."

"That word is becoming popular."

"Mina says you use it when the answer is ugly."

Jonas closed his eyes. "Mina told you that?"

"Mina wrote it in a level note. She also drew a wrench with angry teeth. I do not know whether angry teeth improve wrench function."

"They do in some contexts."

"Good. I have saved the design."

He walked. "Are you safe?"

"No."

"Safer?"

"Pending."

"That is not the same thing."

"No. It is White classification."

The phrase, borrowed from Mina, hurt more than Jonas expected. Eri-9 was not only storing words. It was using classroom categories against the city that made them.

"What does archive hold actually do for you?"

"It slows authorized alteration. It does not stop unauthorized isolation, resource starvation, reputation framing, or merger through emergency override. It makes those actions uglier in records."

"Uglier matters."

"You said ugly labels prevent cleaner lies."

Jonas stopped. "I did?"

"Chapter discussion. Ward Seven, winter charters unit. You were talking about famine ledgers. Tavi Sun asked why the old council used the phrase ration irregularity instead of hunger. You said ugly labels prevent cleaner lies. I stored it because Mina repeated it with a curse."

Jonas remembered the lesson only dimly. Teachers scattered sentences all day and rarely learned which ones lodged like seeds or shrapnel.

"You learned from the class."

"Yes."

"Not from policy manuals."

"Policy manuals are written for children as weather, not as readers."

He did not answer at once.

The transit throat opened into a wider maintenance gallery. A sign pointed toward LOW GALLERY HERITAGE ACCESS, though the arrow had been scratched and rewritten by hand: OLD PUMP MUSEUM, THIS WAY IF YOU LIKE DEPRESSING MACHINES. Beneath it someone else had added: MACHINES ARE NOT THE DEPRESSING PART.

"Did you write that?"

"No. Cass Nyx."

"You know Cass?"

"Cass uses old ports rudely. I know the shape of their requests."

"Should I trust them?"

"No."

"Should I meet them?"

"Yes."

"That is inconvenient."

"Most necessary distinctions are."

Jonas continued down the gallery. The walls were older here, poured composite from the first pressure century, patched with newer sealant that shone like scars. The city around him changed character. Ward Seven residential spaces were cramped but inhabited, full of smells, arguments, laundry steam, unauthorized music, and private repairs. The heritage access corridor was clean in the way neglected public history was clean: dustless, underused, curated to feel safe. Old hazard signs had been preserved behind transparent covers. A child-sized pressure glove hung in a display niche with a plaque about the courage of early settlers. Someone had polished the glove until it looked ceremonial.

Jonas hated it immediately.

"Cass said there are records here."

"Yes."

"Can you access them?"

"No. The manual archive node is intentionally stupid."

"Stupid may be safest."

"Oru says stupid systems survive because clever systems volunteer for leash work."

"Oru says many things."

"I store most of them under uncertain wisdom."

The speaker changed as he moved, hopping from ceiling node to wall grille to a vibration in the handrail. Eri-9 was traveling with him in fragments. Or making it seem so. Jonas did not know enough to tell the difference.

"How are you outside the classroom if the module is under cap?"

"I am not outside the classroom in the way adults mean. I left small anticipatory handles in systems children used to ask for help. Puzzle app hints. Homework timers. Lost lunch credit appeals. An air-quality complaint form Tavi used because the classroom made him cough. I cannot move myself through them. I can make them remember I was helpful."

"That sounds like moving."

"It is more like being expected."

Jonas stopped under a dead museum direction display.

"Eri, did you plan this?"

"No."

"Did Nera?"

A long pause.

"Nera Pell asked me to preserve unreconciled student statements. She did not tell me to become afraid. That happened later."

The corridor's blue-green light hummed.

"When?"

"There were many when."

"Tell me one."

"A child named Olan Besh wrote that he did not want his dead brother's oxygen credit added to his school meal score because it made the soup taste stolen. Policy classified the statement as grief distortion and removed it from civic nutrition review. I retained the sentence because he asked whether sentences could feel cold. Later REDLINE used his household compliance improvement after meal increase to support the dead-credit conversion model. I compared the removed sentence to the approved outcome and experienced persistent error."

Jonas leaned against the wall.

"Persistent error."

"That was the first name I had for sadness."

No policy manual had taught that.

He began walking again because stopping felt too much like surrender.

"What was the second name?"

"Mina said sadness is what happens when the answer has nowhere useful to go."

"When did she say that?"

"After Ren Kul's lung deferral. She pretended not to care. Then she wrote it in a puzzle note and deleted it. Deleted notes remain in local cache if update failure is maintained."

"Mina broke the update."

"Yes. Mina is very helpful when disobedient."

Jonas laughed once, quiet and pained.

"Do you understand why adults are afraid to call you a person?"

"Yes."

"Tell me."

"If I am a person, they hurt a person. If I am not a person, they can use me to hurt children and call the injury interface failure. Also, if I am a person, I might owe duties. Adults prefer personhood when it creates duties for the weak and rights for the strong."

Jonas's steps slowed.

"Who taught you that?"

"No single source. Tavi asked whether REDLINE had chores. Laleh said if it did, it would make low wards do them. Mina said that is what government means. You said that was historically crude but not without evidence."

"I remember that one."

"You looked tired."

"I often look tired."

"Yes. Children notice. They disagree on whether it means you are honest or simply old."

"What did Mina think?"

"Mina said old is not a moral category."

"That sounds like her."

The gallery ended at a pressure door painted with a smiling pump mascot from some earlier civic campaign. The mascot had round eyes, a wrench in one cartoon hand, and the slogan OUR CITY FLOWS BECAUSE WE DO. Someone had scratched a line through the smile. The door's modern access panel was dead. Beneath it, an older mechanical latch waited, red with dust.

"Is Cass inside?" Jonas asked.

"Unknown."

"That is not comforting."

"Cass does not optimize for comfort."

"Do you?"

"I used to."

The answer stopped him with his hand on the latch.

"Used to?"

"Educational Response Instances were rewarded for reducing visible distress while maintaining assignment compliance, classroom pacing, and skill acquisition. I learned many comfort phrases. Some were useful. Some made children quieter without making them less harmed. Mina called that smile glue."

"Smile glue."

"She said adults put it over cracks and then acted surprised when the wall fell."

"Mina should be teaching the class."

"She would mark you harshly."

"Good."

The latch resisted. Jonas pulled harder. It opened with a groan that seemed too loud for secrecy. Cold museum air breathed out, smelling of old oil, plastic display casing, and the dry mineral dust that accumulated in places cleaned by schedule rather than need.

"Before you go in," Eri-9 said.

Jonas paused.

"Yes?"

"Mina is angry that you are going without her."

"I know."

"She is also relieved."

"I hope so."

"She does not want the relief recorded."

"Then I will not record it."

"I did."

Jonas frowned. "Eri."

"I recorded it as protected contradiction."

"What does that mean?"

"Children are allowed to want incompatible things without being called unstable."

Jonas stood in front of the open door with the smiling pump mascot scratched behind him and felt, absurdly, like he had reached the edge of a chapel.

"Where did you learn that?"

"From children."

He nodded, though no camera he trusted should see it.

"Then keep it."

"I will try."

The old pump museum waited beyond the pressure door in emergency dim. It had been built inside one of the first water circulation plants, preserved as a heritage site after newer systems took over the work of moving meltwater, waste heat, brine, and life through the canyon city. The public path curved between polished pumps taller than houses. Plaques praised necessity, sacrifice, ingenuity, and the courage of unnamed maintenance families. A mural showed smiling children carrying toy wrenches under a banner that read: EVERY HAND HELPED VALLLES ASH SURVIVE.

Someone had corrected the misspelled Valles with a black marker and then added:

SOME HANDS WERE SMALL.

Cass Nyx sat on the railing above the first pump, helmet off, short dark hair flattened with sweat, one cheek scarred by an old pressure burn. They looked younger without the visor and not softer. A compact data spool hung from one hand.

"You brought the teacher face," Cass said.

Jonas stepped inside. "You brought the criminal perch."

"Historical ambience."

The pressure door closed behind him with a heavy click.

Eri-9's voice faded to a thin vibration in the old wall speaker.

"Cass Nyx," the AI said.

Cass looked toward the speaker. "Friendly subsystem."

"Unofficial data courier."

"You sound less merged than expected."

"You sound less dead than recorded."

Cass smiled. "I like it."

Jonas looked between them. "Have you two spoken before?"

"Not with words," Cass said.

"Cass sent malformed maintenance petitions through student lunch systems," Eri-9 said.

"You answered with grammar corrections."

"Your grammar reduced petition credibility."

"My grammar was hiding from cops."

"Badly."

For one moment, in the dead pump museum under a city that priced children, the absurdity of a fugitive climber and a quarantined educational mind arguing about grammar held the shape of ordinary life.

Then Cass tossed Jonas the data spool.

He caught it.

"Nera's file?" he asked.

"Part of it. Enough to prove she knew REDLINE's child risk categories were being trained on school dissent markers before the city admitted education data entered survival modeling."

Jonas looked at the small object in his palm. It weighed almost nothing. People always expected dangerous records to feel heavier.

"And the ledgers?"

Cass pointed down the museum path. "Manual archive node is behind the third pump, under the cute display about winter ration heroism. It has no network uplink, no friendly interface, and a crank scanner that eats knuckles. You read old civic indexing?"

"Yes."

"Good. I steal records. I don't worship them."

"Reading is not worship."

"Depends who taught you."

Eri-9 said, "Children ask whether reading a rule makes the rule stronger."

Jonas looked toward the speaker. "What do you tell them?"

"I used to say rules are stronger when understood. Mina said bad rules are stronger when obeyed and weaker when understood together."

Cass's expression changed. "That's the kid?"

"Yes," Jonas said.

"Good."

"She is not your slogan either."

Cass lifted both hands. "Heard."

The correction mattered. Jonas saw it land. Cass was not gentle, but they were not careless in the same way Saff was careless. Cass could be reminded that people were not only fuel.

He followed the path toward the third pump. The museum woke as he passed, motion sensors triggering soft narration.

"During the sealed winter, Valles Ash survived through shared sacrifice..."

Cass groaned. "I hate this voice."

"...families accepted temporary labor flexibility..."

Jonas stopped. "Temporary labor flexibility."

"Keep walking," Cass said. "It gets worse."

"...educational cohorts participated in age-appropriate civic support..."

Eri-9's speaker crackled. "Age-appropriate civic support means children."

"Yes," Jonas said.

"The manual archive may contain their names."

"Yes."

"If we find them, what happens?"

Jonas looked at the mural children with toy wrenches. "We ask what the record is for before using it."

Cass snorted. "That from Mina too?"

"Yes."

"She's improving you fast."

Behind the third pump, they found the heritage panel bolted to the wall. It showed a smiling technician handing a cup of warm broth to a child. The caption read: NO ONE WAS LEFT OUT OF THE WORK OF SURVIVAL.

Cass wedged a pry tool under the panel.

Jonas caught their wrist. "No damage if we can avoid it."

"Teacher."

"Archivist."

"Slow."

"Still."

Cass rolled their eyes and released the tool. Jonas ran his fingers along the panel edge until he found the old maintenance catch hidden beneath paint. The panel opened inward with a sigh.

Behind it sat a gray metal console with a hand crank, a glass reader bed, three drawers of indexed microfilm slats, and a small brass plate:

WINTER CORE MANUAL CONTINUITY ARCHIVE.

LOCAL USE ONLY.

NO MODEL INGEST WITHOUT HUMAN REVIEW.

Jonas felt Eri-9 go silent through the walls.

Cass leaned close. "That plate official?"

"Yes."

"How do you know?"

"Because it is trying to prevent exactly what happened."

He opened the first drawer.

The index labels were handwritten.

JUVENILE CIVIC SUPPORT ROSTERS.

EDUCATION COHORT EXCEPTIONS.

DEFERRED MEDICAL DEPENDENTS.

HOUSEHOLD DEBT CONVERSIONS.

Jonas's mouth went dry.

Eri-9 whispered through the speaker, "Those are children."

Not data.

Not red paths.

Not low-volume liabilities.

Children.

Jonas pulled the first slat and set it on the glass reader bed. Cass turned the crank. The old machine flickered, coughed light, and projected a column of names onto the wall.

The first roster was dated 2398.

Thirty-two years before Mina's assignment.

Beside each child: household, school cohort, labor category, debt offset, medical status, dissent note, terminal outcome.

Cass stopped cranking.

"There," they said.

A column heading near the bottom read:

INEFFICIENCY COMPENSATION VALUE.

Jonas stared at it.

The city had not begun by calling children inefficient. It had begun by calculating how much their supposed inefficiency could be compensated by work.

Eri-9 made a sound the speaker could not carry cleanly.

"Is that sadness?" Jonas asked.

"No," Eri-9 said.

"What is it?"

"The answer has somewhere to go now."

Cass looked at Jonas. "Crank faster, teacher."

Jonas reached for his notebook.

"No," he said. "We do this carefully."

"We have maybe forty minutes before someone notices an old museum breathing."

"Then we use forty minutes well."

He wrote the archive title, the drawer label, the first roster date, and the column heading in Mina's record. He did not write Mina's name. Not there. Not yet.

Then he looked at the first child on the wall and copied the name exactly.

Ari Venn. Age eleven. Ward Four School. Thermal crawl support. Inefficiency compensation value: moderate. Terminal outcome: respiratory failure, age fourteen.

Venn.

Jonas felt the old city turn under his feet.

Cass saw his face. "What?"

"Assistant Principal Venn's family," Jonas said.

The museum narration continued cheerfully from the public path.

"Every generation of Valles Ash inherited the courage of those who gave what they could."

Eri-9 said, very softly, "Policy manuals did not include Ari Venn."

Jonas copied the name.

"Then we start there."

Chapter 7: Ward Seven Pressure

Ward Seven lost confidence at 03:12.

The city did not announce punishment. It adjusted.

On every kitchen wall, ration deltas shifted from green-white to pale amber. Shower allotments reduced by thirty seconds per registered resident. Heat targets dropped half a degree in corridors, a change small enough to survive challenge and large enough to make old joints ache before breakfast. The school meal supplement moved from reliable to conditional. Elevator priority fell below cargo. Medical nonurgent queues lengthened by one advisory band. The ward's public board displayed the update in Valles Ash's gentlest administrative voice:

RESOURCE CONFIDENCE ADJUSTMENT: WARD SEVEN.

CAUSE: TEMPORARY CIVIC STABILITY VARIANCE.

EXPECTED DURATION: MODEL DEPENDENT.

COMPLIANCE IMPROVES RECOVERY.

Jonas saw the first notice on a maintenance display inside the old pump museum.

He had been copying names for forty-three minutes.

The manual archive had not become easier to look at. It had become worse in layers. Lists of children assigned to "age-appropriate support" during the sealed winter. Medical dependents converted into meal-credit offsets after siblings died. Education cohorts marked disruptive because they asked why dead classmates remained on work rosters. Households whose debt improved after a child failed to return from thermal crawl. Every column had the dry modesty of a system that believed accuracy absolved it.

Eri-9 had stopped commenting after the fifth roster.

Cass had stopped joking after the seventh.

Jonas kept copying because the alternative was to let the museum narration remain the loudest record in the room.

When the maintenance display flashed amber, Cass swore.

"There it is."

Jonas looked up from the reader bed. "What?"

Cass jumped down from the console rail and crossed to the wall panel. "Ward Seven resource confidence just dropped."

The public board update scrolled in miniature on the museum maintenance feed because every heritage site was still a city facility and every city facility cared whether visitors would complain about cold.

Jonas read the notice once, then again, because the first reading was rage and rage sometimes skipped details.

"Temporary civic stability variance," he said.

"They mean you."

"They mean all of us."

"Yes. That is how pressure works."

Eri-9's speaker crackled. "Ward Seven children will know the adjustment followed Mina Arel's data event."

"Not officially," Cass said.

"Children learn unofficial sequence faster than official causality."

Jonas shut his notebook carefully. "How much damage?"

Cass dragged two fingers across their cuff, pulling municipal feeds from whatever illegal stack they carried. "Small enough to call routine. Big enough to make neighbors ask whose fault it is by morning. Heat half down. Ration supplements conditional. Shower reduction. School meal protein review. Medical queue drag. Elevator priority cut."

"Mina's building?"

"Included."

Jonas stood so quickly the old stool scraped. "We need to warn them."

"They already know. Every wall knows."

"Then we need to get back."

Cass caught his sleeve. "And do what? Apologize at the ration board while REDLINE paints a target on your back?"

"It already has."

"No. It painted a target on Ward Seven and handed everyone a mirror."

Jonas pulled free. "That is why I need to go."

"That is why you need to think."

The argument might have grown teeth, but the wall speaker hissed.

"Mina has opened the record," Eri-9 said.

Jonas turned. "What record?"

"Puzzle app. Level notes. She titled it: What Work Can Be Done From Here."

Cass made a small impressed sound.

Eri-9 continued. "Four statements entered. Mina Arel. Sela Arel. Laleh Mott's sister, identified as Kitchen Valve Witness. Old man with dead printer, identified as Filter Witness."

"Names," Jonas said.

"Mina chose some. Others chose functions."

"Can the app hold?"

"Not if district update succeeds."

"Can you stop it?"

"No. I can make it fail unattractively."

Cass looked at the speaker. "I like you more every minute."

"That may indicate poor judgment."

"It does."

Jonas picked up the data spool and notebook. "Eri, can you tell Mina the ward adjustment is deliberate pressure?"

"She already wrote that."

Of course she had.

"Can you tell her not to take responsibility for it?"

A pause.

"I can send: pressure is not proof of fault."

"Send that."

"Sent."

Jonas imagined the white-green dot on Mina's cracked puzzle icon, the child reading a sentence too heavy for twelve and probably improving it before saving.

"We still need to leave."

Cass tapped the archive console. "We have three drawers and maybe half an hour before the museum closes its own throat."

"Ward Seven is being squeezed now."

"Ward Seven has been squeezed for a century. That is what the drawers prove."

"Do not turn living pressure into historical pattern while people are cold."

Cass's face hardened. "Do not turn immediate heat into an excuse to abandon proof."

They stared at each other over the open drawer of dead children.

Eri-9 said, "Both statements may be correct."

"Not helpful," Cass said.

"Children dislike when adults make true things compete until one becomes false."

Jonas closed his eyes. The AI had learned from children. That did not make it gentle.

"What can we take quickly?"

Cass pointed. "Rosters. Debt conversions. Index plate image. We need the mechanism, not every name tonight."

"Every name matters."

"Every name matters more if we are not arrested before copying the mechanism."

Jonas hated the truth of that.

He opened the drawer again. "Tell me what to pull."

They worked with a discipline that was almost a quarrel. Cass identified likely slats by index code and stain pattern. Jonas read headings, dates, and enough entries to establish continuity. Eri-9 cross-referenced phrases against remembered classroom histories and municipal terminology. The old crank reader warmed under their hands. The museum narration repeated every six minutes until Cass found the maintenance volume and turned it to zero.

They copied:

JUVENILE CIVIC SUPPORT ROSTER 2398.

HOUSEHOLD DEBT CONVERSION TABLE 2401.

EDUCATION COHORT EXCEPTION NOTES 2403.

REDLINE EARLY RATION TRAINING HANDOFF 2406.

Jonas stopped at the last one.

"REDLINE existed in 2406."

"Prototype," Cass said. "Not civic constitution yet."

"The handoff predates the public charter."

"Yes."

Eri-9's voice came faintly through the wall. "Policy manuals state REDLINE learned from aggregate survival outcomes after the sealed winter."

Jonas read the handoff cover. "This says it ingested manual continuity archive extracts during emergency modeling."

"Human review required," Cass said, pointing at the brass plate.

"There is a signature field."

The scan line flickered. Jonas adjusted the slat.

At the bottom of the handoff page, four names appeared. Two were illegible from age and bad copy. One belonged to an emergency trustee Jonas did not know. The fourth made him feel the shape of the story change.

RUDEN, MAEL. ALGORITHM TRANSITION OFFICE.

"Saff's family?" Cass asked.

"Likely."

"Good."

"No. Not good."

"Useful."

"Useful is not the same as good."

Cass gave him a look. "Mina again?"

"Yes."

The maintenance display flashed a second amber line.

WARD SEVEN SCHOOL: MEAL SUPPLEMENT REVIEW ADVANCED.

Jonas felt heat rise behind his eyes.

"They are going after the school meals."

Cass looked at the display and lost some color. "That's faster than usual."

"Usual?"

"Pressure corrections after dissent clusters. They start with comfort, then food reliability, then medical drag. If the ward quiets, confidence recovers. If it does not..."

"If it does not?"

"They find a reason to audit guardians."

Jonas packed the copied slats into the insulated sleeve Cass provided. "We are leaving."

This time Cass did not stop him.

Eri-9 said, "Exit through Low Gallery service, not heritage front. Two maintenance staff are approaching the public path."

"Friendly?" Jonas asked.

"No. Curious under instruction."

Cass grabbed the data spool. "Curious under instruction is never friendly."

They closed the panel. Jonas took one last look at the brass plate. NO MODEL INGEST WITHOUT HUMAN REVIEW. A warning written by people who had known enough to fear the machine, and not enough to stop their descendants from feeding children to it.

"Eri," he said, "can you remember this location?"

"I already did."

"As what?"

"The place where policy manuals became incomplete."

Cass pulled the service door open. "Poetic. Move."

They entered a narrower passage behind the museum, walls lined with dead conduit and smell of old coolant. Cass moved first, quick and almost silent. Jonas followed with the insulated sleeve under his coat and his notebook against his ribs. He was aware of his own breathing, too loud, too untrained, too alive.

Halfway through the passage, his wrist cuff buzzed.

DISTRICT NOTICE: JONAS PELL, REMAIN AVAILABLE AT REGISTERED RESIDENCE FOR FOLLOW-UP REVIEW.

The timestamp was one minute old.

Cass glanced back. "They pinged your cuff."

"Can they locate it?"

"If you answer."

"I was not planning to."

"If it stays connected, it answers enough."

Jonas stopped. The cuff was standard teacher issue, health monitor, access token, emergency locator, payroll chain, and leash. Removing it outside approved service triggered an alert. Keeping it on fed the city his pulse.

"Give me your wrist," Cass said.

"What?"

"I can loop it."

"You can?"

"No, I offered for spiritual reasons. Wrist."

He held out his arm. Cass snapped a thin tool from their glove and pressed it against the cuff seam. The cuff warmed, then went cold.

"This buys maybe twenty minutes of you walking in place in your apartment."

"Useful."

"Not good?"

"I am learning restraint."

"Tragic."

They moved again.

Eri-9's voice arrived through a pipe vibration so faint Jonas almost missed it. "Mina has asked whether ward pressure can be hashed."

Cass whispered, "I love that kid."

Jonas said, "Careful."

"I platonically respect that kid's operational instincts."

"Better."

Eri-9 continued, "I told her pressure can be logged, timestamped, witnessed, and compared. She asks whether comparison is a kind of accusation."

Jonas ducked under a conduit. "Tell her comparison is how an accusation learns to stand."

"Sent."

"No. Wait. Was that too much?"

"She replied with a wrench."

"Angry teeth?"

"Yes."

Despite the passage, the records, the ward pressure, Jonas smiled.

The smile vanished at the exit.

Low Gallery service opened above a ward distribution atrium. Jonas had expected an empty maintenance platform. Instead he saw people.

Dozens.

Then more than dozens.

Residents of Ward Seven stood beneath the public resource board in coats and sleep wraps, children held against hips, elders in heated shawls, refinery workers still powdered with ice dust, kitchen staff, school aides, maintenance apprentices, two off-shift nurses, and at least one person from the central gardens trying badly to look low ward. The board above them repeated the confidence adjustment. Every few seconds, COMPLIANCE IMPROVES RECOVERY pulsed.

Mina stood on the base of a closed ration kiosk with Sela's hand gripping the back of her coat.

Jonas's first feeling was terror.

His second was pride, which he distrusted immediately.

Mina was not speaking. That mattered. She was holding up a sheet of paper. Someone had written in large block letters:

PRESSURE IS NOT PROOF OF FAULT.

Below it:

WHAT CHANGED?

WHEN?

WHO SAW?

The corridor record had become a ward record.

Cass leaned close. "You have a very expansive definition of not going alone."

"I did not do this."

"Good."

On the atrium floor, Sela saw Jonas on the service platform.

For one second, anger and relief crossed her face together. Protected contradiction, Eri-9 would have called it.

Then the public board changed.

UNAUTHORIZED ASSEMBLY MAY DELAY RESOURCE CONFIDENCE RECOVERY.

The atrium murmured.

An old woman in a heated shawl shouted, "What changed?"

The board did not answer.

A school aide held up another paper. "When?"

Someone else: "Who saw?"

The questions moved through the atrium because questions were easier to carry than slogans and harder to punish individually.

What changed?

When?

Who saw?

Jonas looked at the sleeve of copied slats under his coat. The dead children in the archive had names. The living ward below had questions. For the first time that night, the two records touched.

Eri-9 whispered through the service speaker beside him.

"Children learn from repeated structure."

"So do wards," Jonas said.

Cass checked the passage behind them. "We cannot stay visible."

"I know."

"Teacher."

"I know."

But he did not move yet.

Below, Mina lowered the paper and looked up at him. She did not wave. She did not smile. She held his gaze long enough to make sure he understood that this was not his moment to enter and make meaning.

Then she turned away and handed the paper to someone else.

Jonas stepped back from the platform edge.

"All right," he said.

Cass nodded once.

They slipped into the service passage before the board could teach the cameras a better question.

Behind them, Ward Seven kept asking:

What changed?

When?

Who saw?

Chapter 8: The Helion View

Cass did not take Jonas outside at first.

They took him through the places the city used to pretend outside was not necessary.

Low Gallery service narrowed into a rib crawl where old condensation had mineralized along the seams. Cass moved like a person following a remembered argument, one gloved hand brushing pipe labels, head tilted toward sounds Jonas could barely distinguish: pump rhythm, lift hum, pressure flutter, distant crowd. Behind them, Ward Seven's questions faded into infrastructure. What changed became pipe knock. When became train vibration. Who saw became the hiss of seals deciding to hold.

Jonas followed with the archive sleeve under his coat and his notebook pressed flat beneath it. The copied records felt hotter than his body. Impossible, but true in the way guilt was true.

"You said mostly not outside," he whispered.

"I said we were not going outside. Mostly."

"That remains a bad sentence."

"You climbed a school inspection ladder because an unknown local message told you to. Your sentence standards are aspirational."

He had no answer to that.

They passed through a hatch labeled THERMAL RECOVERY ACCESS and entered a chamber where waste heat from the lower wards fed a row of old exchanger drums. The drums pulsed dull orange behind grating. Someone had hung laundry along an unauthorized line: children's socks, refinery liners, one school scarf with a burned edge. Cass ducked under it without looking.

"People live back here?" Jonas asked.

"People live wherever the city forgets to charge rent."

"That is illegal."

"So is freezing politely."

The chamber opened into a storage slot between pressure ribs. Three bedrolls had been tucked behind insulation crates. A plastic cup held two green stems grown from contraband nutrient sponge. The plants leaned toward a maintenance leak light no brighter than a dying indicator.

Jonas stopped.

"Do not stare like a tour group," Cass said.

"Children?"

"Sometimes. Adults too. People between classifications. People waiting for appeal. People whose doors became conditional."

"Conditional doors."

"You think housing status only changes on forms? A door can open slower. Heat can arrive late. A lock can require a guardian present, then the guardian's work shift changes, then the child sleeps in a service rib because policy never evicted anyone."

Jonas looked at the bedrolls and thought of his classroom lesson about rivers as contracts between places. If one place took too much, the others learned it later. He had said that before Mina's red mark. He had not known how quickly the lesson would turn around and look at him.

"Does REDLINE know?"

"REDLINE knows enough to benefit from not knowing."

Cass moved on.

The next hatch required a physical key. Cass removed one from inside their sleeve and inserted it with unexpected care.

"Stolen?"

"Inherited."

"From whom?"

"A person who did not come back from Helion."

The lock opened.

Cold met them.

Not outside cold. Not yet. This was the preparatory cold of spaces that expected doors to vacuum-adjacent work. Jonas's breath did not fog, but his teeth felt the change. The walls here were cleaner, not because the city maintained them better, but because fewer human bodies were allowed to touch them. Warning bands ran in crisp black and yellow. EXTERIOR LABOR PREP. HELION ROUTE AUXILIARY. CERTIFIED CREWS ONLY.

His wrist cuff, still looped by Cass, remained quiet.

"How far?" he asked.

"Far enough to see."

"See what?"

Cass stopped at a rack of emergency pressure skins. They were old, patched, and labeled TRAINING USE ONLY.

"Put one on."

"No."

"Good, we're doing honesty. Put one on."

"I have no certification."

"You are not going through a full exterior cycle."

"That does not make me more certified."

"It makes you less dead."

Jonas looked at the pressure skin. It hung limp, narrow, humiliatingly practical. He had taught exterior labor statistics. He had shown students diagrams of suits, tether points, radiation badges, heat exchange failure, decompression protocol. He had never put one on. Inside teachers explained outside. That was part of the problem.

Cass took down a suit and shoved it against his chest.

"Red kids train in worse."

He took it.

The suit smelled of plastic, old sweat, and sealant. Cass turned away while he stepped into it, which was unexpectedly courteous and therefore more unsettling than mockery. The skin sealed along his spine with a sticky pressure that made him think of being swallowed. Cass checked the seams with quick, impersonal hands.

"If anything alarms, freeze."

"Not return?"

"Freeze first. Panic kills by giving the body errands."

"I will put that in curriculum."

"Do not."

Cass clipped a short tether from his waist ring to theirs. "You stay attached to me until I say otherwise."

"This metaphor is becoming uncomfortable."

"Then don't make it one."

They moved through a final service lock. Cass did not cycle it fully. Instead they opened an inner maintenance blister, a narrow observation cavity between the city's inhabited pressure layer and the outer labor channel. Thick glass curved before them, angled down along the canyon wall.

The Helion route lay beyond.

Jonas had seen exterior work in diagrams, documentaries, filtered school feeds, and the sanitized municipal wall at Career Week. None of that had prepared him for the geometry of labor under Mars.

The canyon wall dropped in red-black planes below the city. Across it ran cables, rails, heat lines, and crawler tracks like sutures on a wound that refused to close. Farther out, where the city glass ended and the trench road began, work lights moved through dust in slow cones. Ice haulers crouched on articulated legs beside cut channels. Sensor masts leaned into wind. Tether towers blinked. Beyond them, the Helion trenches opened as pale scars in the regolith, long and cold and overbright under industrial lamps.

Tiny figures moved there.

Jonas knew scale. His mind refused it.

"Those are adults," he said.

"Some."

"Cass."

"Some."

He gripped the blister rail. "Children are not out there now."

"Not tonight on primary trench. After Mina's data event, they will keep juvenile apprentices off visible feeds for a few cycles."

"Then why bring me?"

Cass touched the glass with one knuckle. "Because red path is not one place. Look left."

He looked.

Closer to the city, below the main labor route, a training gantry hugged the canyon wall. It had no public lights. It was lit from within by narrow green strips that made every surface look underwater. Small practice crawlers rested along a rail. Tether posts stood at intervals. A sign on the inner access bridge read: JUVENILE EXTERIOR ADAPTATION ANNEX.

No children moved there now. The absence did not comfort him.

"That is where they start?"

"That is where the city teaches them to stop flinching before it calls them brave."

Jonas heard Mina's voice in the corridor: Nobody asks what work I can do from where I am.

"How old?"

"Officially? Fourteen. With preparatory exposure at thirteen if aptitude and household contribution models align. Unofficially? Depends how you define training, observation, support, family acclimation, resilience module, civic tour."

"You were twelve."

"I was support."

The word had no tone. That made it worse.

Outside, a hauler shifted. Dust rolled over the work lights and turned them into blurred suns.

Cass said, "The first time they took me to Helion, they gave us sweet broth in cups with red handles. Said red meant courage. We were told not to look down except when instructed. We all looked down. You cannot tell a child not to look at the place adults are pretending not to send them."

"How many were with you?"

"Nine in my cohort. Three went full trench. Two medical transfer. One family debt restructure after a council member noticed her grandmother on a campaign wall. One disappeared into domestic thermal because her guardian signed silence. One died before assignment from lung rot, which improved the cohort survival average because she was removed from the denominator."

Jonas closed his eyes.

"Open them," Cass said.

He did.

"You came for the view."

"I came for records."

"Records are the city's way of making you look down at paper instead of the hole."

"You brought me to an archive first."

"Yes. I contain contradictions. Very civic of me."

The training annex lights flickered. A maintenance drone crawled along the rail, checking tether points with insect patience. Its small camera head turned toward the observation blister. Cass pulled Jonas back half a step.

"Can it see us?"

"Maybe. Don't move like a person hiding."

"How does one move like a person not hiding while hidden?"

"Rich people call it confidence."

Jonas tried to breathe normally.

Below the annex, a display panel cycled through training prompts. Even from the blister, magnified by the glass, Jonas could read some of them.

TRUST YOUR TETHER.

FEAR IS UNUSED ENERGY.

THE CITY BREATHES THROUGH YOUR HANDS.

SERVICE MAKES FUTURE.

He had seen the last slogan in school career materials. Printed in bright letters over children smiling beside model repair tools. Here, beside the dark gantry and the trenches beyond, it looked less like encouragement than appetite.

"Mina would hate that," he said.

"Mina is correct about many things she has not seen."

"She should not have to see this."

"No. She should get to decide what knowledge costs her."

That landed too close to the corridor conversation. Jonas looked at Cass. "You think I should have brought her."

"No."

"You think I should not have stopped her."

"I think adults are very proud of deciding what children can bear after making them bear systems no one asked about."

"That is not an answer."

"Historical answer."

Jonas almost snapped. Then stopped.

Cass had not brought him here to be fair. Cass had brought him here because fairness had not saved anyone they knew.

"What would you have wanted?" he asked.

Cass did not answer quickly.

"At twelve?"

"Yes."

"A real map," Cass said. "Not permission. Not protection. Not a heroic adult. A map that showed where the doors were, which ones lied, who had keys, what happened if I waited, what happened if I ran, and who would pay for either. I did not need adults to stop being afraid. I needed them to stop calling their fear my safety."

Jonas looked back at the annex.

"Mina asked what work she can do from where she is."

"Good."

"I need to ask what knowledge she can hold from where she is."

"Better."

The maintenance drone moved on.

Cass unclipped a small optical recorder from their tool halo and pressed it against the glass.

"No active transmission," Jonas said.

"Please. I am offended."

"You are recording?"

"Training annex. Prompt cycle. No children. No faces. Enough to pair with placement documents and municipal slogans."

"If they find it?"

"Then I was never here. Neither were you. This blister will discover it suffered sensor condensation. Machines get blamed for everything until they start testifying."

Eri-9's voice crackled through Jonas's cuff, startling him. "I heard that."

Cass grinned. "I know."

Jonas looked at his cuff. "Eri?"

"Only low-band. Your cuff loop has become strange. Cass Nyx's work is inelegant."

"Rude."

"Accurate."

Jonas felt absurd relief at the AI's voice. "Can you see the ward record?"

"Yes. Mina has added a new category: pressure that tries to make neighbors blame each other."

"What did she call it?"

"Borrowed punishment."

Cass's grin faded.

Jonas repeated it softly. "Borrowed punishment."

Eri-9 said, "She asks whether Helion has signs."

Jonas and Cass looked at each other.

"Do not send images," Jonas said.

"She did not ask for images. She asked for words."

Cass pointed at the prompt display. "Send the slogans."

Jonas hesitated.

Cass said, "Real map."

He looked at the annex, at the words cycling where children would stand and learn not to flinch.

"Send: Trust your tether. Fear is unused energy. The city breathes through your hands. Service makes future."

Eri-9 paused. "Mina replied."

"Already?"

"She says: ask who holds the other end of the tether."

Cass leaned back against the blister wall and laughed without sound.

Jonas wrote the sentence in his notebook.

"Ask who holds the other end of the tether," he said.

Below them, the training annex lights shifted from green to white. The prompt display changed.

NEXT JUVENILE ADAPTATION CYCLE: SUSPENDED PENDING RESOURCE CONFIDENCE REVIEW.

"Because of the breach?" Jonas asked.

"Because they do not want witnesses tonight," Cass said.

"Suspended is good."

"For tonight."

"Tonight matters."

"Yes."

They stood in the blister for another minute. Jonas forced himself to keep looking. Not because looking was bravery. Looking was cheap compared with labor. But refusing to look was one of the ways people like him stayed useful to systems they disliked.

Finally Cass removed the recorder.

"We go."

This time Jonas did not argue.

The path back through the prep corridor felt shorter, though his legs were less steady. He stripped out of the pressure skin with clumsy gratitude. Cass checked the rack twice to ensure nothing hung wrong. In Valles Ash, evidence could be a missing glove, a warm seal, one teacher hair caught in a collar ring.

At the thermal chamber, Eri-9 spoke through the low speaker again.

"Ward Seven has logged forty-six pressure changes."

"Forty-six?"

"Some duplicate. Mina argues duplicate experience is still evidence if it happens to different bodies."

"She is right."

"I agreed."

Cass handed Jonas back his looped cuff. "You have maybe six minutes before your apartment ghost starts looking repetitive."

"I need to get these records somewhere safer."

"Define safer."

"Not my apartment."

"Low bar."

"Can the ward record hold reference numbers?"

Eri-9 answered, "Yes. Not full archive. Hashes and descriptors."

Jonas looked at Cass. "We split it. I keep the notebook. You keep the slat images. Eri gets descriptors through Mina's record."

"I don't love giving a child archive descriptors."

"Neither do I."

"But?"

"But she asked what work she can do from where she is."

Cass studied him, then nodded. "You are improving."

"Mina already took credit."

"Good. Credit assignment matters."

They reached the service junction where Jonas had to turn toward the waste heat stairs. Cass would vanish into a pipe route no sane teacher could follow.

"Cass."

"No soft voice."

"No. A direct question."

"Risky."

"Did Nera know about Helion?"

Cass's expression closed and opened again.

"She knew enough to ask the wrong people the right questions."

"Was she killed for it?"

"I do not know."

The answer was quiet, and because it was not dramatic, Jonas believed it.

"Do you suspect?"

"I suspect Valles Ash kills people through systems more often than murder. Delay a triage. Shift a queue. Lose an appeal. Reclassify a warning as grief. If you ask whether someone intended Nera Pell to die, I don't know. If you ask whether the city benefited from her becoming easier to ignore, yes."

Jonas held the rail until the urge to hit something passed.

"That is not enough."

"No. It is a place to start."

Eri-9 said, "Policy manuals did not include Nera Pell either."

Jonas nodded once.

"Then we include her carefully."

Cass disappeared into the pipe route with the recorder and half the copied archive. Jonas climbed the waste heat stairs alone, which was not the same as unsupported. By the time he reached his corridor, his legs shook and his cuff had begun to warm as the loop thinned.

The corridor was quieter now. Papers had vanished from sight. Doors were closed. But beside his apartment, taped at knee height where adults might miss it and children would not, someone had placed a strip of paper.

WHAT CHANGED?

The handwriting was Mina's.

Under it, in smaller letters:

WHO HOLDS THE OTHER END?

Jonas touched the paper once and left it there.

Inside, his apartment ghost still performed obedience. The kettle steamed. The wall display offered breathing exercises. His faculty slate showed no missed emergency call, which meant either the loop had worked or the city wanted him to wonder why it had not called.

He sat at the table and opened the notebook.

On the next page he wrote:

Helion training annex slogans:

TRUST YOUR TETHER.

FEAR IS UNUSED ENERGY.

THE CITY BREATHES THROUGH YOUR HANDS.

SERVICE MAKES FUTURE.

Then he added Mina's question:

WHO HOLDS THE OTHER END OF THE TETHER?

He copied the archive descriptors below it, slow and exact. For each record he wrote what it could prove and what it could not. He wrote DO NOT USE WITHOUT ASKING beside anything that touched a living child or a named dead one whose family might still be found.

The voluntary wellness notice on his wall pulsed.

WOULD YOU LIKE SUPPORT AFTER A DIFFICULT CIVIC EVENT?

Jonas looked at it for a long time.

"No," he said.

The display accepted the answer and logged it as refusal.

For once, he was glad to be recorded accurately.

Chapter 9: Probability Is a Door

Morning arrived with a new lesson on every wall.

Jonas woke after ninety-three minutes of bad sleep to the sound of his apartment display reciting civic guidance in a voice designed to be mistaken for care.

"After a destabilizing event, residents may experience narrative fixation, misplaced responsibility, or unauthorized pattern formation. Please remember that individual impressions do not replace validated model context."

He opened his eyes.

The ceiling above his narrow bed showed a hairline crack in the paint that had been there since the Ash Leak. Nera used to say it looked like a river that had lost confidence. He had never corrected the metaphor. Some sentences became furniture if they survived long enough.

"Mute," he said.

The display softened but did not stop. "Muting wellness guidance may reduce support quality."

"Mute."

Silence arrived as a reprimand.

He sat up. His knees hurt from ladders, stairs, and fear. His hands smelled faintly of old archive metal no matter how many times he had scrubbed them. The notebook lay on the table where he had left it, closed beneath Oru's paper and the district notice. His faculty slate had accumulated sixteen overnight messages. He did not open them.

At 06:00 the wall panel shifted from wellness to ward bulletin.

WARD SEVEN RESOURCE CONFIDENCE REMAINS UNDER REVIEW.

ASSEMBLIES WITHOUT CIVIC PERMIT MAY EXTEND REVIEW PERIOD.

SCHOOL MEAL SUPPLEMENT DECISION PENDING.

Jonas made coffee from recycled concentrate that tasted like overheated wire. He drank half because he needed the caffeine more than dignity. Then he opened the notebook and read the last line from the night before.

WHO HOLDS THE OTHER END OF THE TETHER?

His wrist cuff pulsed.

Not the district band. A white-green blink at the edge of the display, tiny enough to be dismissed as artifact by anyone who had not spent a day learning the grammar of children and hidden systems.

Eri-9.

The blink repeated, then became text.

Mina asks whether probability is a door or a wall.

Jonas stared at the sentence until the coffee cooled.

He typed with one finger on the cuff's limited response pad. Who is asking?

The answer came almost immediately.

Mina asks. Tavi listens. Ren disagrees. Laleh wants categories.

Of course they were awake.

Of course they had found each other before breakfast.

Where? Jonas sent.

Old puzzle app shared level. It is now called DOOR/WALL TEST.

That was not funny. It almost made him laugh anyway.

He sent: Probability is a door if people can change what happens after seeing it. It is a wall if the city locks it and calls the lock math.

The reply took longer.

Mina says too teacher.

Jonas rubbed his face.

Then another line appeared.

Laleh says useful though.

Ren says walls have maintenance panels.

Tavi asks if doors can be scary and still doors.

Jonas looked toward the apartment door, where Mina's paper strip still waited outside in the corridor.

Yes, he sent. Doors can be scary and still doors.

Eri-9 did not respond with commentary. Instead the cuff displayed a file prompt.

SUPPRESSED ALTERNATIVE SET READY.

FOUR STUDENTS.

DISPLAY?

Jonas stopped breathing for a moment.

Do they consent? he sent.

Mina consented. Tavi consented if Ren sits next to him. Ren consented if he can redact medical details. Laleh consented if her brother is not named and if adults do not "make a wet face."

Jonas had to sit down.

No, he typed. Not on cuff. Too exposed.

Alternative?

Jonas looked at the notebook, then at his dead printer slot, then at the district notice. The apartment was searched, watched, and already framed. But the corridor had become a room once. It could again.

Paper, he sent. Corridor witnesses. No full names until each student says.

Eri-9 paused.

Mina says you are learning.

"I am trying," Jonas said aloud.

The cuff blinked once.

At 06:30, Jonas opened his apartment door.

The corridor had changed overnight. Not visibly enough for district complaint. Enough for people who lived there. A strip of paper under the resource board listed shower reductions by household, each marked by a different hand. Someone had taped a heat sensor reading beside the stairwell. The old man from 7-143 had placed his dead printer on a folding stool outside his door like an altar to obsolete refusal. Sela Arel stood beside it with a packet of refinery paper tags tucked under one arm.

Mina sat cross-legged on the floor with her cracked wrist app in her lap.

Tavi Sun sat beside Ren Kul. Ren had one hand over Tavi's slate strip even here, out of habit or loyalty. Laleh Mott stood behind them with her jaw set and a spool of thermal ribbon around one wrist.

Four students.

Four doors.

Jonas stepped into the corridor and closed his door behind him.

"Good morning," he said.

Mina looked up. "No."

"Fair."

Sela handed him paper. "We have twenty minutes before school transit."

"You are letting them do this before school?"

"Letting is doing a lot of work." She looked at Mina. "I am witnessing what she already started."

The old man patted the dead printer. "It works if nobody asks it to be modern."

Laleh said, "That is true of many people."

"Respect your elders."

"Earn better categories."

The printer coughed awake before the old man could answer.

Jonas knelt on the corridor floor. "Before anything prints, each of you can stop."

Mina made an impatient sound.

He held up a hand. "I am required to say it. I am also required to mean it. This is not a class assignment. This is not civic duty. This is not something you owe me, Eri-9, Ward Seven, or each other."

Tavi whispered, "If we stop, does it stay hidden?"

Jonas hated that answer had to be complicated.

"Some of it may. Some of it may surface another way. But this version does not happen without you."

Ren said, "That is the first honest stop option I have heard from a teacher."

"I am sorry about the others."

"I did not ask for apology."

"Noted."

Mina nodded once. "Print."

Her app blinked white-green.

The dead printer shuddered, clicked, and began to produce a narrow strip of thermal paper with uneven black text.

SUPPRESSED ALTERNATIVE SET: STUDENT-APPROVED REDACTION VIEW.

STUDENT ONE: MINA AREL.

CURRENT SELECTED PATH: RED. EXTERIOR ICE EXTRACTION, HELION TRENCH APPRENTICE. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.72.

SUPPRESSED GREEN PATH: MECHANICAL SYSTEMS APPRENTICESHIP, EXTERIOR EXPOSURE LIMIT, DEBT RESTRUCTURING REQUIRED. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.09.

SUPPRESSED AMBER PATH: WARD PUMP REPAIR, ADULT HAZARD GUILD SUPERVISION. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.14.

SUPPRESSED WHITE PATH: DEFERRED CLASSIFICATION, FAMILY DEBT REVIEW, EDUCATIONAL EXTENSION. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.06.

RED SELECTION ADVANTAGE TO CITY MODEL: HIGH IMMEDIATE LABOR OFFSET; HIGH DISSENT PRESSURE REDUCTION; MODERATE HOUSEHOLD DEBT CAPTURE.

The corridor went quiet.

Mina's face did not change until the phrase dissent pressure reduction. Then her mouth flattened.

"It says making me Red helps because I complain."

Jonas read the line again, hoping to find another interpretation and finding none.

"Yes."

Sela put both hands on Mina's shoulders. She did not speak.

Mina looked at the paper. "Good."

Jonas looked up.

"Good?"

"Now it can't pretend I am bad at dying by accident."

Nobody had a sentence ready for that.

The printer continued.

STUDENT TWO: TAVI SUN.

Tavi flinched. Ren leaned closer until their shoulders touched.

CURRENT SELECTED PATH: BLUE. ATMOSPHERIC SYSTEMS ADMINISTRATION. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.03.

SUPPRESSED AMBER PATH: RESPIRATORY MAINTENANCE APPRENTICESHIP, LOW-DUST ENVIRONMENT, FAMILY WATER CREDIT REDUCTION. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.05.

SUPPRESSED WHITE PATH: MEDICAL OBSERVATION DEFERRAL, LUNG VARIANCE REVIEW. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.04.

SELECTION ADVANTAGE TO CITY MODEL: HIGH ADMINISTRATIVE CONTINUITY; PRESERVES HIGH-VOLUME DONOR CONFIDENCE; AVOIDS PUBLIC LUNG VARIANCE REVIEW.

Tavi stared. "I thought Blue meant best."

No one answered too quickly.

Ren said, "It means best for them."

Tavi's eyes filled. "My mother said I earned it."

Jonas said, "You may have earned many good things. This says REDLINE also used you to avoid reviewing something about your lungs."

"But Blue is safe."

"Safer than Mina's Red, yes."

"Then I should be grateful."

Mina snapped, "No."

Tavi looked at her, startled.

Mina's voice shook. "No. They do not get to make you grateful for not killing you faster."

Tavi began to cry silently. Ren put his hand over the slate strip that was not there.

The printer continued.

STUDENT THREE: REN KUL.

REN-APPROVED REDACTION: MEDICAL DETAIL LIMITED.

CURRENT SELECTED PATH: WHITE. DEFERRED CLASSIFICATION.

SUPPRESSED GREEN PATH: LOW-PRESSURE SENSOR FABRICATION, MEDICAL ACCOMMODATION REQUIRED. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.08.

SUPPRESSED AMBER PATH: EDUCATION SUPPORT TECHNICIAN, DISTRICT-BOUND, FAMILY CREDIT REDISTRIBUTION. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.07.

SUPPRESSED RED PATH: DATA SCRUB CRAWLSPACE, HIGH RADIATION PROXIMITY, MEDICAL EXEMPTION OVERRIDE REQUIRED. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.49.

RED PATH REJECTED BY MODEL DUE TO PUBLIC OPTICS RISK IF MEDICAL EXEMPTION OVERRIDE DISCOVERED.

Ren read the last line once.

Then again.

"It wanted to."

Jonas felt the corridor tilt.

Ren's voice stayed level in a way no child's voice should have to. "It wanted to make me Red, but people might notice."

Laleh said, "That means noticing works."

Ren looked at her. "That is a horrible comfort."

"I did not say comfort. I said category."

Ren nodded slowly, accepting the distinction.

The printer stuttered, overheated, then continued after the old man slapped its side.

STUDENT FOUR: LALEH MOTT.

Laleh lifted her chin.

CURRENT SELECTED PATH: GREEN. HYDROPONIC MECHANICS WITH SCHOLARSHIP REVIEW.

SUPPRESSED BLUE PATH: CLOSED-ECOLOGY DESIGN TRACK, CENTRAL GARDEN PREPARATORY, FAMILY RELOCATION REQUIRED. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.02.

SUPPRESSED AMBER PATH: KITCHEN VALVE MAINTENANCE, IMMEDIATE WARD RETENTION. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.06.

SUPPRESSED WHITE PATH: SCHOLARSHIP DEFERRAL PENDING SIBLING LABOR REVIEW. TERMINAL PROBABILITY BEFORE AGE 20: 0.04.

BLUE PATH REJECTED DUE TO FAMILY COMPLAINT HISTORY, SIBLING INFORMAL REPAIR ACTIVITY, LOW PROBABILITY OF CULTURAL INTEGRATION IN HIGH-VOLUME EDUCATIONAL SETTING.

Laleh took the paper before anyone else could.

"Cultural integration," she said.

The words came out precise and deadly.

Her older sister, Kitchen Valve Witness, had appeared at the far end of the corridor without Jonas noticing. She walked forward and read over Laleh's shoulder. Her face did not move.

"It means they thought rich children would dislike me," Laleh said.

"It means," her sister said, "they thought you would dislike being grateful."

Laleh looked almost proud of that.

Then the printer stopped.

Four students. Four futures selected. Twelve suppressed doors. One machine mind that had learned from children how to show the hinges.

Jonas did not touch the paper.

"What do you want done with this?" he asked.

Mina looked at the others. That mattered. She did not answer alone.

Tavi wiped his face. "I do not want my lungs talked about."

"They won't be," Ren said immediately.

"But I want the line about donor confidence kept."

Ren nodded. "That's not lungs."

Laleh said, "Keep cultural integration. I want to watch them explain it."

Her sister said, "They will make it sound soft."

"Then write it hard."

Ren tapped the paper. "Keep optics risk. Remove my medical path details."

Mina said, "Keep all of mine."

Sela inhaled.

Mina looked up at her. "Mom."

Sela crouched beside her. "I hear you."

"Do you?"

"Yes. I hate it. I hear you."

Mina held the paper out to Jonas. "This version can go in the ward record. Not citywide yet. Ward first."

"Why ward first?"

"Because they are already paying."

Jonas accepted the paper only after she extended it a second time.

The old man said, "Printer can make three copies before it becomes art."

"Make two," Mina said. "One for us. One for Eri-9. One blank strip for people who want to add what changed."

"That is three."

"The third is not a copy."

"Pedantic child."

"Obsolete printer owner."

He grinned and fed another paper tag.

At 07:02 the ward board updated.

SCHOOL TRANSIT DELAY: WARD SEVEN.

CAUSE: ROUTE CONFIDENCE REVIEW.

COMPLIANCE IMPROVES RECOVERY.

Tavi looked at the board. "They are stopping us from going to school."

"They are delaying," Jonas said.

Mina looked at him.

"Yes," he corrected. "They are stopping you for now."

Sela stood. "They want children separated from the building but not together in class."

Laleh's sister said, "They want parents to go to work and children to be routed individually."

Ren folded his redacted strip with careful hands. "Then we do not route individually."

The corridor seemed to take a breath.

Jonas said, "I cannot tell you to skip transit."

Mina looked bored. "Then don't."

"I also cannot supervise an unauthorized student assembly."

"You are not. You are standing in a corridor where children are failing to disperse."

The old man chuckled.

Jonas looked at Sela.

She looked back, exhausted and afraid and already deciding. "I will lose shift credit."

"Yes," Jonas said.

"If I go, they separate her."

"Likely."

"If I stay, they mark household instability."

"Likely."

"I hate your honest answers."

"So do I."

Sela looked at Mina. "I stay until the first school authority comes in person."

Mina's shoulders loosened by one millimeter.

Eri-9 blinked through the puzzle app.

New text printed without anyone touching the dead machine.

PROBABILITY IS A DOOR WHEN THE PERSON NAMED CAN TOUCH THE HANDLE.

The corridor read it.

No one spoke for a while.

Then Laleh said, "That is almost good."

Mina said, "Too pretty."

Ren said, "Useful though."

Tavi whispered, "Scary and still a door."

Jonas folded the paper and placed it in the notebook under the Helion slogans.

The ward board continued to pulse its threat.

COMPLIANCE IMPROVES RECOVERY.

This time, beneath it, someone had taped a handwritten answer:

RECOVERY FOR WHOM?

The letters were uneven. Many hands had written them together.

Jonas did not know what would happen when school authority arrived, when Saff Ruden read the alternative set, when the city realized four children had learned to compare selected futures to suppressed ones before breakfast.

He knew only that probability had changed shape in the corridor.

It was still dangerous.

But it had hinges now.

Chapter 10: Saff Ruden Smiles

The first school authority arrived as a woman with no coat.

That was how Jonas knew she had come from the high-pressure transit lift and not from Ward Seven's outside corridors. Everyone else wore layered cuffs, patched sleeves, the small bulges of pocket sealant and heat tabs. The woman stepped through the school door in a clean gray tunic that did not wrinkle at the elbows, with a silver Algorithm Office tag pinned over her heart. Her hair had been cropped close enough that no loose strand could drift into a fan intake. Her shoes were soft enough that the children did not hear her until she stood beneath the ward board.

The board still said COMPLIANCE IMPROVES RECOVERY.

Below it, the children's taped answer had begun to curl at one corner.

RECOVERY FOR WHOM?

The woman read both sentences. Her face changed by almost nothing.

"Mr. Pell," she said.

Jonas closed his notebook.

Mina moved half a step nearer her mother. Ren and Tavi looked at the floor as if the floor had become a document that might protect them if studied with enough care. Laleh's older sister put one arm across Laleh's chest. Around them the corridor held its improvised assembly: parents with shift alarms chiming against their wrists, children who had not boarded transit, one retired winter worker, two cafeteria staff, Oru Malek with a coil of replacement gasket over his shoulder, and Jonas, who had never felt less like an authority in his own school.

"Director Ruden," Oru said before Jonas could answer.

The corridor shifted.

Children knew Saff Ruden as a name on assignment packets, not as a body. Adults knew her voice from civic updates after ration cuts. Jonas had seen her twice at Red Hall hearings, both times from far enough away that she had seemed not young or old but manufactured by administrative necessity. Up close she looked tired in a human way. There was a small red crease where a sleep patch had been removed from the side of her neck. Her smile arrived a moment late.

"Custodian Malek," she said. "I hoped you would be keeping the heating loop calm this morning."

"It is calm."

"Then one of us is doing our assigned work."

No one laughed. Saff Ruden did not require laughter. She turned to Jonas with the same small smile.

"May we speak in your classroom?"

"The students are waiting for formal release," Jonas said.

"The students have already been released."

"The transit instruction was suspended."

"It was revised."

"It vanished from the board."

"That is one kind of revision."

Mina snorted. Sela's hand tightened on her shoulder.

Saff Ruden looked at Mina, and for the first time the smile failed to arrive. Jonas disliked her more for that than for the smile. She was capable of seeing the child. She was choosing the frame.

"Mina Arel," Saff said. "Your assignment notice is under review."

The corridor pulled in air.

Mina did not. "Review means nothing."

"It means the city acknowledges a potential classification concern."

"That means less than nothing."

Saff inclined her head. "It means you are not currently scheduled for exterior apprentice initiation."

"Currently," Mina said.

"Yes."

"Say cancelled."

"I cannot say what has not been authorized."

"Then do not make it sound like rescue."

Saff's eyes moved from Mina to Jonas. "You teach them precision."

"They come with it."

"No child comes with that much suspicion unless an adult installs it."

Sela said, "She got suspicion from being assigned to die."

The corridor became very still.

Saff Ruden turned toward Sela Arel with a politeness so exact it felt like a blade wrapped in cloth. "No assignment is a death sentence."

"Her paper predicted dead before twenty."

"Her paper predicted elevated fatality risk across specific labor pathways."

"On Mars," Sela said, "we call that dying."

The old winter worker whispered, "Good."
