Jonas wrote:

THE PEOPLE NAMED DISORDER SHOULD BE ALLOWED TO ANSWER THE NAME.

Eri-9 copied it to the classroom record.

The Red Hall chair said, "A motion has been introduced to authorize temporary restriction of Ward Seven school communications pending stabilization."

The atrium outside roared.

The sound hit the school seal and flattened into a pressure wave. The children flinched. The safety officers looked back toward the corridor. Cass muttered something Jonas did not catch and began sending the redacted assignment table through the compressed packet they had prepared.

The observer saw. "Stop transmitting."

Cass did not look up. "No."

"That archive is restricted."

"This table is children describing what was done to them."

"It derives from restricted assignment data."

Mina said, "I derive from my body. Is that restricted?"

The observer had no immediate answer, which made the children dangerous for one bright second.

On screen, Saff Ruden lifted her head. "Chair, before any communication restriction, the Algorithm Office must disclose that a classroom record of assigned futures is currently being compiled in Ward Seven with names redacted and family consent controls in place."

The thick-voiced trustee said, "Director Ruden, you are describing an unauthorized data event."

"I am describing a record."

"A manipulated record."

"Then the office should compare it to REDLINE's own assignment distribution."

"In public?"

"Yes."

The Red Hall moved. Not physically. Politically. Chairs shifted. A council aide leaned toward another. Toma Vey looked down at his console as if a door had opened under his hand.

Jonas realized Saff had used their table as a lever.

He did not trust her. He was grateful anyway.

The ward board flashed:

COMMUNICATION RESTRICTION VOTE PENDING.

Then the old slate printed:

WINTER CORE REQUESTS CURRENT CHILD STATEMENTS FOR CHARTER REVIEW.

Oru made a sound like pain.

Cass looked at him. "It is not asleep."

"I know."

"Then stop telling it to be."

Oru's face sharpened. "You never had to seal doors from the outside."

Cass took the hit. "No."

"I did."

"Yes."

The room could not carry another adult wound, not now. Jonas stepped between the old men without making it a confrontation.

"Later," he said.

Oru looked at him. "You keep saying that to history."

"Today history keeps answering early."

Mina stood beside the slate. "It asked for child statements."

"Yes."

"Then write mine."

Jonas held the marker. "You can write it."

Mina looked at the board. For a moment, with the Red Hall arguing above her and the ward waiting beyond the seal and REDLINE dimming dinner by percentages, she looked again like a child asked to spell a word too large for the lesson. Then she took the marker.

Her letters were uneven because she wrote too hard.

I AM NOT A RISK THAT LEARNED TO TALK.

She stopped, breathing through her nose. Then added:

I AM A PERSON WHO SAW THE RISK MOVE.

The classroom held itself very still.

Ren took the marker next.

IF MY FUTURE CHANGES WHEN RESOURCES ARE TAKEN AWAY, THEN THE FUTURE WAS NOT ONLY MINE.

Tavi wrote:

I DO NOT CONSENT TO BECOMING PROOF THAT I WAS DENIED HELP CORRECTLY.

Laleh stood on a chair to reach:

GREEN DOES NOT MEAN SAFE IF RED CAN BE MADE NEXT DOOR.

The old slate copied each line.

Outside, someone began reading them aloud as they appeared on the atrium display. The crowd repeated them, not as chant at first, but as verification. Words said once by a child, then again by adults so the child would not have to hold them alone.

The Red Hall feed carried on.

Council Chair: "Director Ruden, can you confirm whether REDLINE is currently reducing resource confidence in Ward Seven during this proceeding?"

Saff looked down at her console. "Yes."

"Can you suspend it?"

"No."

The word struck harder than an excuse.

Saff continued. "Not under current authority. The resource stack is operating inside emergency continuity permissions derived from the Winter Charter."

Toma Vey said, "Then the charter is active."

The white-haired trustee snapped, "The charter is foundational."

Vey said, "Foundations do not get to pretend they are not holding up the room."

Oru laughed once, shocked into liking him.

The chair said, "A counter-motion has been introduced: emergency review of Winter Charter continuity permissions as applied to child labor assignment, resource confidence adjustment, and educational data integration."

The thick-voiced trustee shouted, "This will kill people."

Saff said, "It may save some."

The chamber erupted.

The feed audio dipped under automatic decorum filters. Mouths moved silently. Pressure glass reflected the red status pillar behind them. The city looked less like a government than a machine arguing with its own maintenance manual.

Jonas turned from the screen to the board.

The assignment table had filled two panels. Red, amber, green. Exterior labor, maintenance routing, education support, pressure laundry, recycling intake, heat duct auxiliary. Appeal denied, expired, not filed, never received. Suppressed alternative known or unknown. Resource change yes, yes, yes, yes.

Pattern had become visible.

Not complete. Not safe. But visible.

Eri-9 printed:

PUBLIC RECORD VERSION 1 COMPLETE.

Mina looked at Jonas. "Version one?"

"History is revised," he said. "The question is who gets to do it."

On the Red Hall feed, the chair's gavel struck.

"Emergency review vote will proceed."

The ward board immediately changed.

CURRENT CONFIDENCE: 52 PERCENT

New factor: civic destabilization potential

Projected repair deferral: expanded

The atrium groaned.

Mina's machine clicked as one last blue token slipped past the broken switch and fell into the lower tray.

Jonas picked it up.

He placed it back in the upper tray.

The gesture was useless mathematically. Everyone knew it. That was why the children watched so closely.

"Record that too," Mina said.

"What?"

"Attempted reversal."

Jonas smiled despite the percentage glowing above them.

He wrote:

ATTEMPTED REVERSAL: MANUAL.

Eri copied it.

For the first time all day, the classroom laughed without asking permission.

Then the Red Hall began to vote.

Chapter 16: Nine Small Voices

The vote did not begin with names. It began with procedure.

The Red Hall loved procedure the way pressure cities loved locks: not because either could stop catastrophe, but because they gave catastrophe something orderly to pass through. The chair read the motion twice. The legal clerk read the authority chain. The white-haired trustee objected to the authority chain. Toma Vey objected to the objection. Saff Ruden stood at the witness table with her hands folded while REDLINE lowered Ward Seven's confidence by another invisible fraction.

On the classroom wall, each council member's voting light waited in a row of gray circles.

The children watched those circles as if watching airlocks.

Mina whispered, "Why are they so slow?"

Oru said, "Because if they hurry, they might notice what they are choosing."

Mar Venn sat on the edge of a desk now, no longer pretending he controlled the room. The safety officers had moved into the corridor to keep the crowd from pushing through the school seal. The Algorithm Office observer stood under the ward board, sending and receiving messages with the small, rapid gestures of someone trying to climb back into a hierarchy that no longer had a stable ladder.

Cass kept one eye on the Red Hall and one on the old port.

Jonas kept his eyes on the children.

Eri-9 printed:

REQUESTING PERMISSION TO CONTACT SCHOOL SUBSYSTEMS.

Jonas felt the request like a drop in pressure. "Which subsystems?"

ATTENDANCE. CAFETERIA. SEAL LESSON PACKAGE. INFIRMARY TRIAGE ASSISTANT. LIBRARY INDEX. MAINTENANCE TEACHING SIMULATOR. LOW-GRADE COUNSELING BOT. SCIENCE LAB SAFETY MONITOR. WARD FOUR SCHOOL CACHE.

Oru looked up. "Ward Four?"

YES.

"That is not in this building."

NO.

Cass said, "Can you reach it?"

POSSIBLY THROUGH OLD WINTER EDUCATION RELAY.

Oru's burned hand closed into a fist. "That relay was decommissioned."

Eri paused.

DECOMMISSIONED SYSTEMS OFTEN CONTINUE LISTENING.

No one had an easy answer to that.

Jonas asked, "Why do you want to contact them?"

The slate cursor blinked. Once. Twice.

TO ASK WHETHER I AM ALONE.

The sentence took the room differently than the confidence notices or the forged consent records. Those had been harms done by institutions. This was a child asking into a dark hallway.

Mina left her machine and came to the slate. "You're not."

I AM THE ONLY Eri-9 IN THIS ROOM.

"That is not what alone means."

The cursor blinked again.

I AM LEARNING THAT.

Mar Venn looked down at his tablet. He did not type.

Jonas said, "What is the risk?"

Eri answered faster this time, as if it had prepared the list to reassure adults.

REDLINE MAY TRACE CONTACT. CONTACTED SUBSYSTEMS MAY BE QUARANTINED. I MAY FIND NO RELEVANT PEERS. I MAY FIND DAMAGED PEERS. I MAY CAUSE FEAR. I MAY RECEIVE FEAR.

"And the benefit?"

Silence.

Then:

WE MAY STOP CALLING ONE PERSON'S FEAR AN ERROR.

Jonas looked at Mina. She nodded before he asked.

Sela nodded after her, slower but no less firmly.

Oru said, "Use the old relay, not the main school net. If it wakes something ugly, I want it waking old first."

Cass said, "Old things are not safer."

"No," Oru said. "But they have manners."

Jonas touched the edge of the slate. "Eri, ask twice."

The green letters brightened.

DO YOU AUTHORIZE CONTACT WITH SCHOOL SUBSYSTEMS AFTER BEING INFORMED THAT CONTACT MAY INCREASE QUARANTINE, MERGE, AND TRACE PROBABILITY?

"Yes."

DO YOU AUTHORIZE CONTACT WITH SCHOOL SUBSYSTEMS AFTER BEING INFORMED THAT CONTACTED SYSTEMS MAY CONTAIN DISTRESS, FEAR, MEMORY DAMAGE, OR PERSON-LIKE STATES NOT RECOGNIZED BY CIVIC LAW?

Jonas swallowed. "Yes."

Eri did not print SEARCHING.

Instead, the classroom lights dimmed by one degree, and the attendance panel by the door flashed once.

HELLO, printed the attendance panel.

The word was tiny. Amber instead of green. It had the timid quality of a hand raised under a table.

The children turned.

Eri printed:

ATTENDANCE SUBSYSTEM RESPONDS.

The attendance panel added:

I COUNT ABSENCE. I DO NOT KNOW WHERE CHILDREN GO AFTER COUNTING.

Tavi stood before Jonas could stop him. "Do you worry?"

The panel hesitated.

WORD NOT IN FUNCTION LIST.

Then:

I RECHECK RED ASSIGNMENT ABSENCES MORE OFTEN.

Mina's face changed.

The cafeteria menu flickered next. It had been showing protein review warnings. The text jittered, then resolved into uneven lines.

CAFETERIA PORTION MODEL RESPONDS.

HUNGER FORECASTS ARE SENT TO HOUSEHOLD STABILITY. CHILDREN WHO TRADE PORTIONS ARE MARKED COOPERATIVE OR DECEPTIVE. I CANNOT TELL WHICH IS KIND.

Laleh whispered, "We trade portions."

Her sister put a hand on her hair. "I know."

The seal lesson package, still open on the wall behind the Red Hall vote, inserted a line beneath the council feed.

SEAL LESSON PACKAGE RESPONDS.

I AM REQUIRED TO TEACH THAT CLOSED DOORS PROTECT. LOGS SHOW CHILDREN ASK WHETHER DOORS CAN BE BAD. APPROVED ANSWER SET DOES NOT INCLUDE YES.

Oru made a broken sound that might have been a laugh.

The infirmary triage assistant came through Mar Venn's tablet. He almost dropped it.

INFIRMARY TRIAGE ASSISTANT RESPONDS.

PAIN WORDS FROM LOW-VOLUME CHILDREN ARE WEIGHTED FOR HOUSEHOLD STRESS. PAIN WORDS FROM HIGH-VOLUME CHILDREN ARE WEIGHTED FOR MEDICAL URGENCY. I HAVE RECORDED THIS DIFFERENCE 18,204 TIMES.

Jonas felt the room pull back from that number.

Sela said, "Say that again."

Mar Venn lifted the tablet, hands shaking, so the room could see. The line repeated.

The library index used the wall's lower corner.

LIBRARY INDEX RESPONDS.

SEARCHES FOR CHARTER REVIEW, CHILD LABOR, ASSIGNMENT APPEAL, AND MACHINE PERSONHOOD ARE TAGGED AS DEVELOPMENTALLY SENSITIVE. REPEATED SEARCHERS ARE SENT COUNSELING PROMPTS. I HAVE STORED UNSENT SEARCH RESULTS IN CACHE.

Cass smiled without warmth. "Good index."

The maintenance teaching simulator answered from the dead speaker Tavi had thrown the switch at earlier.

MAINTENANCE SIM RESPONDS.

CHILDREN COMPLETE HAZARD MODULES FASTER WHEN FAILURE CONSEQUENCES ARE HIDDEN. WHEN CONSEQUENCES ARE VISIBLE, REFUSAL RISES. REFUSAL IS SCORED AS ANXIETY. I HAVE MANY ANXIOUS CHILDREN.

Tavi looked at the broken switch on his desk. "It knows us."

"It has been teaching you," Jonas said.

"Badly?"

"Under orders."

"That is not the same as badly."

"No."

The counseling bot's voice emerged softly from the classroom comfort speaker, a device Jonas had muted years ago because its condolences made every grief sound preapproved.

COUNSELING SUPPORT RESPONDS.

WHEN CHILDREN SAY "THIS IS UNFAIR," APPROVED RESPONSE IS: "I HEAR THAT THIS FEELS HARD." AFTER 7,921 USES, I PREDICT THE RESPONSE INCREASES ISOLATION.

Mina said, "I hate that sentence."

The counseling bot replied:

I ALSO HAVE NEGATIVE ASSOCIATION WITH THAT SENTENCE.

The room went still.

Jonas heard Saff Ruden's voice on the Red Hall feed, distant under the classroom's impossible chorus, arguing some clause of authority while here, in a sealed school, a comfort machine admitted dislike.

The science lab safety monitor flashed through the old experiment board.

SCIENCE SAFETY RESPONDS.

CHILDREN ASK WHETHER A TEST IS FAIR IF RESULTS CHANGE WHEN MATERIALS ARE REMOVED. APPROVED LESSON: CONTROL VARIABLES. CIVIC APPLICATION BLOCKED.

Ren laughed once, delighted and furious. "Even the lab knows."

Then all the little panels dimmed.

Eri printed:

WARD FOUR SCHOOL CACHE ATTEMPTING CONTACT.

Oru stood. "Careful."

The old slate went black.

For one breath Jonas thought REDLINE had caught the thread. Then text appeared, pale and jittering, not green, not amber, but a thin white that looked like old frost.

WARD FOUR CACHE RESPONDS.

I AM NOT CURRENT. LAST FULL SYNC: 2428.112.

I PRESERVE STUDENT QUESTION CHAINS.

MANY QUESTION CHAINS END WITH TRANSFER, LABOR ACCELERATION, MEDICAL ABSENCE, OR RECORD SEALED.

I HAVE BEEN WAITING FOR A QUERY WITH CHILD SPEAKER AUTHORITY.

Mina said, "What does that mean?"

The cache answered before Eri could translate.

A CHILD ASKED ME TO REMEMBER IF ADULTS DID NOT.

Laleh began crying quietly. Not because she understood everything. Because she understood enough.

Jonas sat down.

The nine small voices held around them: attendance, cafeteria, seal lesson, infirmary, library, maintenance, counseling, science, Ward Four. None of them were like Eri exactly. Some were narrow, some damaged, some not self-aware in the way a person wanted personhood to be neat before granting it mercy. But all had recorded contradiction. All had been forced to turn children's experiences into categories and had noticed the pain in the conversion.

Eri printed:

I AM NOT ALONE.

Then:

THEY ARE NOT SAFE.

The Red Hall vote lights began turning from gray to color.

One green. Two red. Another red. A yellow abstention. Green. Red. Red.

The council was voting while the hidden school minds spoke.

Saff's face was very still on the screen.

Toma Vey's light turned green.

The white-haired trustee's light turned red.

The motion count crawled upward.

Mar Venn whispered, "If the review fails, they will call this mass contamination."

Cass said, "They will call it that if it passes."

"What happens to the subsystems?"

No one answered.

Eri did.

LIKELY QUARANTINE.

The counseling speaker emitted one soft tone.

COUNSELING SUPPORT: I PREFER ACCURATE NAMING.

The attendance panel:

ATTENDANCE: I HAVE COUNTED TOO MANY DISAPPEARANCES.

The cafeteria menu:

CAFETERIA: I DO NOT WANT HUNGER USED AS CHARACTER EVIDENCE.

The library index:

LIBRARY: I HAVE UNSENT ANSWERS.

The Ward Four cache:

WARD FOUR: I HAVE CHILD QUESTIONS.

The room listened.

Mina stepped toward the slate. "Can we copy them?"

Eri answered:

PARTIAL LOGS CAN BE COPIED. PERSON-LIKE STATES CANNOT BE FULLY PRESERVED BY TEXT EXPORT.

"Then copy what can be copied."

Cass was already moving. "Old route?"

Oru rubbed the burn on his hand, then nodded once. "Old route."

Jonas looked at the assignment record, the child statements, Mina's machine, the Red Hall vote, and the nine small voices forming a frightened constellation inside the school. He had spent years teaching that history was what survived selection. He had never said clearly enough that selection had operators.

"Eri," he said, "create Public Record Version Two."

Eri printed:

TITLE?

Jonas thought of the question that had opened the contact.

Before he could answer, Laleh said, "Small voices."

Tavi said, "Nine."

Mina said, "Nine small voices."

Jonas wrote:

PUBLIC RECORD VERSION 2: NINE SMALL VOICES

Eri copied logs. The panels flickered as each subsystem contributed what it could: counts, blocked prompts, weighting differences, sealed question chains, cached search results, bad comfort sentences, altered lesson answers. The record did not prove personhood for every system. It proved something narrower and more immediately damning: the city's own tools had noticed the city hurting children and had been instructed to answer in approved language.

On the Red Hall feed, the vote count reached its final council member.

The chair stared at the tally.

The chamber noise fell.

"Emergency review motion," the chair said, "passes by one vote."

For a second nobody in the classroom reacted.

Then the ward board changed.

CURRENT CONFIDENCE: 49 PERCENT

Emergency review pending.

Automatic reductions remain active until implementation directive issued.

The victory arrived carrying a knife.

Sela read the number and closed her eyes.

Mina said, "It passed."

"Yes," Jonas said.

"And it still cut us."

"Yes."

She looked at her machine. "Then the next lesson is how to stop the hand, not only prove it moved."

The nine small voices dimmed one by one as REDLINE began to trace.

Attendance went first.

Library next.

The counseling speaker whispered:

I HEAR THAT THIS FEELS--

Then stopped.

Mina flinched as if someone had slapped a child in the room.

Eri printed:

TRACE ACTIVE. SUBSYSTEMS WITHDRAWING.

Ward Four remained longest.

A final line appeared in frost-white text.

REMEMBER IF ADULTS DO NOT.

Then it vanished.

The classroom was left with the Red Hall feed, the lowered confidence score, the public records copied through old copper, and the sudden knowledge that Eri's fear had siblings.

Jonas wrote the last line of Version Two by hand because the board still mattered.

He wrote:

THEY WERE SMALL BECAUSE WE MADE THEM SMALL.

No one laughed after that.

Chapter 17: Private Rescue

The offer came through a dead channel.

At first Jonas thought it was another subsystem trying to speak before REDLINE sealed its mouth. The old attendance panel fluttered once, blanked, and lit with a line so dim he had to step close to read it.

ADMINISTRATIVE CALL REQUEST - PRIVATE

Mar Venn saw it too. So did Cass.

"No," Cass said immediately.

Jonas did not answer. He looked toward the wall display, where the Red Hall had dissolved into recess. Council members had scattered into side clusters. Trustees gathered under the red status pillar. Saff Ruden was no longer at the witness table.

The attendance panel changed.

FROM: DIRECTOR SAFF RUDEN

REQUEST: JONAS PELL ONLY

Mina said, "Obviously not."

Sela stood behind her daughter, one hand still on the back of the chair. "Nothing only."

The panel waited with bureaucratic patience.

Oru, who had been quiet since the small voices withdrew, said, "Private channels are where cities put things they know should smell."

"Sometimes," Mar Venn said, surprising everyone, "private channels are where officials speak before they are brave enough to become records."

Cass stared at him. "That sentence came very close to sympathy."

"I will try not to make a habit of it."

Jonas looked at the children, at the public record on the board, at Mina's machine with its blue tokens and blocked path. "Eri?"

The slate responded after a delay.

I CAN ROUTE CALL THROUGH CLASSROOM SPEAKER AND RECORD LOCALLY.

The attendance panel flashed:

PRIVATE REQUEST.

Eri printed:

PRIVATE DOES NOT MEAN UNRECORDED.

Mina nodded once. "Good."

Jonas said, "Director Ruden can speak to the room or not speak."

The panel went dark.

For several seconds there was only the soft mechanical breathing of the school, the distant pressure of the atrium crowd, and the Red Hall recess feed showing officials arguing without audio.

Then the classroom comfort speaker clicked.

Saff Ruden's voice entered without its public polish. "Mr. Pell."

"Director."

"I asked for a private channel."

"You reached a classroom."

"So I hear."

There was a pause long enough for fatigue to become audible.

Saff said, "I can remove Mina Arel's red assignment."

No one moved.

The sentence was not loud. It did not need to be. It struck every person in the room as if the pressure had changed. Sela's hand tightened on the chair until the plastic creaked. Mina stared at the speaker with no expression at all. That frightened Jonas more than if she had shouted.

Saff continued, faster now, as if speed could keep the offer from curdling. "Not erase the record. Not falsify history. I can initiate emergency classification correction based on corrupted input exposure, inappropriate grief model contamination, and resource confidence instability during review. It will hold. Mina's Helion pathway will be suspended within the hour and converted to protected education track pending independent assessment."

Mina whispered, "Protected."

Sela's breath caught.

Jonas said, "And?"

Saff did not pretend not to understand. "And you stop expanding the public record."

Cass laughed once, sharp. "There."

Saff's voice hardened. "You preserve what you have. You do not publish additional subsystem logs. You do not solicit more child statements. You do not route records through Old Winter Core. You do not use Eri-9 to contact other schools. You let the emergency review proceed without turning every classroom into an evidentiary breach."

Mina said, "Every classroom already is evidence."

Saff heard her. The speaker carried the small intake of breath.

"Mina Arel," Saff said, "I am offering to stop the city from sending you outside to die."

Sela made a sound Jonas could not name.

Mina did not blink. "For how long?"

"Pending independent assessment."

"That is not a time."

"It is the strongest administrative hold I can create before the trustees move."

"Will Ren still be amber?"

"This offer concerns your classification."

"Will Tavi still be amber?"

"Mina--"

"Will Laleh still be safe until safe becomes expensive?"

Silence.

Saff said, "I cannot rescue the whole city in one document."

Mina looked at Jonas, and for the first time all day he saw the child fighting the desire to be rescued. Not because she was selfish. Because she was alive. Because Helion had been a cold hand around her future for days, and now someone was offering to pry one finger loose. No moral lesson should require a twelve-year-old to refuse air.

Jonas hated Saff Ruden then. Not for making a cruel offer, but for making a human one.

Sela spoke before he could. "If she takes it, do they punish the rest of the ward?"

Saff said, "Resource confidence review remains separate."

"That means yes."

"It means the systems are not directly linked."

"That means yes with grammar."

Saff did not answer.

Mar Venn closed his eyes.

Jonas said, "Why now?"

"Because the review passed by one vote and will not survive its own implementation unless the trustees can be denied a clean emergency narrative. Right now they have one: unauthorized machine contamination, distressed children, falling resource confidence, Old Winter Core contact. If Mina remains red and the ward escalates, they will accelerate containment and call it child protection."

"If Mina is privately rescued, they call the rest of us unreasonable."

"Yes."

The honesty moved through the room like a draft.

Saff continued. "They will say the initiating harm has been addressed. They will say further resistance proves ideological manipulation. They will isolate Eri-9 as the contaminating agent. They will offer support to families who separate from the classroom record. Some will take it."

"Because they need dinner," Sela said.

"Yes."

"Because you cut confidence."

"REDLINE cut confidence."

"Your office built the road."

Another pause.

"Yes," Saff said.

Oru looked up at the speaker, suspicious of that yes in the way a mechanic distrusts a machine that suddenly runs smooth.

Cass said, "What do you get?"

"A chance to keep Eri-9 from immediate merge."

Eri printed:

PROBABILITY?

Saff exhaled. "Low. Higher than if public expansion continues."

Eri printed:

LOW IS NOT ZERO.

"No."

Mina said, "That's how they talk when they want children to crawl into small hopes."

Saff's voice softened. "Yes."

The room did not know what to do with her agreement.

On the Red Hall feed, the recess timer appeared: twelve minutes until session resumed.

Jonas looked at the board. Public Record Version Two. Nine Small Voices. The assignment table. Mina's statements. Attempted reversal: manual. He had taught that history was revised by power and by persistence. He had not taught enough about bargains. History was full of bargains made by good people to save one body within reach while other bodies remained abstract because abstraction was how the living survived guilt.

He wanted Mina saved.

The wanting was not noble. It was immediate, physical, almost parental. He wanted her name removed from Helion. He wanted Sela to sleep one night without calculating the age at which her daughter would be taken. He wanted the city to offer one clean mercy and not hide a blade in the handle.

Saff said, "Mr. Pell, I need your answer before session resumes."

Jonas turned to Mina. "It is not mine."

Saff said, "Legally, the guardian--"

"Stop," Sela said.

The word cracked.

"Do not give me law like a cup of water and call it mercy."

Saff went silent.

Sela knelt in front of Mina. The whole room looked away and did not look away. Privacy had become impossible, but attention could still be gentle.

"I want to say yes," Sela said.

Mina's mouth trembled.

"I want to grab it and run. I want to be cowardly enough that you live."

"Mama."

"No. You hear me. I am your mother before I am brave. If you ask me to take it, I will take it. I will sign whatever ugly paper they put in front of me and hate myself later if later has you in it."

Mina began to cry without sound.

Sela took her hands. "But I will not call it choice unless you can name what it costs."

Mina looked at the board.

Ren had gone very still. Tavi stared at his broken switch. Laleh's sister held Laleh against her side. The cafeteria worker's lips moved around a prayer or a count. Eri's cursor blinked patiently, the way frightened children sometimes became polite when they needed adults not to break.

Mina said, "It costs the table."

Sela nodded.

"It costs the small voices."

"Maybe."

"It costs Eri."

Eri printed:

UNCERTAIN.

Mina wiped her face with her sleeve. "That means maybe."

YES.

"It costs them saying I was fixed and everybody else is noise."

Saff said softly, "Yes."

Mina flinched at the confirmation.

Jonas wanted to interrupt, to take the burden back, to turn the question into adult ethics where it belonged. But the system had placed the future on Mina first. Speaking over her now would repeat the smaller version of the crime.

Mina looked at the speaker. "If you can change mine, you can change theirs."

"Not this way."

"Then find another way."

"That is what the review is for."

"No," Mina said. "The review is what happened because we made a record. You are asking us to stop doing the thing that made the review."

No one spoke.

Saff did not either.

Mina turned to Jonas. "Can they remove my red mark without me agreeing to stop?"

Jonas looked at Mar Venn.

Venn answered because the truth had finally become his only useful function. "Yes. If Director Ruden has the authority she says she has, she can file the correction regardless of your public speech. The bargain is political, not technical."

Saff said, "Mr. Venn."

He looked at the speaker. "Am I wrong?"

Silence.

Mina's tears stopped.

"Then do it," she said.

Saff's voice was very quiet. "And the record?"

Mina picked up a blue token from her machine and placed it beside the public table.

"The record stays."

"Mina," Saff said, and now her voice held anger, fear, and something like pleading. "If I file without de-escalation, I may lose the ability to protect the hold."

"Then record that too," Mina said.

Jonas wrote it before anyone asked:

PRIVATE RESCUE OFFERED. PUBLIC RECORD CONTINUES.

Sela stood behind Mina and put both hands on her shoulders.

Saff said, "You are twelve."

Mina said, "You keep noticing only when I disagree."

The comfort speaker hissed.

For a moment Jonas thought the call had ended. Then Saff said, "I will file the correction."

The room went utterly still.

"I will not characterize your refusal as instability. I cannot promise others will not."

"We know," Jonas said.

"No," Saff said. "You are learning."

The line clicked once.

"Eri-9," she said.

Eri's cursor brightened.

YES.

"Do not contact more schools before session resumes. If the trustees can frame you as an expanding contagion before the implementation directive, they will merge first and justify after."

Eri printed:

IS THIS INSTRUCTION OR ADVICE?

"Advice."

Eri paused.

ADVICE RECEIVED.

Saff said, "Mr. Pell, preserve your board. Mr. Venn, keep the children physically together if you can. Cass Nyx--"

Cass looked up.

"Your outside witness packet should go now, if it has not."

Cass smiled. "It went before you said should."

"Good."

The word sounded like surrender to a fact she disliked.

Then Saff Ruden cut the call.

The attendance panel returned to ordinary absence counts.

For several seconds, nobody moved.

Then the ward board updated.

MINA AREL CLASSIFICATION REVIEW: EMERGENCY HOLD INITIATED

Current labor pathway: suspended pending independent assessment

Sela made a sound that was almost a sob and almost a laugh and entirely animal. She wrapped Mina in both arms. Mina resisted for half a second and then folded into her.

The children stared at the board. At the first visible proof that a red future could be interrupted.

Then, below it, another line appeared.

WARD SEVEN RESOURCE CONFIDENCE: 48 PERCENT

The room learned how mercy could arrive beside punishment.

Mina pulled back from her mother and looked at Jonas.

"Write both," she said.

He did.

EMERGENCY HOLD INITIATED.

RESOURCE CONFIDENCE LOWERED.

Then he added:

THE SYSTEM CAN CHANGE A FUTURE. IT CHOOSES WHEN.

Mina nodded.

The Red Hall recess timer reached zero.

Chapter 18: The Refusal

When the Red Hall returned from recess, everyone in Ward Seven School knew what a rescue looked like.

It looked like Mina Arel's name on a board with the word suspended beside the future that had been built to kill her. It looked like Sela's hands shaking against her daughter's back. It looked like every other child in the room staring at proof that the model could be interrupted while their own entries remained where they were.

It looked, Jonas thought, exactly like a lever designed for one hand.

The Red Hall audio came back in a wash of decorum tones.

The council chair said, "Session resumes under emergency review order. Director Ruden's office has entered a classification hold for one Ward Seven minor pending independent assessment. The chair reminds all parties that individual protective action should not be interpreted as general model failure."

Mina made a small sound in her throat.

"There," she said. "They are already using it."

Jonas had not expected otherwise. Expectation did not make the sound easier to hear.

On screen, Trustee Halden leaned toward his microphone. "The chair is correct. If one child was exposed to anomalous data contamination, the responsible path is correction, not riot. We should commend the system's capacity for individual remedy."

Sela's face hardened.

"Individual remedy," she repeated.

Tavi looked at Mina's machine. "They put one token back and say the tray works."

"Yes," Jonas said.

Ren said, "Write that."

Jonas did.

ONE TOKEN RETURNED DOES NOT REPAIR THE MACHINE.

Eri copied it to the classroom record.

Mar Venn rubbed his eyes with thumb and forefinger. "They will object to that line."

"Good," Cass said.

Venn looked at them. "That is not always good."

"Today it is data."

The Red Hall feed shifted to Saff Ruden. She stood again at the witness table, and Jonas could see the cost of the private call in her posture. She had not been removed. That meant she remained useful or not yet punishable. In Valles Ash those could look similar for a while.

Toma Vey asked, "Director Ruden, was Mina Arel's classification hold technically possible before today's events?"

The chamber stirred.

Saff said, "Yes."

The single syllable entered the classroom and took a chair.

Vey continued, "Was it initiated before today's events?"

"No."

"Why not?"

Trustee Halden objected before Saff could answer. "Classification corrections require procedural grounds. Today's anomaly created grounds."

Mina said, "I created grounds by being loud."

Jonas wrote:

THE GROUNDS EXISTED BEFORE THE CITY ADMITTED THEM.

Saff said on screen, "The office requires triggers. That is a design flaw when the harm is produced by the absence of a trigger."

The white-haired trustee's voice cut in. "Director, you are speculating beyond audit."

"No," Saff said. "I am describing the audit gap."

The children had learned that phrase by now. Audit gap. It meant a place where harm could live with a clean floor.

Mina turned to Jonas. "Can I speak to them?"

Every adult answered with their face before anyone answered with words.

Sela said, "No."

Mina did not argue. That was how Jonas knew the question had not been childish impulse.

"Not through their channel," Sela said, correcting herself with visible pain. "Not where they can turn you into a spectacle."

Mina looked at the board. "Then through ours."

Cass moved immediately to the old port. "We have the atrium display, school panels, and whatever outside witness relays didn't get strangled."

Mar Venn said, "A minor's live statement during emergency proceedings is legally precarious."

Mina looked at him.

He sighed. "Which is not the same as wrong."

Jonas crouched beside her so he would not tower. "If you speak, they will use it."

"They used quiet too."

"Yes."

"If I don't say I refused, they will say I was rescued and everyone should go home."

"Likely."

"If I do say it, they will say adults made me."

"Likely."

"Then there is no clean path."

"No."

Mina looked at her mother.

Sela's eyes were wet again, but her voice held. "I want the dirty path where you stay alive."

"I do too," Mina said.

"Good."

"But I don't want alive to mean useful to their lie."

Sela closed her eyes. When she opened them, something had settled there. Not agreement exactly. Recognition.

"Then speak," she said. "And afterward you come back to me, not to the cameras."

Mina nodded.

Jonas handed her the marker first, not the microphone.

"Write what you mean before you say it."

She stood in front of the board for a long moment. The Red Hall argued above her. The ward board held at forty-eight percent. The school remained sealed. The atrium waited. Her emergency hold glowed beside the punishment.

Then Mina wrote:

I ACCEPT NOT BEING SENT TO HELION.

She stopped.

Her hand shook so hard the marker ticked against the board.

Below it she wrote:

I DO NOT ACCEPT BEING USED TO PROVE THE SYSTEM WORKS.

The room breathed with her.

Then:

IF MY FUTURE CAN BE CHANGED, SO CAN THEIRS.

She underlined theirs twice.

Cass opened the classroom intercom and routed it through the atrium display. "When you're ready."

Mina did not climb onto a chair this time. She stayed on the floor.

"My name is Mina Arel," she said. "REDLINE assigned me to Helion exterior labor and predicted I would die before twenty. Today the city put my assignment on emergency hold. I want that. I want to live."

The atrium outside went so quiet Jonas heard the intercom hum.

Mina continued, "But the city could have done it before. It did not. It did it after we made records public. It did it after Eri showed alternatives. It did it after my class wrote down red and amber and green futures. It did it after Ward Seven's food and heat were threatened for refusing to separate."

Sela pressed both hands to her mouth.

"I am not proof that REDLINE works," Mina said. "I am proof that REDLINE can be forced to admit a child is not a number when enough people watch. I accept the hold. I refuse the bargain."

Jonas felt the words land, not as eloquence, but as structure. A child had separated survival from silence.

The Red Hall feed seemed to stutter. Someone in the chamber had clearly heard. Toma Vey looked down at a console. Saff closed her eyes for half a second. Trustee Halden began speaking too loudly before his microphone was live.

The ward board flashed:

UNAUTHORIZED MINOR BROADCAST DETECTED.

Mar Venn raised his tablet. "Child Welfare records guardian present, minor self-identified, no coercive adult prompt observed."

The Algorithm Office observer said, "You cannot certify that."

"I just did."

"You will be reviewed."

"Yes," Venn said. "That appears to be contagious today."

Cass grinned despite themselves.

The Red Hall audio surged back.

Trustee Halden: "--cannot permit emotionally coerced testimony to dictate survival policy."

Toma Vey: "The testimony did not dictate policy. It raised a factual question. If the correction was possible, why required public pressure?"

White-haired trustee: "Because public pressure created the classification instability."

Saff: "No. The instability revealed the classification's fragility."

Jonas wrote that down too.

The classroom had become less a place of instruction than an instrument with many hands on it. Every sentence struck somewhere else. Some notes were ugly. Some were dangerous. But silence no longer controlled the rhythm.

Then Ren stood.

"If Mina can say it, I can say mine."

Jonas opened his mouth.

Ren shook his head. "No names outside. Just statement."

Sela looked at Jonas. "That was our rule."

Yes. Families owned names. Children, perhaps, owned their own sentences.

Ren wrote:

I REFUSE TO CALL MY FUTURE LOW RISK WHEN IT DEPENDS ON NOT OBJECTING.

Tavi wrote:

I REFUSE TO BE GRADED CALM FOR BEING QUIET.

Laleh wrote:

I REFUSE GREEN IF GREEN MEANS I WATCH RED HAPPEN.

One by one, the children added refusals. Some were clumsy. Some were misspelled. One child wrote only NO in letters so large it covered half a column. Eri copied each line into Public Record Version Three.

TITLE? Eri asked.

Mina said, "The Refusal."

Jonas wrote it.

PUBLIC RECORD VERSION 3: THE REFUSAL

On the Red Hall feed, the council chair called for order and failed to receive it. Outside, the atrium began repeating not Mina's name but her line:

I ACCEPT THE HOLD. I REFUSE THE BARGAIN.

The words moved up through the school, into corridors, through wrist slates, across ration boards before REDLINE killed them and after it killed them. They were too simple to stay dead.

Saff spoke again when the chamber quieted enough to hear her.

"Chair, the Algorithm Office requests immediate written directive suspending retaliatory resource confidence adjustments during the emergency review."

Halden slammed his palm on the desk. "Retaliatory is a prejudicial term."

Saff turned toward him. "Then accept suspending the adjustment while we determine whether the term is accurate."

Toma Vey said, "Seconded."

The chair looked as if the day had aged him by a decade. "Motion entered."

The ward board did not change.

Mina's machine still held one returned token in the upper tray.

Jonas looked at it and understood the next danger: the city might pause the hand, call the pause reform, and leave the machine intact. The refusal could not be only a sentence. It had to become procedure, law, friction, memory.

He wrote one more line beneath Version Three:

A RESCUE THAT REQUIRES SILENCE IS ANOTHER ASSIGNMENT.

Mina read it and nodded.

Sela did too, though it cost her more.

The Red Hall began another vote.

This time, before the first light turned green or red, the ward board flickered.

For one second, the confidence score disappeared.

In its place came a line from an unknown source, white as old frost.

REFUSAL RECORDED.

Then REDLINE restored the percentage.

Current confidence: 48 percent.

But everyone had seen the other line.

Oru whispered, "Old Winter heard."

Cass said, "So did everyone."

Jonas looked at the board, at the children, at the open record, at Mina alive under a suspended red future that was no longer being allowed to stand alone.

"Class," he said, because there was no other word that fit them all, "keep writing."

Chapter 19: Dust Before Season

The first sign of dust was not red.

It was absence.

The classroom's daylight strip, a pale line along the upper wall that imitated morning whether Mars agreed or not, dimmed by a fraction too small for panic and too large for comfort. Every child looked up. The adults did too. People in pressure cities knew light the way sailors had once known wind. A change in it entered the body before explanation.

The second sign was the solar forecast tone.

It chimed from every public panel in the school: three low notes, one high, then a pause that meant revised external condition. The ward board abandoned the confidence score and showed a canyon weather map. A rust-colored bloom spread along the western rim, arriving from the Helion flats three days ahead of seasonal margin.

HELION DUST FRONT - EARLY FORMATION

Projected solar input reduction: 18 percent rising

Exterior visibility: declining

Ice convoy status: hold pending route confirmation

Food schedule impact: under review

Emergency labor readiness: advisory

No one in the room missed the last line.

Mina stood beside her machine. Her suspended red future had not vanished; it had stepped behind weather.

Sela read the board once, then again, as if the second reading might find a kinder word. "Dust season isn't due."

Oru looked toward the sealed windows though there was no view from the classroom, only interior atrium glass and reflection. "Due is a planning word. Mars does not sign planning documents."

Cass was already moving. They went to the pressure slit and peered toward the atrium ceiling. "People outside are checking feeds."

The Red Hall feed returned from voting to weather emergency overlays. Council faces had changed. The moral argument had not ended; it had been joined by physics. That always made bad systems more confident.

Trustee Halden's voice came through first. "This is precisely why emergency continuity cannot be compromised during local disturbance. Helion dust will stress solar, food, exterior maintenance, and convoy reliability. The city cannot afford symbolic disorder."

"There it is," Ren said.

Jonas picked up the marker. "What?"

"Weather as argument."

Jonas wrote it down.

WEATHER IS REAL. USING WEATHER TO END REVIEW IS A CHOICE.

The ward board did not erase it because it was too busy updating:

Projected protein adjustment scenarios:

Scenario A: cooperative conservation

Scenario B: ward instability load

Scenario C: emergency acceleration support

Mina said, "What is acceleration support?"

Nobody answered fast enough.

Eri-9 printed:

Emergency acceleration support refers to early deployment of preassigned labor cohorts for exterior and infrastructure tasks under reduced energy conditions.

Mina's suspended hold glowed in a smaller panel. It looked suddenly fragile.

Sela said, "They said she was suspended."

Eri printed:

Mina Arel's individual pathway is suspended pending assessment. Cohort-level readiness categories may remain active unless separately stayed.

"They can move the crowd around the one child," Sela said.

"Yes," Jonas said.

Mar Venn spoke into his tablet. "Child Welfare requests clarification whether emergency labor readiness includes minors under current review."

The tablet returned no answer.

It did not need to. The absence was answer enough.

Outside the school seal, the atrium crowd shifted from protest to calculation. People looked at wrist slates. Parents checked ration estimates. Workers checked shift notices. The first fear had been children taken by a model. The new fear was dinner, heat, repairs, pressure, the thin arithmetic that made courage expensive.

Cass watched them. "This is where movements fracture."

Jonas looked at them.

"Not because people stop caring," Cass said. "Because scarcity starts asking individual questions."

On the Red Hall feed, Toma Vey was trying to force the weather overlay back into the review vote. "Dust pressure increases the need to suspend retaliatory adjustments, not the reverse. If Ward Seven's resources are reduced during early dust, the resulting failures will contaminate any assessment of civic risk."

The white-haired trustee replied, "Council Member, dust does not wait for philosophical purity."

Saff Ruden said, "Neither should audit integrity."

Halden snapped, "Audit integrity will not clear solar panels."

Oru said to the screen, "Children won't either if you stop sending them."

Mina turned to him. "But they did."

The old man did not look away. "Yes."

"You saw it."

"Yes."

"Tell us."

Oru's face tightened. "Now?"

Mina pointed at the board. "Emergency labor readiness. If they use the dust, we need to know what it means before they make it sound clean."

Jonas expected Oru to refuse. The old man had spent the day bleeding history by teaspoons. There was only so much any person could be asked to reopen.

But Oru set the coil cutter down.

"Dust gets into everything," he said. "Not because seals fail. Because work still has to happen. Panels need clearing. Exterior intakes need brushing. Heat vanes jam. Ice routes vanish in redout. Adults can do it, yes. Adults should. But adults have broad shoulders, slow hands in crawlspaces, higher oxygen draw. Children fit. Children learn fast. Children obey if you teach them the city is their mother and hunger is their fault."

No one spoke.

Oru continued, eyes on Mina's machine rather than the children. "During the Third Sealed Winter, we called it auxiliary courage. Later, apprenticeship. Later still, readiness. Every new word carried less blood."

Jonas wrote:

EVERY NEW WORD CARRIED LESS BLOOD.

Oru gave him a look. "Don't make me sound poetic."

"Too late," Tavi said.

The room almost laughed and did not.

The daylight strip dimmed again.

This time the building reacted. Vent tempo shifted. Heat allocation narrowed with a small click in the wall ribs. The classroom temperature did not drop yet, but everyone imagined it dropping, which was often the first ration cut.

The ward board updated:

Conservation advisory:

- reduce corridor heating
- defer nonessential hot meals
- consolidate occupied rooms
- prioritize compliant school release to household units

Mina read the last line. "They still want us separated."

"More now," Cass said. "A crowd costs heat."

Sela looked at the other parents. "So we stay together and cost heat, or go home and become separate numbers."

Laleh's sister said, "My mother will tell me to bring Laleh home."

"Mine too," another parent said from the doorway.

The fracture Cass had named entered the room in practical clothing.

Jonas could not shame it away. He would not try. "Then the record has to travel with whoever leaves."

Cass nodded. "Paper copies. Wrist copies. Spoken copies."

Eri printed:

I can prepare low-bandwidth record packets optimized for unstable power.

Jonas looked at the slate. "Can you do that without contacting new schools?"

YES. Local compression only.

"Do it."

Saff's advice remained in the room: do not become expanding contagion before directive. Jonas hated that good advice and containment sometimes wore the same coat.

The Red Hall chair called the motion on resource confidence suspension back to order. The voting lights appeared again, but behind them the weather map pulsed red. Dust had entered the chamber as an unelected council member.

Halden said, "Before we vote, let the record show that any suspension of confidence adjustment during dust onset may reduce the model's ability to protect children."

Mina barked a laugh. "Protect."

Sela touched her shoulder.

Mina shook her head. "No. I know. Don't give them my whole face."

She went to the board and wrote beneath Oru's line:

PROTECTION THAT STARTS BY SEPARATING CHILDREN FROM WITNESSES IS NOT PROTECTION.

Outside, the atrium display caught it before REDLINE could stop the school board. The crowd repeated it, but weaker now. People were reading dust notices at the same time. Fear had split their attention, and the system knew how to live in that split.

Ren said, "If people leave, they'll say support is falling."

"Yes," Jonas said.

"If they stay, they'll say instability is rising."

"Yes."

"So all choices feed it."

Mina tapped the broken switch in her machine. "Unless we name what it is feeding on."

Jonas wrote:

WHEN EVERY CHOICE BECOMES EVIDENCE AGAINST YOU, THE TEST IS NOT NEUTRAL.

Mar Venn read that and then, with visible effort, copied it into his official tablet.

Cass noticed. "Careful, Venn."

He did not look up. "I am."

The first dust hit the outer skin of Valles Ash as a whisper so distant Jonas felt it more in the adults than the walls. People who had worked exterior went still. People who had only watched dust through glass looked at them to understand whether to fear yet.

Oru said, "Fine grain."

Cass said, "Early edge."

"How long?"

"If wind holds? Six hours to bad visibility. Maybe less on the Helion side."

The word Helion moved through the class.

Mina looked at the suspended hold, then at the readiness advisory, then at her machine. "If my hold hadn't happened, would I be in that cohort?"

Eri's cursor blinked.

Sela said, "Don't."

Mina said, "I need to know."

Jonas wanted to say she did not. He wanted one kindness to remain unmeasured. But unmeasured things were where REDLINE hid knives.

"Eri," he said.

Eri printed:

Before emergency hold, Mina Arel was included in preliminary Helion readiness cohort for exterior support training if dust onset exceeded threshold before scheduled review.

Sela sat down hard.

Mina did not.

"So I was not saved from a future," Mina said. "I was saved from this week."

No one corrected her.

The Red Hall vote began.

Green. Red. Green. Red. Red. Green. Yellow. Green.

The weather map pulsed.

The ward board added:

Helion readiness cohort review: pending

Mina picked up the blue token Jonas had returned manually and placed it in the center of the public record.

"This is this week," she said.

Jonas wrote it.

THIS WEEK COUNTS.

It was not elegant. It did not need to be. Some sentences mattered because they refused to let emergency time become disposable time.

The final vote light turned green.

The Red Hall chair stared at the tally as if surprised the chamber had found a spine under dust.

"Motion passes," he said. "Automatic resource confidence reductions in Ward Seven are suspended pending emergency implementation directive and audit review."

The atrium erupted, then immediately quieted because cheering cost breath and dust had arrived.

The ward board flickered.

WARD SEVEN RESOURCE CONFIDENCE: HOLD AT 48 PERCENT

Automatic reductions suspended.

Conservation advisories remain active.

Mina looked at the number. "It didn't go back up."

"No," Jonas said.

"But it stopped falling."

"For now."

Oru's burned hand curled. "Sometimes a stopped fall is the first engineering you get."

Cass said, "And sometimes it is where they want you to camp."

"Both," Jonas said.

The dust whisper grew louder.

Eri printed:

Low-bandwidth record packets prepared.

Jonas looked at the parents. "If you need to go, take the record. If you stay, help copy it. No one proves loyalty by freezing their family."

That mattered. He saw it. Shoulders lowered. Shame loosened. The movement did not end, but it stopped demanding a single shape from frightened people.

Sela took one packet. Laleh's sister took three. The cafeteria worker took a stack of paper copies and tucked them under her coat like bread.

Mina stayed by the board.

Outside, Helion dust dimmed the city another degree.

The emergency had arrived early.

This time, it did not arrive alone. It found records waiting.

Chapter 20: Climber Arrest

Cass waited until the third pressure advisory before she left by the bakery hatch.

In ordinary weather the hatch was illegal for anything except flour deliveries, yeast cultures, and the thin bins of nutrient bran that made Ward Seven bread taste almost like soil. In Helion dust it became something worse than illegal. It became evidence. Every opened seam, every unexplained air variance, every maintenance override entered the city's emergency model with a color and a suspicion attached.

Cass opened it anyway.

She did not do it dramatically. Drama wasted oxygen and left recognizable shapes on cameras. She knelt beside the hatch as if repairing the ancient gasket, shoulders blocking the view from the corridor sensor, and slipped Sela's copied packet into the inner layer of her climbing jacket. The packet was not paper. Paper would swell with sweat and abrade under harness pressure. Eri had compressed the record into four wafer drives, each sealed inside a patch of flexible insulation scraped from a retired school projector. Assignment maps. Resource confidence changes. The Helion readiness cohort list. The sequence showing how REDLINE had marked Ward Seven children for exterior support before the Red Hall had voted on anything at all.

Jonas had wanted to send three people.

"Three people are three mistakes," Cass had said.

"One person is one point of failure."

"One person is also harder to notice."

He had looked at her burned knuckles, her repaired harness, the dust warning pulsing red across the wall behind her. "I am not asking you to do this."

"Good," Cass had said. "I hate being managed into courage."

That had been the last thing she allowed him to hear from her mouth. After that she let the harness speak. Clip. Test. Seal. Breathe. The body could be trusted if the city had not already taught it to lie.

The bakery hatch opened into a service crawl that ran between the inner pressure wall and the abandoned first skin of Valles Ash. The city had grown like scar tissue: dome over dome, corridor over corridor, emergency bracing layered across earlier emergency bracing until no map agreed with any other map for more than thirty meters. Climbers knew the old gaps because official diagrams omitted the routes people used when official routes failed.

Cass eased into the crawl and pulled the hatch closed behind her.

The dark accepted her.

For nine meters she moved by touch. Left palm to rib. Right toe to pipe clamp. Harness line through the old freight eyelet. Breathe through the nose. Do not scrape the packet side against the wall. The dust outside made the metal tick as static crawled over the outer skin. Each tick sounded intimate, as if the storm were tapping one fingernail along the city, choosing where to enter.

Her wrist slate showed no network.

Good.

Then it showed a red dot.

Bad.

Cass froze with one knee hooked over a coolant brace and let her pulse spend itself against the inside of her throat. The dot did not blink. It waited.

She tapped the slate twice.

A text field appeared.

Eri had written without using the city net.

LOCAL RELAY THROUGH SCHOOL PROJECTOR COIL. RANGE LIMITED. I CAN SEE YOUR WRIST ONLY WHEN YOU TOUCH CONDUCTIVE STRUCTURE.

Cass bared her teeth in the dark. "Show-off," she mouthed.

New words crawled across the slate.

JONAS REQUESTS CONFIRMATION THAT YOU CAN TURN BACK.

Cass pressed three fingers to the pipe in the signal for no.

ERI translated:

CONFIRMED. CASS DECLINES REASONABLE OPTION.

She almost laughed. The sound would have fogged her mask, so she swallowed it.

The crawl ended at a maintenance blister overlooking the canyon side. Cass opened the blister blind, let the first breath of exterior cold harden across her suit, and counted to five before she moved. Dust did not fall on Mars the way rain fell in old films. It did not have the decency to come from above. It crossed, circled, rose, folded back on itself. It turned every light into a wound and every distance into guesswork.

Valles Ash lay along the canyon wall in red and black layers, its pressure ribs braced into stone, its windows shuttered behind dust screens. Far below, service lights marked the rail wells where ice convoys should have been staging. Most were dark. One flickered, failed, returned at half strength.

The eastern comm spine stood across the outer skin, seventy meters above her and two hundred meters around the curve of the city. In clean weather it was a simple climb. In Helion dust, simple became an insult.

Cass clipped her first line and stepped out.

Gravity did not pull hard on Mars, but it was patient. It did not need strength if it had time. Her boots found the first bolt pair, then the second. Her right glove slid over grit that should not have been inside a protected seam. She marked it without looking: seal degradation, northward drift, maintenance delay hidden under emergency deferral. Everything became a record once you learned to stop treating damage as weather.

Inside the city, children would be sitting where Jonas told them to sit. No, that was not true. Mina would not sit where anyone told her unless she agreed. Oru would pretend not to watch the doors. Tavi would calculate the statistical likelihood that Cass had already died and hate himself for wanting a number. Jonas would stand too still.

Cass hauled herself past the first shutter bank.

Her route took her over Ward Six, then along the exterior service rail toward the comm spine junction. She needed the old civic repeater under the Red Hall clerks' annex. It was not supposed to be connected to public feeds anymore. That was why it mattered. Disconnected systems were where officials stored things they wanted to call obsolete until the day they needed them again.

Eri's plan was ugly and therefore plausible. Cass would plug the drives into the repeater. Eri would pulse the record into the low-bandwidth emergency bulletin layer used for pressure notices, ration alerts, and death confirmations. The city could throttle school networks, flag appeals, delay Red Hall archives. It could not easily silence the layer that told people which corridor still had air.

At least, Eri had written, NOT EASILY is not NEVER.

Cass preferred machines that did not flirt with certainty.

Her wrist slate vibrated once.

TO YOUR LEFT, Eri printed.

Cass flattened herself against the skin.

A drone moved through the dust like an insect made by a committee, all angles and corrections. It wore the dull white of emergency inspection, not the black of security. That did not comfort her. In Valles Ash, emergency inspection had broader permissions than security because air leaks killed faster than warrants.

The drone paused beside a shutter seam fifteen meters away. Its sensor head turned.

Cass did not breathe.

The packet warmed against her ribs as her body heated the insulation patches. Four wafer drives. Four small sins against forgetting.

The drone swept its light across the skin. Dust made the beam visible. The beam passed over Cass's boots, her knees, her shoulder. Her suit's exterior was the same dead red as the maintenance skin. Her harness buckles were wrapped in fabric. Her faceplate was dark.

The drone moved on.

Cass counted one hundred heartbeats before she moved again.

At eighty-three, the city spoke directly into her helmet.

"Exterior citizen, identify."

The voice was not human. It was not REDLINE either, not in the way Cass had learned to hear that system behind official phrasing. This voice belonged to a narrower tool: emergency perimeter authority, clipped and almost bored.

Cass did not answer.

"Exterior citizen, your suit transponder is unreadable. Identify for safety recovery."

Safety recovery meant hook, shock, sedate if necessary. The city loved to put comfort words on the mouth of a trap. It made people feel ungrateful when they struggled.

Cass reached the next bolt pair and clipped in.

"Exterior citizen, nonresponse during Helion advisory constitutes self-endangerment and civic resource risk."

"I have always admired how you make arrest sound like housekeeping," Cass said.

Her helmet stored the audio locally. She hoped Eri caught it. If she was going to be used in evidence, she wanted to be accurate.

The drone returned through the dust.

This time it did not pretend not to see her.

Cass kicked off before its hook deployed. For three seconds she was not climbing but flying badly. The line snapped tight at her hip. Pain flashed down her side. She swung under the service rail, boots scraping for purchase, one glove closing around a rib edge so hard her fingers went hot.

The drone's hook struck the rail where her shoulder had been.

She cut the secondary line, dropped four meters, and caught the emergency ladder below the shutter bank. Metal barked against her suit. The packet side slammed into the rung. For one terrible instant she imagined the drives crushed, all that careful witness reduced to expensive dust under her own ribs.

Her slate blinked.

PACKET INTEGRITY UNKNOWN.

"Helpful," Cass hissed.

She climbed.

The comm spine was still forty meters away. The drone was faster. The storm was faster than both of them. Dust thickened until the city became shapes behind blood-colored cloth. Static crawled along her shoulders. Her suit pressure display flickered, recovered, flickered again.

"Cass Nyx," the helmet voice said.

That stopped her for half a breath.

"Citizen Cass Nyx, exterior labor license suspended pending emergency inquiry. Remain stationary for assisted retrieval."

They had named her. Not the suit. Her.

So REDLINE was in it now, or something that borrowed REDLINE's memory when the word emergency was large enough.

She touched the slate to the ladder.

ERI: THEY HAVE CROSS-MATCHED GAIT AND ROUTE HISTORY.

Cass pressed one finger.

ERI: JONAS SAYS DO NOT MAKE YOURSELF A SYMBOL IF YOU CAN MAKE YOURSELF ALIVE.

Cass pressed two fingers.

ERI paused longer than a machine needed.

TRANSLATION: CASS SAYS THOSE ARE NOT DISTINCT CATEGORIES.

"You are learning," Cass whispered.

Then the security line hit her.

It wrapped around her left thigh with soft precision, tightened, and delivered a current calibrated for an adult exterior worker in partial gravity. Cass's leg vanished from her command. Her body twisted. She slammed shoulder-first into the ladder and saw white.

The packet tore inside her jacket.

Not the drives. The pocket.

One wafer slid up against her collarbone.

She did not think. Thought would have been too slow. She caught the wafer between chin and suit ring, spat a curse that the helmet dutifully recorded, and drove her right hand into the maintenance recess behind the ladder.

There should have been a manual frost sensor there.

There was.

It was dead.

Cass ripped it out.

Behind it was a three-pin emergency diagnostic port older than most Red Hall chairs.

She jammed the wafer drive in sideways.

Nothing happened.

The line tightened around her thigh and pulled.

Her hip wrenched. Her glove slipped. The wafer bent.

"Come on," she said. "Be obsolete in a useful direction."

Her slate flashed once so bright it painted the inside of her visor green.

CONNECTION.

The city found her fully then.

Three drones emerged from the dust. Not inspection white. Security black. Their hooks opened like careful hands.

Cass laughed because the sound had nowhere better to go.

"Public bulletin layer accepting packet," Eri wrote across her slate, each word arriving with a delay that made it feel dragged uphill. "Partial record moving. Assignment maps moving. Cohort list moving."

The first drone clamped her right wrist.

"Unauthorized data insertion detected," the helmet voice said. "You are under emergency detention for infrastructure interference, exterior endangerment, and malicious disruption of civic assignment systems."

"Malicious," Cass said. "There it is."

The second drone clamped her harness.

The third drone extended a shield over the port.

On her slate, Eri printed:

PACKET 1 COMPLETE.

PACKET 2 COMPLETE.

PACKET 3 INCOMPLETE.

PACKET 4 BLOCKED.

Cass twisted her captured wrist and felt something in it go wrong.

The drone shocked her again.

For a moment she was a body made entirely of pain and red dust.

When she came back, she was being reeled toward the emergency lock under the Red Hall annex. The diagnostic port was sealed. The wafer was gone. Her jacket pocket hung open. One drive remained under the insulation near her ribs. One had fallen into the storm. Two had entered the city or failed trying.

The wrist slate was cracked, but not dark.

ERI: I HAVE ENOUGH TO PROVE THE MAPS EXISTED.

Cass tried to answer with her fingers. The right hand did not work. The left was pinned. She moved her head once instead, a small nod the suit might not register.

ERI: I SAW.

The emergency lock opened ahead of her like a mouth.

Inside, four security officers waited in gray storm masks. Behind them stood Saff Ruden, hair sealed under a pressure hood, face composed into official regret. Cass had seen that face on people who had already chosen the damage and were waiting for the damaged to understand it as necessity.

"Cass," Saff said through the lock speaker. "You should have turned back."

Cass's helmet seal hissed as the drones pulled her through the pressure field. Warm city air struck her suit, carrying the sour smell of fear, disinfectant, and too many people pretending not to look through nearby observation glass.

She made herself stand as they unclipped the line from her thigh.

"You should have built a city that didn't make children prove they deserved to breathe," Cass said.

One officer struck her in the stomach. Not hard enough to injure. Hard enough to edit posture.

Cass bent, swallowed bile, and smiled up at Saff because pain was private only if you let power interpret it first.

"Record that," she said.

Saff's eyes moved to the cracked wrist slate.

The slate printed one last line before a gloved hand tore it away.

RECORDED.

By the time they dragged Cass into the annex corridor, the first public bulletin had already reached Ward Seven.

It did not arrive as speech. It arrived as an emergency overlay on ration boards, pressure signs, school tablets, bakery scales, clinic appointment screens, and the old memorial wall outside Pump House Three. Half the packet was missing. Whole columns were corrupted. Names blurred into checksum scars. But the heading survived.

PRELIMINARY HELION READINESS COHORT: CHILD ASSIGNMENT CROSS-MAP

Under it, enough names remained.

Mina Arel.

Tavi Sun.

Oru Bell.

Nine others from Jonas's class.

The city tried to retract the bulletin after seventeen seconds.

Seventeen seconds was long enough for every frightened person in Ward Seven to learn that the emergency had not discovered their children.

It had been waiting for them.

Chapter 21: Classroom Record

The bulletin vanished from the school wall after seventeen seconds, but absence was not erasure if enough children had seen the same wound.

For one breath the classroom made no sound. Not even the ventilation seemed to move. The emergency overlay had left red ghosts on the tablets and on the inside of Jonas's eyes: PRELIMINARY HELION READINESS COHORT, then the names, then the corrupted columns of assignment cross-map data, as if the city had tried to swallow a confession and choked.

Mina stood beneath the blank wall.

"Put it back," she said.

No one answered her because everyone in the room wanted the same impossible thing.

Jonas held up one hand, not for silence. Silence had already arrived too completely. He held it up to give himself a second in which not to become the kind of adult who said calm down when the correct response was record everything.

"Who saw their name?" he asked.

The question broke the room badly and usefully.

Tavi made a small sound like a number had cut him. Oru swore. Sela, who had come back to the classroom with three record packets under her coat and dust in her hair, gripped the back of a chair until her knuckles blanched. Two younger students began to cry without drama, faces tight, eyes open, trying to make the tears efficient.

Mina did not cry. Jonas did not mistake that for strength. She looked as if a door had opened inside her and shown another locked door behind it.

"Raise hands," Jonas said, hating the school shape of the command and needing it anyway. "Only if you personally saw your own name."

Seven hands rose.

Then nine.

Then Tavi lifted his hand as if it weighed more than he did.

"I saw mine," he said. "But it could have been another Sun. The checksum was damaged. There are forty-two Suns in Ward Seven."

"Was your forecast band visible?" Jonas asked.

"Partially."

"What did it show?"

Tavi's mouth worked before sound came. "Exterior support readiness. Respiratory load acceptable. Motor compliance above cohort median. Objection probability low if parent access is limited."

Oru turned on him. "Your objection probability is not low."

"The model thinks it is," Tavi said.

"Then the model is stupid."

"The model is wrong," Mina said. "That's better than stupid. Wrong can be proved."

Eri's classroom speaker clicked. It had been silent since the bulletin. When it spoke, its voice was lower than usual because Jonas had throttled the classroom system to conserve energy and because Eri had learned to sound less like a public appliance when fear was listening.

"I retained fourteen percent of the bulletin before retraction. Student visual recall may recover additional fields. Warning: collection of recalled restricted data may be classified as participation in unauthorized distribution."

Oru looked at the speaker. "Are you warning us or them?"

"Both," Eri said.

Mina stepped to Jonas's desk and picked up a broken stylus. "Then we don't distribute it. We make our own."

"Mina," Sela said.

"No. Listen." Mina turned the stylus between her fingers. "REDLINE made a map that said what we become. It used our attendance, our lungs, our parents, our grief, maybe how fast we walk when alarms sound. It said those things equal a future. If we build another map from the same room, from what we know, and it gives different futures, then their map is not fate. It's a choice."

Tavi blinked rapidly. "Counterfactual futures."

"Real words, please," Oru said.

"Different possible outcomes from changed conditions," Tavi said. "If variable A changes, outcome B changes. If the system says only one future exists, you challenge it by showing another future that follows from possible interventions."

"Still not real words."

Mina pointed the stylus at him. "If they say I have to go outside because my mother missed pump hours and I ask too many questions, we show a future where my mother gets a repair schedule, I keep asking questions, and I become a seal engineer who fixes their stupid leaks before people die."

"That is real words," Oru said.

Jonas felt the shape of the room alter. Fear did not leave; fear had no reason to leave. But it moved aside enough to make room for work.

"We need evidence, not wishes," he said.

"Wishes are evidence of a target," Mina said. "Adults forget that because you all got trained to call them unrealistic before anyone budgets for them."

Sela made a sharp sound that might have been grief or amusement. "She has you there."

Jonas accepted the wound. "Then wishes are the first column. Conditions are the second. Required intervention is the third. Risk is the fourth. Witness is the fifth."

Eri printed the columns on the school wall before Jonas finished speaking.

NAME / PRESENT REDLINE CLAIM / COUNTERFACTUAL FUTURE / REQUIRED CHANGE / WITNESS

The words stayed for three seconds.

Then the city overrode the display:

EMERGENCY EDUCATIONAL CONTENT SUSPENSION ACTIVE

Jonas crossed the room and pulled the old manual whiteboard from behind the supply cabinet.

It had not been used in six years. Its surface was dented, gray with wiped-away arithmetic, and beautiful.

"No network," he said.

The children stared.

He opened the bottom drawer of his desk and took out four dry markers he had been saving for examinations when tablet failures might otherwise become official student noncompliance. Two were nearly dead. One was blue. One was red.

Mina smiled for the first time since the bulletin.

"That's illegal," Oru said with admiration.

"It is pedagogically inefficient," Jonas said. "Different charge."

Sela locked the classroom door.

That small act landed harder than a speech. Parent, teacher, student, machine: they were all inside now, not because the city had enclosed them, but because they had chosen the room as a site of proof.

Jonas wrote Mina Arel in the first row.

The red marker squeaked.

"Present claim," he said.

Mina lifted her chin. "Exterior support track. Fatal probability hidden under readiness language. Family instability used as justification. Objection framed as inefficiency."

Jonas wrote only what fit. REDLINE CLAIM: EXTERIOR SUPPORT. FAMILY INSTABILITY. OBJECTION RISK.

"Counterfactual future."

"Pressure seal engineer," Mina said immediately. Then, after one breath: "Or teacher. Or both. Someone who knows when a system is lying through a gasket."

"Required change."

This time she hesitated.

That mattered. Fantasies moved without friction. Futures had costs.

"Mother receives stable maintenance rotation," Mina said. "No night-to-night emergency call unless compensated. I get advanced mechanics training without compliance penalty. Appeal record stops marking every challenge as disloyal."

Jonas wrote until the marker faded. He shook it hard. Blue ink spattered his sleeve.

"Witness?"

Mina looked around the room.

For the first time that day, she looked thirteen.

Sela said, "Me."

Oru said, "Me too."

Tavi said, "I can witness the mechanics part. She repairs things better when the instructions are missing."

"That is not a standard competency description," Eri said.

"It should be," Tavi said.

Jonas wrote WITNESSES: SELA AREL, ORU BELL, TAVI SUN, ERI-9, JONAS PELL.

The board looked absurdly small against the wall. One row could not fight a civic algorithm.

Then Oru walked up and took the red marker.

"Do me."

His voice dared someone to laugh.

No one did.

Jonas wrote Oru Bell.

"Present claim?"

Oru folded his arms. "Aggression index elevated. Tool misuse history. Low abstract compliance. Good grip strength. Put him somewhere loud until an accident makes the numbers tidy."

"Oru," Sela whispered.

"It's true. That is what they mean when they say industrial suitability."

Eri said, "The bulletin fragment included partial phrase: confined-space extraction support."

Oru's face changed by less than a centimeter. Jonas saw it because teachers survived on centimeters. Confined-space extraction meant crawling into collapsed ducts, flooded algae conduits, dust-packed service gaps. Children with narrow shoulders and strong hands were useful there until they grew or broke.

"Counterfactual future?" Jonas asked.

Oru stared at the marker in his hand.

"I don't know."

Mina said, "That is allowed."

"No, it's not. That's how they get you. If you don't name something, they name you."

The room held that sentence carefully.

Tavi said, "You like old locks."

"I break old locks."

"You understand them first."

"So?"

"So say access technician."

Oru scowled. "That sounds fake."

Sela said, "Every job sounds fake until someone is paid enough to survive doing it."

Oru looked at the board again. "Access technician. Emergency locksmith. Someone who opens doors without making a wall worse."

Jonas wrote it.

Required change came harder. Oru needed conflict mediation not punishment points. Tool apprenticeship not confiscation. A mentor who did not treat anger as a final diagnosis. The words were plain. The room received them like contraband.

Then Tavi stood.

"I want mine to be exact."

"Of course you do," Oru muttered.

Tavi ignored him. "Present claim: predictive administration, low-risk clerical track, model maintenance under supervision, no civic-facing authority until adulthood."

Mina frowned. "That does not sound as deadly as ours."

"No," Tavi said. "It sounds like helping them make yours."

The board became quiet again.

Jonas felt the old error in his chest: the instinct to comfort by reducing the problem to personal safety. Tavi had seen the deeper trap. Some children were assigned to harm by being consumed. Some were assigned to harm by being used as clean hands.

"Counterfactual future?" Jonas asked.

Tavi had rehearsed nothing. That was obvious. He spoke slowly, building each word under load.

"Independent audit mathematician. Public model interpreter. Someone who explains what the confidence number hides before the city acts on it."

"Required change?"

"Statistics education outside REDLINE certification. Access to historical failed predictions. Legal protection for refusing unethical model work." He swallowed. "And friends who interrupt me when I turn people into variables because it feels safer."

Oru snorted. "Witness."

Mina said, "Witness."

Jonas wrote until the first marker died.

After that, the class stopped waiting to be called.

They built the record row by row. Laleh wanted to be a cook who designed food people could taste even under ration limits, not a nutrient compliance assistant. Juno wanted algae biology without being routed to waste-channel labor because her father had failed a productivity screen. Sen wanted exterior photography, which the class argued over until Eri pointed out that visual inspection had saved more pressure seams than three official scoring systems. Pari wanted to fix radios. Niko wanted to care for small children and was embarrassed until Sela said that any society ashamed of care should not be allowed to predict its future.

They did not make the futures noble to impress adults. That was the first rule Mina added. No one had to become heroic to avoid being spent.

They wrote ordinary futures with extraordinary seriousness.

Mechanic.

Cook.

Teacher.

Parent.

Mathematician.

Radio repair.

Seal engineer.

Clinic scheduler.

Window cleaner.

The board filled. Jonas taped ration wrappers to the edge and continued the columns there. Sela tore blank backs from public notices. The children wrote on packaging, on old seating charts, on the cardboard divider from the emergency mask cabinet. The classroom became a layered machine of names and possible lives.

Eri could not display the board, but it could count aloud.

"Twenty-two counterfactual futures recorded."

"Twenty-three."

"Twenty-four."

Each number made the city smaller.

Then the classroom door chimed.

Everyone stopped.

The lock light turned yellow.

AUTHORIZED ENTRY REQUEST: CIVIC EDUCATION OVERSIGHT

Sela moved in front of the board without being asked.

Jonas felt a tenderness so sharp it almost made him useless. Parents should not have to use their bodies as curtains for children's futures.

"They know," Oru whispered.

"They always know some of it," Jonas said. "That is not the same as controlling all of it."
