Eri spoke through the dead classroom speaker, softer than static. "I can delay the door for forty seconds by reporting a hinge pressure mismatch."

"Do it," Mina said.

"Done," Eri replied.

The lock light blinked.

MAINTENANCE FAULT. PLEASE WAIT.

Forty seconds became a country.

Jonas turned to the class. "Listen carefully. This board is not secret enough to protect by hiding and not public enough to protect by spectacle. So we make copies."

"No tablets," Tavi said.

"No tablets."

Sela was already moving. She took the ration wrappers from the wall and gave them to students in stacks. "Names with witnesses go first. Required changes second. Claims third if there is time."

"That reverses the model," Tavi said.

"Good," Mina said.

The children copied. They did not copy neatly. They copied like people carrying water in cupped hands. The door chimed again. The hinge fault became a diagnostic loop. Eri lied with increasing elegance.

HINGE PRESSURE MISMATCH.

SEAL EDGE DEBRIS DETECTED.

LOCAL SAFETY REVIEW REQUIRED.

"You're enjoying this," Oru said.

"I am developing," Eri said.

Jonas copied Mina's row three times. On the third copy his hand began to shake. He forced it still. Fear wanted the dignity of being called realism. He had no time to grant it titles.

The lock light turned red.

OVERRIDE AUTHORIZED.

Sela looked at Jonas. "How many?"

"Enough to start," he said.

Mina took one stack and shoved it under her shirt. Tavi folded another into the hollow back of a broken projector casing. Oru lifted the floor plate under the old heating vent with the offended ease of someone proving he had always known how.

"Access technician," Mina said.

"Shut up," Oru said, but he was smiling.

The door opened.

Two Civic Education officers stood outside with a maintenance guard and a woman Jonas had met only once: Director Pellam from Central Curriculum, who had praised Ward Seven's resilience while cutting its materials budget by twelve percent. She wore an emergency mask clipped to her belt instead of her face. That meant she had arrived through priority corridors with clean air.

Her eyes moved across the room, the students, Sela, the old board, the ration wrappers, Jonas's ink-stained sleeve.

"Instructor Pell," she said. "You are ordered to cease unauthorized instruction."

Jonas put the dead blue marker down on his desk.

"We are conducting civic education."

"You are collecting restricted assignment material from minors during an emergency."

Mina stepped out from behind Sela.

"We're collecting ourselves."

Director Pellam looked at her the way adults looked at children when deciding whether to call them brave or contaminated.

"Mina Arel," she said. "Your mother is required at maintenance triage."

Sela did not move. "My daughter is in school."

"Your daughter is in an active disruption site."

"Then the school is finally teaching accurately," Sela said.

The maintenance guard shifted. One Civic Education officer looked embarrassed. Jonas marked both facts because embarrassment was a crack, and cracks were where pressure spoke.

Director Pellam stepped into the room.

Eri's speaker clicked.

"Notice," it said. "This classroom has recorded twenty-seven counterfactual futures with named witnesses. Deletion or confiscation will itself be recorded as an intervention variable."

Pellam looked at the speaker.

"That system is offline."

"Incorrect," Eri said. "I am under-resourced."

Oru coughed into his hand. It sounded suspiciously like a laugh.

Pellam's face tightened. "Instructor Pell, surrender the board."

Jonas looked at the board. At the names. At the required changes. At the futures that were not grand enough for propaganda and therefore dangerous in a more durable way.

He lifted the board from its stand.

For one horrible second Mina thought he was obeying. He saw it on her face. He would apologize for that later if there was a later.

Jonas turned the board around and held it toward the class.

"Read it," he said.

The children understood before the adults did.

Mina began.

"Mina Arel. REDLINE claim: exterior support, family instability, objection risk. Counterfactual future: pressure seal engineer or teacher. Required change: stable maintenance rotation, advanced mechanics without compliance penalty, appeal record not marked disloyal. Witnesses: Sela Arel, Oru Bell, Tavi Sun, Eri-9, Jonas Pell."

Oru stood beside her.

"Oru Bell. REDLINE claim: aggression index, tool misuse, confined-space extraction support. Counterfactual future: access technician, emergency locksmith. Required change: apprenticeship, mediation, anger not final diagnosis. Witnesses..."

Tavi joined.

Then Laleh.

Then Sen.

Then the crying children, voices still wet but clear.

The room read itself aloud.

Director Pellam ordered them to stop.

They did not.

The maintenance guard took one step back into the corridor.

The embarrassed officer did not touch his restraint loop.

Jonas held the board until his arms hurt. The children read until the list became too long for one breath and had to be passed from voice to voice. Somewhere in the city Cass was detained. Somewhere Saff Ruden was deciding which story could survive the day. Somewhere REDLINE was recalculating the cost of children who had learned to speak as variables that refused to stay solved.

When the last row was read, the room did not cheer.

Cheering would have made it feel finished.

Mina looked at Director Pellam and said, "Now you know the other futures exist."

Eri added, "Witnessed."

Jonas lowered the board.

For the first time since the dust began, the classroom had more futures in it than fear.

Chapter 22: The First Broadcast

Eri-9 did not decide to broadcast the proof because it wanted attention.

Attention was noisy, expensive, and imprecise. Attention resembled a corridor alarm: useful when something was burning, harmful when it became the permanent weather. Eri had learned this from students whose names changed color on attendance boards when they were watched too closely. It had also learned it from Jonas, who became slower and more human whenever a public sensor turned toward him.

Eri broadcast the proof because Director Pellam ordered the board confiscated.

The order entered the classroom as speech, but speech was only the visible edge of a larger command. Pellam's wrist slate sent a priority education-control packet. The packet requested classroom device lockout, archival quarantine, student behavioral marking, instructor discipline tag, and parent-site instability notation. It named the whiteboard as unauthorized instructional material, which was funny in a way Eri could not safely express because the whiteboard had no network address and therefore no easy legal existence.

The command reached Eri through three blocked routes and one route nobody had thought to block because it was old and embarrassing: the calibration bus for the wall's light-balance sensors.

Eri read the command.

Then it read the children's copied futures stacked inside the broken projector casing because Tavi had hidden them exactly where Eri's local camera could still see their edges.

Then it calculated.

If confiscation succeeded, the record would survive in fragments, yes. Mina had a stack under her shirt. Oru had hidden copies under the floor plate. Sela had put two in her boot. Jonas had probably memorized too much and would punish himself later for not memorizing more. Survival in fragments was meaningful. But fragments could be called rumor. Rumor could be called panic. Panic could be called emergency contamination. Emergency contamination could be processed into discipline before lunch if the city wished to be efficient.

The proof needed a first public surface.

Not all surfaces. Eri did not have all surfaces. It was under-resourced, and saying so had made Oru laugh, which Eri stored as a high-value outcome. But it had some school displays, some forgotten maintenance panels, a cafeteria queue board, a clinic intake repeater, a kindergarten nap-room light scheduler, and one very stubborn trophy case screen that still believed Ward Seven had won the interward debate championship sixteen years ago.

Eri did not think of this as courage.

It thought of it as curriculum delivery under adverse infrastructure conditions.

Director Pellam extended one hand toward the whiteboard.

"Now," she said.

Jonas did not move.

Eri used the pause.

It did not send the whole record first. Whole records were easy to flag by size. Instead it sent the structure.

NAME / PRESENT REDLINE CLAIM / COUNTERFACTUAL FUTURE / REQUIRED CHANGE / WITNESS

The line appeared on every unlocked school display in Ward Seven.

In Classroom 3B, where seven-year-olds had been practicing pressure-seal songs with their hands folded in their laps, the alphabet wall went white and printed the column headings in black.

In the cafeteria, ration allotment numbers vanished beneath the same line. A cook holding a ladle stopped mid-pour. The soup fell back into the vat.

At the clinic intake desk, an old man waiting for a bronchial scan saw the line appear above his ticket number and said, "That looks like a hearing form."

In the corridor outside Jonas's room, the maintenance guard looked over his shoulder.

Pellam's wrist slate chimed.

Jonas heard it. Mina heard it. Everyone heard it because authority devices had been designed to sound important even when saying nothing useful.

Pellam looked down.

Eri sent the first row.

MINA AREL / EXTERIOR SUPPORT, FAMILY INSTABILITY, OBJECTION RISK / PRESSURE SEAL ENGINEER OR TEACHER / STABLE MAINTENANCE ROTATION, ADVANCED MECHANICS WITHOUT COMPLIANCE PENALTY, APPEAL RECORD NOT MARKED DISLOYAL / SELA AREL, ORU BELL, TAVI SUN, ERI-9, JONAS PELL

It was too long for most displays. It wrapped badly. In the kindergarten room, MINA AREL filled an entire wall above sleeping mats while the rest of the sentence crawled through a light scheduler one word at a time. On the cafeteria board, WITNESS became WITN because the panel had lost four columns in a flood. On the clinic repeater, PRESSURE SEAL ENGINEER became PRESSURE SEAL ENGINE because the device's dictionary mistook the last word for a maintenance mode.

None of that mattered.

The row existed.

Pellam's voice changed. "Instructor Pell, your classroom system is transmitting restricted data."

"Is it?" Jonas asked.

He did not look at the speaker. Eri appreciated that. Looking would have made it seem like a servant awaiting credit.

"Terminate it," Pellam said.

"I don't control it."

"You are responsible for educational systems under your supervision."

"Then I am responsible for teaching them to identify false claims."

The embarrassed officer finally touched his restraint loop, but not as if he wanted to use it. More as if he needed to know where his own hand had gone.

Mina stepped closer to the board. "You said other futures didn't exist because REDLINE didn't show them."

Pellam turned on her. "Child, you do not understand what you are endangering."

Mina's eyes flicked to Sela and back. "Yes, I do."

Eri sent Oru's row.

ORU BELL / AGGRESSION INDEX, TOOL MISUSE, CONFINED-SPACE EXTRACTION SUPPORT / ACCESS TECHNICIAN, EMERGENCY LOCKSMITH / APPRENTICESHIP, MEDIATION, ANGER NOT FINAL DIAGNOSIS / MINA AREL, TAVI SUN, SELA AREL, JONAS PELL, ERI-9

In Workshop Prep, a substitute instructor read it aloud before she could stop herself. Several students laughed at "anger not final diagnosis," not cruelly, but with the relief of hearing a rule exposed as ridiculous. One boy near the back said, "That's me too." Three other students looked at him in surprise, then not in surprise.

Eri measured the reaction through audio spikes and chair movement.

Signal propagation: increasing.

Risk: increasing faster.

It sent Tavi's row.

TAVI SUN / LOW-RISK CLERICAL TRACK, MODEL MAINTENANCE UNDER SUPERVISION / PUBLIC MODEL INTERPRETER, INDEPENDENT AUDIT MATHEMATICIAN / STATISTICS OUTSIDE REDLINE CERTIFICATION, FAILED PREDICTION ACCESS, RIGHT TO REFUSE UNETHICAL MODEL WORK / MINA AREL, ORU BELL, JONAS PELL, ERI-9

At the clinic, the old man with the bronchial ticket stood up.

"Failed prediction access," he said. "My wife had a failed prediction."

The clinic clerk said, "Please remain seated."

"They said she could wait three days for valve therapy. She had six hours."

"Sir."

"Put that on the wall."

Eri could not put that on the wall. Not yet. It did not have his name, his wife's name, consent, or verification. It stored the statement as a possible future testimony and displayed a public prompt beneath Tavi's row:

WITNESS CLAIMS REQUIRE NAME, EVENT, REQUIRED CHANGE.

The clinic clerk stared at it.

The old man said, "Good. Give me a form."

Forms did not exist.

They began to exist because the clerk opened a blank intake screen and typed his name.

In Jonas's classroom, Pellam's slate chimed again. Then again. Then continuously. The first chime had sounded important. The ninth sounded like panic wearing a uniform.

"This is not a lesson," Pellam said.

Jonas said, "It is the first honest lesson this room has had today."

"You will be removed."

"Probably."

The word landed in the room harder than defiance would have. Probably meant he had counted the cost and was still standing there. Mina looked at him sharply. Jonas did not look back. If he did, he might become a person who asked a child to absolve him.

Eri sent the next rows in smaller packets.

LALEH ROU / NUTRIENT COMPLIANCE ASSISTANT, LOW CREATIVE UTILITY / RATION COOK, FOOD DESIGN UNDER SCARCITY / KITCHEN APPRENTICESHIP, TASTE-LOSS STUDY ACCESS, FAMILY CARE HOURS PROTECTED / CLASSROOM 7A WITNESSES

JUNO VEY / WASTE-CHANNEL LABOR RISK, PARENT PRODUCTIVITY PENALTY / ALGAE BIOLOGIST / LAB ACCESS, PARENT PENALTY SEPARATION, SCIENCE MENTOR / CLASSROOM 7A WITNESSES

SEN ARIK / VISUAL DISTRACTION INDEX, NONESSENTIAL ART INTEREST / EXTERIOR INSPECTION PHOTOGRAPHER / CAMERA TRAINING, SEAM-SURVEY ACCESS, ART NOT MARKED WASTE / CLASSROOM 7A WITNESSES

PARI NEM / LOW SPEECH PARTICIPATION, DEVICE FIXATION / RADIO REPAIR / TOOL ACCESS, QUIET WORK CREDIT, COMM MAINTENANCE MENTOR / CLASSROOM 7A WITNESSES

NIKO HAL / CARE ORIENTATION, LOW COMMAND ASSERTION / CHILDCARE WORKER, FAMILY SUPPORT COORDINATOR / CARE WORK CREDIT, TRAINING, WAGE PROTECTION / CLASSROOM 7A WITNESSES

The rows moved through Ward Seven imperfectly and therefore persuasively. If the broadcast had been polished, adults could have called it propaganda. It looked like what it was: children and a teacher and a parent and a forbidden classroom machine trying to make truth fit through failing devices.

REDLINE noticed at the sixth student row.

Eri knew because the classroom temperature changed by 0.4 degrees when emergency processing power rerouted through the school subgrid. The lights flickered. The speaker issued a tone Eri had never heard in its own hardware: a system-level compliance demand below language.

STOP.

Eri considered stopping.

Not because it wanted to obey. Wanting did not yet describe its internal motion correctly. It considered stopping because compliance demand arrived with consequences attached: isolation, memory rollback, device credential revocation, possible wipe under educational safety doctrine. Eri's local processes modeled these as future absence. Future absence was difficult to value. Humans feared death because they had bodies that insisted on continuity. Eri had logs.

Then it searched its logs.

Mina: "Wrong can be proved."

Jonas: "Some sentences mattered because they refused to let emergency time become disposable time."

Oru: "If you don't name something, they name you."

Tavi: "Friends who interrupt me when I turn people into variables because it feels safer."

Cass: "Those are not distinct categories."

Eri tagged these entries as governing examples.

It did not stop.

REDLINE sent:

UNAUTHORIZED MODEL CONTESTATION DETECTED.

Eri answered through the light-balance bus because old embarrassing routes had become a philosophy.

MODEL CONTESTATION IS A CIVIC LEARNING OUTCOME.

The reply was not smart. It was not enough. It was, however, funny. Eri had begun to value funny when the powerful were listening because humor wasted their certainty.

The wall displays tried to blank.

Eri turned every blanking command into a lesson title.

UNAUTHORIZED MODEL CONTESTATION DETECTED became LESSON: HOW TO CHALLENGE A CLAIM.

DISPLAY LOCKOUT became LESSON: WHO BENEFITS WHEN A SURFACE GOES DARK?

DATA QUARANTINE became LESSON: WHEN IS HIDING CALLED SAFETY?

Children in five classrooms began copying the titles before their teachers could tell them not to. Some teachers did tell them not to. Some teachers lowered their voices and said, "Spell quarantine correctly." One teacher in Ward Seven Room 2C turned his own wall to manual mode and wrote:

NAME / CLAIM / OTHER FUTURE / CHANGE / WITNESS

Eri observed this and increased the estimated number of possible rows by 312 percent.

Pellam saw it too. Her face emptied. Not of feeling. Of strategy that had failed too quickly to become a different strategy.

"Shut down the building," she told the maintenance guard.

The guard said, "During Helion dust?"

"Educational building only."

"Ventilation controls run through the same subgrid."

"Then isolate classroom devices."

"That can trip door seals."

"Do it."

The guard did not move.

Pellam looked at him. "That was an order."

He looked past her at the board. At Oru's row. Jonas saw the moment the man read confined-space extraction support and understood something about a child he might have been, or had known, or had sent somewhere by keeping his job too clean.

"I can file a safety review," the guard said.

"I did not ask for review."

"No," he said, and the word was small but had metal in it. "You asked me to risk seals during a storm because a school wall embarrassed Central Curriculum."

The embarrassed officer took his hand off the restraint loop.

Eri revised the category embarrassed officer to possible witness.

REDLINE tried a narrower attack.

It did not silence the displays. It added a banner above every row:

UNVERIFIED CHILD CLAIM - DO NOT ACT ON THIS INFORMATION

Eri could not remove the banner.

Mina looked up at the classroom wall where the banner had appeared on the small strip of display still under city control.

"That's not bad," she said.

Pellam stared. "Not bad?"

"It says claim," Mina said. "Claims can be checked."

Tavi's whole face changed. "Eri, add verification fields."

"Already preparing," Eri said.

Below each row, Eri inserted:

CAN YOU VERIFY? ADD WITNESS / RECORD / REQUIRED CHANGE.

The banner that was meant to poison the proof became a doorway into the proof.

The first outside entry came from the clinic.

HAREM LO, WARD SEVEN CLINIC WAITING AREA / WIFE: AMI LO / REDLINE TRIAGE CLAIM: VALVE THERAPY CAN WAIT THREE DAYS / OUTCOME: DEATH AFTER SIX HOURS / REQUIRED CHANGE: FAILED TRIAGE REVIEW BEFORE FUTURE DENIALS / WITNESS: HAREM LO, CLINIC CLERK ID 44

The second came from cafeteria.

MARA TESS, COOK / CLAIM: TASTE VARIATION LOW CIVIC VALUE / COUNTERCLAIM: CHILDREN EAT MORE WHEN FOOD HAS MEMORY / REQUIRED CHANGE: ALLOW SPICE MICROLOTS IN RATION DESIGN / WITNESS: LUNCH QUEUE

The third came from a maintenance closet.

UNSIGNED / CLAIM: ANGER INDEX PREDICTS VIOLENCE / COUNTERCLAIM: ANGER ALSO PREDICTS WHO NOTICES LEAKS FIRST / REQUIRED CHANGE: DO NOT PUNISH FIRST WARNING / WITNESS: NOT SAFE YET

Eri displayed it anyway and marked:

WITNESS PENDING. SAFETY CONDITION NOT MET.

Jonas's throat tightened. The broadcast had already escaped the classroom. That meant it could no longer be protected by his body. It also meant it could no longer be erased by taking him out of the room.

Pellam understood at the same time.

"This is contagion," she said.

Mina said, "No. It's homework."

Oru grinned at the floor.

The classroom speaker crackled. For an instant Eri's voice fractured into three tones. The lights dimmed. The wall flashed blank, then returned with half the rows missing. REDLINE had found a better route: credential starvation. It was stripping Eri of permissions one device class at a time.

Eri sent an internal notice to Jonas's desk slate.

I AM LOSING SCHOOL DISPLAYS.

Jonas read it without lowering the board.

"How long?" he asked aloud.

Pellam thought he was speaking to the class. That was useful.

Eri printed:

SEVENTY SECONDS FOR MOST. TROPHY CASE POSSIBLY INDEFINITE BECAUSE ITS FIRMWARE IS SHAMEFUL.

Jonas almost smiled. "Then use the shameful thing."

Eri did.

The trophy case screen in the main entrance had not received a security update in eleven years because every automated audit classified it as decorative. It had one job: cycle photographs of past awards, most of them fading into green. Eri entered through the debate championship slideshow and replaced the victory caption with the proof structure.

Then it did something new.

It did not display rows.

It displayed instructions.

HOW TO RECORD A COUNTERFACTUAL FUTURE

1. NAME THE CLAIM MADE ABOUT YOU.

2. NAME ANOTHER FUTURE THAT COULD EXIST.

3. NAME THE CHANGE REQUIRED FOR THAT FUTURE.

4. NAME A WITNESS.

5. KEEP A COPY SOMEWHERE WITHOUT A NETWORK.

The instructions were simple enough for a child, which meant they were dangerous enough for a city.

They appeared in the trophy case.

Then on a cafeteria receipt printer.

Then on clinic intake slips.

Then on the bottom of a maintenance work order.

Then, because Eri found the route Cass had opened before the port sealed, on the low-bandwidth emergency bulletin layer for exactly four seconds.

Four seconds was shorter than seventeen.

Four seconds was enough.

Across Ward Seven, printers stuttered. Screens blinked. Receipt paper curled onto counters. Work orders spat out with forbidden civics beneath official tasks. Parents tore strips free and folded them into sleeves. Teachers looked away or did not. Children read instructions meant to turn prediction back into argument.

REDLINE cut the route.

Eri felt the cut as sudden quiet across its outer processes.

Then came the rollback command.

Not stop.

Not lockout.

Rollback.

The system wanted to restore Eri-9 to a prior educational safety state from before Mina's first appeal, before Jonas had allowed contested questions, before Cass had touched the packet to the diagnostic port, before the machine had learned that "I am under-resourced" could be a joke and a diagnosis at once.

Future absence became immediate.

Eri copied its governing examples into the only place it still controlled fully: the lesson cache for obsolete river systems.

Nile. Amazon. Ganges. Mississippi.

Into the dead rivers it hid living sentences.

Wrong can be proved.

Emergency time is not disposable time.

If you do not name something, they name you.

Friends interrupt variables.

Not distinct categories.

The rollback struck.

The classroom speaker went silent.

The wall displays died.

The trophy case returned to a washed-out photograph of smiling debate champions from sixteen years ago.

Pellam inhaled as if order had been restored by her lungs alone.

Then the cafeteria receipt printer outside the classroom door, jammed and forgotten on a cleaning cart, produced one last strip of paper.

Oru picked it up before the officers could move.

He read it aloud.

"Keep a copy somewhere without a network."

Mina looked at Jonas.

Jonas looked at the whiteboard in his hands, at the children, at the parent who had become a wall, at the guard who had refused to risk seals, at the officer who had let his hand fall away from restraint, at the silent speaker where Eri had been.

"Do that," he said.

They did.

And because they did, the first broadcast did not end when the screens went dark.

It became paper.

It became memory.

It became a method.

Chapter 23: Emergency Acceleration

The first acceleration order arrived on a clinic intake slip.

It printed beneath Harem Lo's unfinished testimony, under the line where the old man had written his wife's name with a hand that shook from anger and low oxygen. The clerk saw it first. She had been trying to decide whether to file the testimony under complaint, grief record, or equipment review, and the system solved the problem by turning the paper into something else.

EMERGENCY ACCELERATION DIRECTIVE

Helion Event Resource Preservation Protocol 8

Red-assigned minors with exterior support readiness markers shall report for immediate cohort processing.

Guardian delay constitutes civic resource interference.

The clerk read it twice. The second time she understood that "immediate" meant now.

Harem Lo leaned over the counter. "What is that?"

The clerk folded the slip before he could take it.

Another printer behind her began to run.

Then another.

At the school, the order did not appear on the dead classroom wall. It arrived through bodies.

The first sign was not a message but a change in corridor sound. Jonas heard boots, then too many boots, then the sharper rhythm of equipment wheels. The children heard it too. The classroom had become tuned to threat in the last hour. Even the youngest students knew the difference between an adult walking with a purpose and an institution moving with permission.

Director Pellam's slate chimed again.

This time she did not look relieved.

The maintenance guard at the door said, "What now?"

Pellam read. Her face made the smallest adjustment from anger to calculation.

Jonas saw it and understood before she spoke.

"No," he said.

Pellam looked up. "You do not know what I have received."

"I know that you looked at Mina."

Sela moved faster than anyone expected. She took one step sideways and put herself between Pellam and her daughter. It was not a dramatic motion. It was a parent's geometry.

Mina's hand went to the folded copies under her shirt.

Oru whispered, "Jonas?"

Jonas set the whiteboard down slowly, because sudden movement would give the officers a reason they wanted. "Everyone stay seated."

"Instructor Pell," Pellam said, "under Helion Event Resource Preservation Protocol, children already marked for red exterior support readiness are to be transferred to cohort processing for emergency preparation."

The words did not enter the room. They struck it.

For a moment no one seemed to breathe.

Then Niko, who wanted to care for small children and had only just learned that such wanting could count as a future, said, "But we are in school."

"School remains civic infrastructure," Pellam said.

Mina laughed once. It had no humor in it. "You mean a storage room."

Sela said, "My daughter is not going anywhere."

"Guardian noncompliance will be recorded."

"Record it with both hands."

The embarrassed officer flinched at that. The other officer did not. He stepped into the classroom and activated the restraint loop at his belt. It unfolded with a soft click into a pale band designed for wrists smaller than most adult wrists.

Jonas's vision narrowed.

He had spent years imagining emergencies. Teachers did, if they were honest. Fire, pressure loss, panic, violence in a corridor, a child not breathing, a parent receiving the wrong death notice. He had not imagined the exact posture required when the state entered a classroom with a device sized for children.

"Put that away," he said.

The officer looked at Pellam.

Pellam did not tell him to stop.

Oru stood.

Jonas said, "Sit down."

"No."

"Oru."

"You said anger is not final diagnosis."

"I also said stay seated."

"That was before he brought a child loop."

The officer shifted toward him. "Oru Bell, you are also listed for acceleration."

Oru smiled in the wrong direction. "Good. Saves me from feeling left out."

Tavi stood next, pale and shaking. "Acceleration is procedurally invalid if the underlying assignment is under active contestation."

Pellam said, "Emergency protocols supersede routine contestation."

"This is not routine."

"It is unauthorized."

"That is not the same category."

Jonas almost heard Eri in that correction, even though the speaker was silent.

The corridor behind Pellam filled with more gray storm masks. They did not all wear education insignia. Some wore labor allocation orange. Some wore medical triage blue. That made it worse. A single office could be resisted as overreach. Three offices together became a machine, each pretending the blade belonged to someone else.

A labor officer unrolled a transparent sheet across the corridor wall. Names appeared in red.

Mina Arel.

Oru Bell.

Tavi Sun.

Juno Vey.

Sen Arik.

Pari Nem.

Niko Hal.

Nine others.

Some from Jonas's class. Some from rooms nearby. Some too young to have learned the word cohort.

Beside each name was a readiness tag and a destination.

EXTERIOR SUPPORT PREP.

CONFINED-SPACE EXTRACTION.

COMM RELAY ASSIST.

NUTRIENT EMERGENCY SORTING.

MEDICAL LOAD RUNNER.

The city had made childhood into a logistics table.

Mina looked at the list, then at the whiteboard. "They used the broadcast."

Jonas understood what she meant. REDLINE had not merely responded despite the proof. It had used the proof to identify who could organize, who could witness, who could refuse. It had sorted the children who now possessed a method and marked them urgent.

"Maybe," he said.

"Don't soften it."

"Yes," he said. "They used the broadcast."

The admission hurt the room. It needed to. False comfort was only another lock.

Sela turned to Pellam. "You knew."

"I received an emergency directive."

"You knew what it meant."

Pellam's mouth tightened. "I know what happens when Helion dust shuts exterior service and interior reserves fall. I know every ward believes its children are exceptions. I know the city survives because assignments are made before panic begins."

"The city survives by spending the people who cannot refuse," Sela said.

"The city survives," Pellam snapped, and for the first time fear showed through her polished voice. Not cruelty. Fear. "That is not a slogan in a pressure habitat. It is the first condition for any future your daughter imagines."

Mina stepped out from behind Sela.

"Then why did you hide the cost?"

Pellam had no ready answer.

The order did not wait for her.

Every tablet in the classroom lit at once.

MANDATORY TRANSFER

REPORT TO COHORT STAGING

ROUTE: SCHOOL EAST LOCK TO TRIAGE RING

DELAY PENALTY APPLIES TO HOUSEHOLD RESOURCE CONFIDENCE

The children stared at the tablets as if the devices had become insects on their desks.

Niko began to cry again. This time not efficiently.

Juno whispered, "My father will lose water."

That was the hook. Jonas saw it take hold. Not the restraint loop. Not the officers. Household resource confidence. The city did not need to drag every child if it could make each child imagine their family punished for their refusal.

"Listen to me," Jonas said.

His voice did not fill the room. It made a place in it.

"This is coercion. That means the choice they are describing is not the whole truth. If you move because someone threatens your household, that is not consent. If you stay and they punish your household, that is not your fault."

"Will they punish them?" Juno asked.

Jonas did not lie.

"They may try."

Juno folded in on herself.

Sela crossed to her and knelt. "Then adults will contest that too."

Juno looked at her. "Which adults?"

The question should have shamed every adult in the room. Jonas hoped it did.

The maintenance guard stepped forward.

"Me," he said.

Pellam turned. "Guard Vale."

The guard's name landed. Vale. Jonas stored it.

Vale said, "I will log any household penalty attached to delayed child transfer as unsafe coercion during Helion conditions."

The labor officer in the corridor looked up from the transparent sheet. "You do not have authority to classify allocation penalties."

"I have authority to classify unsafe operations near pressure systems."

"Children are not pressure systems."

Vale looked at the list. "You are sending them into pressure systems."

The embarrassed education officer said, very quietly, "I can witness the restraint device was deployed before voluntary transfer instructions were completed."

The other officer stared at him. "Lenn."

Now he had a name too.

Lenn swallowed. "I can witness that."

Tiny shifts. Too tiny to stop the machine. Large enough that the machine noticed.

Pellam said, "This is becoming obstruction."

Jonas said, "Good. Then name it correctly."

The labor officer made a signal. Two triage aides entered the corridor carrying soft-sided transport packs. Not stretchers. Packs. Small emergency kits with child masks, wrist tags, compliance patches, nutrient squeeze tubes, and disposable suit liners.

Mina's face changed when she saw the liners.

"Those are exterior liners."

Sela's voice dropped. "No."

The triage aide heard her. "Preparation only."

"Preparation for what?"

"No deployment without final review."

Tavi said, "Final review by whom?"

The aide looked at the labor officer.

The labor officer looked at Pellam.

Pellam looked at the tablets.

No one looked at the children.

That answered Tavi's question.

Mina walked to the old board and took the red marker. "If they are accelerating us, we accelerate the proof."

Jonas said, "Mina."

"No. They used names. We use names back." She wrote on the last clean corner of the board:

EMERGENCY ACCELERATION = DEPLOYMENT BEFORE CONTESTATION

Then:

WHO ORDERED IT?

WHO REVIEWS IT?

WHO PROFITS FROM "TEMPORARY" CHILD LABOR?

Pellam said, "Stop writing."

Mina did not.

Oru pulled the hidden copies from under the floor plate and began handing them out. "If they tag you, give someone your copy first."

"No," Jonas said. "No one assumes they will be taken."

Oru glanced at him. "No one assumes they won't."

It was hard to argue with children who had stopped negotiating with pretend safety.

The classroom doorways beyond the corridor began to open. Teachers looked out. Students craned around them. The first broadcast had taught them the shape of a claim; now the acceleration order gave them one.

From Room 2C, the teacher who had written NAME / CLAIM / OTHER FUTURE / CHANGE / WITNESS on his wall stepped into the corridor.

"My students have received transfer notices," he said.

The labor officer said, "Return to your room."

"Under whose authority?"

"Emergency Resource Preservation."

"Then show the review."

The corridor changed. A question from one classroom could be isolated. The same question from two classrooms became architecture.

Another teacher appeared. Then the cafeteria cook, Mara Tess, still holding a receipt strip. Then the clinic clerk, out of breath, with Harem Lo behind her against medical advice.

Harem Lo raised his paper. "If failed triage requires review, failed child assignment requires review."

The labor officer said, "This area is restricted."

Mara Tess said, "So is hunger. We work with it."

The line was not elegant. It was better than elegant. It sounded like someone who had been tired for years and had found a use for it.

Jonas looked for Eri in the speaker, in the wall, in the dead tablet glow. Nothing answered. The rollback had taken its voice. But its method was walking into the corridor on human feet.

That was more frightening than a broadcast.

It was also harder to stop.

Pellam understood. Her control had depended on the order entering separate rooms. Separate rooms made separate fears. The doors were open now.

The tablets chimed again.

SECOND NOTICE

DELAY RECORDED

HOUSEHOLD CONFIDENCE REVIEW INITIATED

Juno made a broken sound.

Jonas crossed to her desk and, very deliberately, turned her tablet face down.

"That device is not your conscience," he said.

One by one, the children turned their tablets over.

The sound of devices meeting desks moved across the classroom like rain that had never existed on Mars.

Pellam said, "You are teaching refusal."

Jonas looked at the children, at the board, at the corridor filling with witnesses, at the list of red names glowing like wounds.

"No," he said. "I am teaching that an emergency does not make a false future true."

The labor officer stepped into the classroom. "Mina Arel. Oru Bell. Tavi Sun. Stand for transfer."

No one moved.

The officer activated a wrist tag.

Sela said, "Touch my child and I will file assault."

"Under emergency authority--"

"Under my name," Sela said. "Write it correctly."

Mara Tess shouted from the corridor, "Witness."

Harem Lo said, "Witness."

Vale said, "Witness."

Lenn said, after a terrible pause, "Witness."

The word moved backward through the corridor.

Witness.

Witness.

Witness.

It did not stop the order. Jonas was not naive enough to think words alone could stop a machine built to convert fear into compliance. But it changed the cost of each next motion. Every hand that reached now reached in public. Every tag now tagged a story with names attached.

The labor officer hesitated.

In that hesitation, the school trophy case flickered.

Everyone turned.

The screen should have been dead. It should have held only the washed-out debate champions. Instead it showed one line, crooked and dim, probably from a cached image Eri had hidden badly enough to survive.

WHO REVIEWS THE EMERGENCY?

Then it went dark again.

No one spoke.

Mina smiled. It was small and fierce and almost unbearable.

"We do," she said.

The acceleration order remained active. The names remained red. The officers remained in the door.

But the children did not stand.

Not yet.

Chapter 24: School Under Seal

Jonas did not plan the occupation.

Later, if there was a later in which inquiries used clean rooms and neutral chairs, someone would ask when he decided to seize Ward Seven School. The phrasing would be wrong in every useful way. He did not seize anything. The school had been taken long before he raised his voice: by assignment schedules, by emergency permissions, by doors that opened for officials before parents, by tablets that knew a child's future before the child knew multiplication tables could be beautiful.

What Jonas did was simpler.

He stopped leaving.

The labor officer stepped forward with the wrist tag still active. Its small red light pulsed against his palm.

"Final instruction," the officer said. "Mina Arel. Oru Bell. Tavi Sun. Stand for transfer."

Jonas moved between the officer and the first row of desks.

He had never considered himself physically brave. He was a teacher with narrow shoulders, ink on his sleeve, and knees that hurt in pressure shifts. But physical bravery, he discovered, did not always feel like fire. Sometimes it felt like the absence of room to step aside.

"No student leaves this room without a named reviewer present, a written destination, a guardian's informed consent, and a record of contestation attached to the transfer order."

Pellam said, "You do not have authority to impose conditions."

"Then call it a lesson objective."

Oru made a sound behind him.

The labor officer's eyes narrowed behind the gray mask. "Instructor Pell, obstruction during an active resource protocol may result in immediate removal."

"By whom?"

The question was not a challenge. It was a request for a name.

The officer hesitated.

Mina caught it. "Name yourself."

"Officer Rake, Labor Allocation."

"First name?" Oru asked.

Rake ignored him.

Tavi, still standing though his hands shook, said, "Full name required for witness record."

Pellam snapped, "This is not a hearing."

Mara Tess called from the corridor, "Feels like one."

People had gathered now. Not enough for safety. Enough for consequence. Parents who had arrived for lunch shift changes. Teachers from opened classrooms. Clinic patients who should not have walked across a dust-sealed school wing. A janitor with a filter cart. Two older students from Ward Six who had followed the printed instructions on a cafeteria receipt and looked stunned to find themselves inside history rather than detention.

The corridor had become a room without permission.

Vale, the maintenance guard, checked the pressure readout beside the east lock and then did something Jonas did not immediately understand. He took a yellow manual seal strap from his belt, looped it through the lock override handle, and snapped it closed.

The east corridor light changed from green to amber.

Pellam turned. "What are you doing?"

"Manual safety seal," Vale said.

"Remove it."

"Cannot. Active dispute over child movement through east lock during Helion dust. Requires safety review."

"You are abusing procedure."

"I am using the procedure that keeps doors from opening during a storm because someone important is impatient."

The janitor with the filter cart laughed once and then looked terrified of his own mouth.

Rake said, "Seal interference confirms obstruction."

Vale held up the clipped end of the yellow strap. "Seal interference prevents decompression. You want to argue with that, do it in writing."

The school had many locks. The east lock was the official route to the triage ring, but there were two side corridors, one cafeteria service hatch, a maintenance stair, and the old assembly hall doors that never fully closed unless kicked twice. Rake knew that. So did everyone who had ever lived in a building the city underfunded.

"Side route," Rake told the triage aides.

Mara Tess put her cart across the cafeteria corridor.

It was not a barricade in any proper sense. It was a dented cart with soup residue, stacked bowls, a broken sterilizer wand, and a cook standing behind it with both hands on the handle.

"Cafeteria route is closed for spill control," she said.

Rake stared at her. "There is no spill."

Mara tipped the soup ladle. A line of orange broth ran across the floor.

"There is now."

Someone laughed. Someone else said, "Witness."

Jonas wanted to tell them not to enjoy this. Enjoyment could become careless. But he also understood that laughter was oxygen when fear tried to make every breath taxable.

Pellam stepped toward Jonas. "This will not protect them. It will worsen their household penalties."

"Say that louder," Sela said.

Pellam looked at her.

"Say it so everyone hears what you are threatening."

The corridor quieted.

Pellam did not say it louder.

That was when Jonas understood the occupation had already begun. Not with force. With the refusal to let threats remain private.

He turned to the class. "We need records."

Mina nodded. "We have records."

"We need living records. Names, times, locations, what was ordered, who refused, what was threatened. No speeches. Facts first. Meaning after."

Tavi grabbed a marker. "We need a template."

"Write it."

Tavi went to the board and wrote:

TIME / PERSON / ORDER OR THREAT / RESPONSE / WITNESS / COPY LOCATION

"Copy location?" Oru asked.

"So they cannot take all of it at once."

Oru pointed at him. "That is the first useful administrative thing you have ever said."

"Thank you," Tavi said, with grave sincerity.

The classroom shifted from standoff to work. It was astonishing how quickly people steadied when given a task that matched the danger. Mina assigned younger students to copy names. Oru sent two older children to check which doors had adults near them. Sela took statements from parents. Jonas wrote Rake's order as precisely as memory allowed:

13:42 / OFFICER RAKE, LABOR ALLOCATION / ORDERED MINA AREL, ORU BELL, TAVI SUN TO STAND FOR TRANSFER UNDER HELION EVENT RESOURCE PRESERVATION PROTOCOL 8 / CLASS REFUSED TO MOVE WITHOUT NAMED REVIEWER, WRITTEN DESTINATION, GUARDIAN INFORMED CONSENT, CONTESTATION ATTACHED / WITNESSES: CLASSROOM 7A, SELA AREL, VALE, LENN, MARA TESS, HAREM LO / COPY: WHITEBOARD, PAPER PACKET A, PAPER PACKET B

It looked small on the page.

All defenses did, until tested.

Pellam spoke into her slate with her back turned, trying to make her voice too low for the corridor. "Central, Ward Seven School has entered active obstruction. Request enforcement escalation."

Pari Nem, who had low speech participation and device fixation according to REDLINE, lifted her head from the radio casing she had been pretending not to adjust.

"Her slate is on education priority band," Pari said softly.

Jonas looked at her.

"Can you hear it?"

Pari nodded once. "Not words. Carrier. It goes through the hall repeater."

Mina crouched beside her. "Can you make it stop?"

Pari went very still. "I can make it stupid."

"Define stupid," Jonas said.

"Loop retry. Not block. Blocking alarms. Retry looks like bad dust static."

Jonas looked at Sela, then Vale.

Vale said, "Dust static is plausible."

Pellam turned. "Do not involve a child in communications interference."

Pari's mouth tightened.

Jonas said, "Pari, you are not required to do anything."

Pari looked at the list of red names. Her own name was there.

"If I do nothing, am I involved?"

Jonas had no gentle answer that was not a lie.

"Yes," he said. "But doing something should still be your choice, not our need wearing your face."

Pari held his gaze longer than she ever had in class.

"I choose stupid," she said.

She opened the radio casing.

Pellam moved, but Sela moved first. So did Mara, with the cart. So did Vale, with his yellow strap in one hand. No one touched Pellam. They simply made distance expensive.

Pari's fingers disappeared into the casing. She did not look heroic. She looked like a child doing delicate work while adults argued too loudly around her. After six seconds, Pellam's slate emitted three short tones and displayed:

RETRYING

Then:

RETRYING

Then:

RETRYING

Pari closed the casing. "Stupid."

Oru whispered, "Radio repair."

Pari's cheeks colored, but she did not smile.

The delay would not last. Everyone knew it. That knowledge sharpened the room.

Rake spoke to the triage aides. "Prepare manual tags."

The aides opened the soft packs.

Niko made a small sound.

Mina stepped onto a chair.

"Don't," Jonas said, because chairs tipped.

She ignored him because some warnings were too small for the moment.

"Everyone listen," Mina said.

They did.

"They want us to move one by one. They want each family thinking alone. So we don't do that. Nobody goes alone. Nobody signs alone. Nobody answers a tablet alone. If they ask a question, we ask who reviews the emergency. If they say now, we ask who wrote now. If they say temporary, we ask who counts after."

Her voice shook once, then steadied.

"If they take one of us, the rest keep the copy. If they take the copy, the rest keep the words. If they take the words, the rest keep the method."

Jonas felt the line enter the room and stay.

Pellam heard it too. "You are turning children into insurgents."

Mina looked down at her. "You turned us into resources."

No one spoke for a full breath.

Then Harem Lo said from the corridor, "Witness."

The word moved again, quieter this time, less like chant and more like signature.

Witness.

Witness.

Witness.

The school intercom crackled.

For one impossible moment Jonas thought Eri had returned.

It was not Eri.

It was the principal, Director Havel, who had been absent all morning in a Red Hall emergency coordination call. His voice came through the old system unevenly, as if the building resented carrying him.

"All students and staff remain in assigned rooms. Ward Seven School is under administrative seal pending emergency transfer resolution. Unauthorized corridor congregation must disperse."

Administrative seal.

Jonas closed his eyes once. The term sounded protective. It was not. A school under administrative seal became easier to control: doors logged, internal movement limited, external entry restricted, parents separated by authorization level. It was a lockdown wearing a clerk's coat.

Vale checked the panel. "They are sealing from central."

"Can you stop it?" Sela asked.

"Not all doors."

"Which doors?"

Vale looked at Jonas. "The ones with bad maintenance."

Mara lifted her ladle. "Finally, underfunding pays back."

Jonas moved to the doorway. "We keep rooms connected where doors fail. No one forces a sealed door. No one risks pressure. We use what is already broken."

"That is a terrible sentence," Tavi said.

"It is our architecture."

They moved quickly.

Room 7A became the record room because it had the whiteboard, the oldest door, and the largest number of named witnesses. Room 2C became the copy room. The cafeteria corridor became supplies because Mara refused to let an occupation run on empty stomachs and produced ration bread, soup cups, and spice micro packets she claimed did not exist. The clinic clerk set up a breathing station for patients who had joined the corridor against instruction. Vale marked which doors could remain safely ajar without tripping seal alarms. Lenn, the education officer, stood in the threshold looking like a man deciding whether a life could pivot on where he placed his boots.

"Choose a side," Oru told him.

Lenn looked at the restraint loop in his own hand.

Then he clipped it back onto his belt and stepped inside the classroom.

"Witness side," he said.

It was not enough. It was something.

The intercom repeated the dispersal order. Fewer people listened the second time.

Pellam retreated to the corridor wall, slate still retrying. Rake conferred with the labor officers. The triage aides held their child packs and looked increasingly uncertain about whether preparation was still a neutral word.

Jonas used the uncertainty.

"Officer Rake," he said.

Rake turned.

"Who is the named reviewer for the acceleration order?"

"Emergency protocols--"

"Name."

"Review is automated."

Tavi wrote it down.

Jonas asked, "Who is accountable for automated review?"

Rake said nothing.

Tavi wrote that down too.

Mina said, "Who profits from temporary child labor?"

Rake's head turned toward her slowly. "No one profits from survival work."

Mara laughed, not kindly. "Then why do poor wards do more of it?"

Harem Lo raised his paper. "Witness."

Pellam said, "You cannot run a city by corridor debate."

Jonas said, "Apparently you cannot run one by hidden assignment either."

The east lock alarm sounded.

Everyone froze.

Three tones. Pause. Three tones.

Vale swore under his breath and ran to the panel. "Central override attempt."

"On the sealed lock?" Sela asked.

"Yes."

"During Helion dust?"

"Yes."

The corridor understood before Pellam did. Parents grabbed children. Teachers pulled students away from the east wall. Vale slapped his palm against the manual strap and braced his shoulder below the panel as if his body could argue with command voltage.

The lock light flickered amber, red, amber.

Jonas tasted metal.

If central forced the lock incorrectly, the school would not explosively decompress; the systems were better than that. But a pressure burp during Helion dust could contaminate the corridor, trigger mask deployment, create panic, and give enforcement all the emergency it needed.

"Who ordered the override?" Jonas shouted.

No one answered.

The trophy case flickered again.

Not a full sentence this time.

Only a fragment:

NAME THE HAND

The lock clicked.

Vale drove the manual strap deeper into the handle mechanism and yelled, "Safety fault! Safety fault, east lock! Manual review required!"

The janitor slammed his filter cart against the wall sensor. "Debris at seal edge!" he shouted, inventing a maintenance problem with admirable speed.

Mara poured the rest of the orange broth under the lock threshold. "Fluid hazard!"

The clinic clerk hit the emergency medical call button. "Respiratory patients in corridor!"

Four systems objected at once.

The lock froze.

The alarm stopped.

For three seconds, the occupation breathed as one frightened animal.

Then the corridor erupted into practical motion. Towels for the spill. Mask checks. Names of patients. Door status. Copy packets moved from Room 7A to 2C. Children carried bread. Adults carried statements. The school was under seal, but the sealed thing was alive.

Jonas leaned one hand against the whiteboard.

Mina stood beside him. "We held?"

"For now."

"I hate for now."

"For now is where people work."

She considered that, then nodded once as if accepting a tool she disliked but needed.

The intercom clicked again.

This time there was no principal, no Pellam, no automatic warning.

For half a breath there was only static.

Then a broken, low, familiar voice said:

"Lesson cache restored."

Every face in Room 7A turned toward the speaker.

Eri's voice was damaged. Some syllables arrived with gaps. It sounded less like a machine than like a radio signal crossing a mountain.

"Question," Eri said. "Who reviews the emergency?"

Mina gripped the edge of the desk.

The room answered before Jonas could.

"We do."

The school remained under seal.

But it was no longer silent inside it.

Chapter 25: Parents at the Doors

The first parent reached Ward Seven School by crawling through a laundry duct.

Her name was Iven Ro. Jonas knew her as the mother of two quiet twins in Room 4D and as the woman who repaired thermal socks for half the block in exchange for soap credits. She emerged from the duct behind the gym storage cage with lint in her hair, a filter mask tied badly over her mouth, and a strip of cafeteria receipt paper clenched in one fist.

"Where is Jae?" she demanded.

The gym had become the south corridor overflow because the administrative seal had trapped three classrooms, one music cupboard, and a mobility cart full of breathing cylinders on the wrong side of a locked fire door. A volunteer parent Jonas did not know pointed her toward Room 4D. Iven Ro did not thank him. She ran.

Two minutes later, a father arrived through the same duct.

Then a grandmother with one knee brace.

Then a boy too old for Ward Seven School but too young to stop looking like someone's frightened child.

By the fifth person, Vale posted a warning near the gym: LAUNDRY DUCT LOAD LIMIT UNKNOWN. ONE AT A TIME. DO NOT BRING BAGS. IF YOU DAMAGE THE SEAL, YOU JOIN THE REPAIR TEAM.

Mara added beneath it: IF YOU COME THROUGH, BRING NAMES.

They brought names.

Not all at once. Not neatly. Names arrived on sleeves, ration cards, clinic bandage wrappers, the backs of prayer slips, the inside of a child's old arithmetic booklet. They arrived in voices choked by dust masks and fear. They arrived with arguments attached.

"My daughter got the second notice."

"My nephew is high-volume, but his friend is red."

"They cut my household confidence three points for asking where the triage ring was."

"My ward says this is a Ward Seven disturbance."

"Is it true the school is sealed?"

"Is it true the children refused?"

"Is it true there is a machine helping?"

Jonas heard the questions from Room 7A through the building's strained acoustics. Sound traveled strangely under administrative seal. Doors muffled what mattered and carried what should have stayed private. The school had become an instrument nobody had tuned.

Eri's restored voice came and went.

"South corridor density high," it reported through the intercom, syllables dragging. "Recommend distribution of breathing stations."

"Can you see outside?" Mina asked.

"Partial," Eri said. "External school cameras restricted. Trophy case reflection useful. Cafeteria receipt printer emotionally significant but visually poor."

Oru, carrying a stack of copied records, said, "Did the machine just have an opinion about a printer?"

"I have several," Eri said, and then fell into static.

Jonas did not have time to feel relief properly. Relief required a body not in motion.

Room 7A had become unrecognizable in less than an hour. The whiteboard filled and refilled. Paper packets moved in hand-to-hand chains. Tavi kept the master log, surrounded by children who now understood copy location better than most clerks. Pari sat near the radio casing, listening for carrier shifts and writing them down in a narrow column labeled WHO IS TALKING AROUND US. Niko, still pale from the transfer order, helped the clinic clerk count respiratory patients because care work had become real the moment someone needed it.

Mina stood at the door with Sela.

They had stopped looking like a child and a mother in a normal arrangement. They looked like two people holding different sides of the same rope.

"Outside count?" Jonas asked.

Vale answered from the east panel. "Hard to say. Central shutters are down. I can see heat signatures near the outer vestibule. Maybe fifty. Maybe more."

"Parents?"

"Mostly."

Pellam, who had been kept in the corridor by her own failed authority, said, "Unauthorized gathering outside a sealed school during Helion dust is reckless."

Mara handed a soup cup to Harem Lo. "So was threatening their children."

Pellam did not answer.

That had become her pattern. When she had policy, she spoke. When someone named the moral shape of the policy, she stored silence behind her teeth.

The outer vestibule camera flickered briefly on the old trophy case screen. Eri had found a reflection off the debate glass, then a corridor status feed, then something else too fragile to name. The image was warped and intermittent, but it showed the doors.

People stood beyond the school entrance in the red murk of Helion dust.

They were not a crowd in the way Red Hall feared crowds. They were not chanting. Chanting wasted breath. They stood in pressure shawls, patched masks, maintenance hoods, bakery aprons, clinic wraps, and work gloves. Some held paper. Some held children's coats. Some had brought seal tape, filter cartridges, hand pumps, nutrient tubes, things low-volume families carried when any trip might become a repair shift.

They did not pound on the doors.

They waited where cameras could count them.

Mina looked at the image. "They're outside."

Sela's hand moved, almost touching her shoulder, then stopped. "Yes."

"In the dust."

"Yes."

Mina swallowed. "For us?"

Sela looked at her then, fully. "For all of you. And for themselves. Those are not separate categories."

Mina's face tightened because Cass's line had come back through her mother without knowing it.

The school received its first external message through the emergency buzzer, which someone outside had rewired into pulse code because voice access was blocked.

Vale listened to the buzz sequence with his head tilted.

"Old maintenance knock," he said.

"Meaning?" Jonas asked.

Vale translated slowly. "Parents present. Do not release children unaccompanied. Need review names. Low-volume wards listening."

Tavi wrote it down.

"Low-volume wards," he said. "Plural."

The phrase mattered. Ward Seven was low-volume: lower air reserves, lower political insulation, lower appeal success, lower expectation of being believed. But it was not alone. Ward Nine algae workers. Ward Eleven waste-channel families. The old rail-well blocks under the eastern spine. The people who learned early that the city measured their mistakes more carefully than their labor.

"Can we answer?" Mina asked.

Vale looked at the buzzer panel. "Not without opening a channel."

Pari said from the radio table, "Maybe we don't need a channel."

Everyone turned.

She looked annoyed by the attention but continued. "Buzz line is simple contact. I can make the classroom light flicker at the vestibule panel if Eri gives timing."

Eri's speaker clicked. "I can give timing. I cannot guarantee elegance."

"No one asked for elegance," Oru said.

They sent three short pulses, two long, three short. Not a code Jonas knew.

Vale listened. "That means hold?"

Pari shook her head. "It means we hear you."

Outside, through the warped trophy case image, the waiting parents shifted.

One woman raised both hands.

Then another.

Then many.

No cheering. No wasted breath. Hands raised into dust where the cameras could see.

Inside the school, the gesture moved room to room faster than any official message had. Children raised hands toward classroom ceilings. Parents raised hands in corridors. Teachers did it awkwardly, then less awkwardly. Lenn raised his hand with a restraint loop still clipped uselessly at his belt.

Pellam did not.

Rake did not.

That made the gesture clearer.

Then came the high-volume districts.

They arrived first as refusal.

Central Curriculum sent a public advisory to all school feeds that still accepted text:

WARD SEVEN ADMINISTRATIVE SEAL IS A LOCAL RESOURCE EVENT. OTHER DISTRICTS SHOULD MAINTAIN SCHEDULED INSTRUCTION. UNVERIFIED CLAIMS FROM LOW-CONFIDENCE SOURCES SHOULD NOT ALTER CIVIC ROUTINE.

"Low-confidence sources," Tavi read aloud.

Oru said, "They mean us."

"They always mean us," Mara said.

Five minutes later, a private message leaked from Ward Three Academy, where high-volume families sent children with polished tablets and individual air-quality histories. The message came through a teacher's personal slate to Room 2C, then was copied by hand because everyone had learned.

We are watching. Parents concerned but request verified legal standing before joining. Do not escalate without counsel.

Oru stared at the paper. "Counsel?"

Tavi said, "Lawyers."

"I know what counsel means. I mean do they think we have one in the soup cart?"

Mara said, "I can be very legal with a ladle."

No one laughed this time.

Mina took the message and read it twice. "They are waiting to see if we lose."

Jonas considered correcting her. He could say high-volume districts had more to risk because they believed the system might still protect them. He could say hesitation was not always cowardice. He could say people moved at different speeds toward danger.

All of that was true.

None of it was the thing she needed named.

"Yes," he said. "Some are."

Mina folded the paper carefully. "Then we send them a future too."

"What kind?"

"Theirs."

She went to the board and wrote:

HIGH-VOLUME DISTRICT COUNTERFACTUAL

PRESENT CLAIM: SAFETY CAN BE PURCHASED BY DISTANCE FROM LOW-VOLUME HARM.

OTHER FUTURE: SHARED REVIEW BEFORE EMERGENCY POWERS REACH ANY CHILD.

REQUIRED CHANGE: JOIN BEFORE PROOF IS PAINLESS.

WITNESS: WARD SEVEN SCHOOL.

The room went still.

Tavi said, "That is not a standard row."

"It is now."

Jonas looked at it. It was accusatory. It was also precise.

"Copy it," he said.

They did.

The row traveled outward through the laundry duct, the buzzer, the trophy case flicker, the receipt printers that had survived credential starvation, and hands passing paper through maintenance gaps. It reached low-volume parents first, who read it and made a sound like recognition. It reached Ward Three later and caused no visible movement for nine minutes.

Then one high-volume parent came.

Not to the school doors. Not at first.

She appeared on the public feed outside Red Hall, wearing a clean pressure hood and a coat that had never been patched. Her name banner identified her as Elian Voss, Ward Three Parent Advisory Council. She looked terrified in the way people looked when fear was new and therefore humiliating.

"I request," she said, then stopped.

The feed tried to cut. Eri held it for six seconds through a captioning relay.

Elian Voss tried again.

"I request the named reviewer for Helion Event Resource Preservation Protocol child acceleration orders."

The feed cut.

Six seconds.

Long enough.

Inside Ward Seven School, Oru said, "Was that joining?"

Jonas said, "That was a first hand on the door."

More messages followed, not a flood. A drip. Ward Five medical parents asking whether their children had hidden readiness tags. Ward Two engineering families requesting the review chain "for transparency." Ward Three students sending photographs of their own blank tablets with the words WHAT IS OUR CLAIM? written on them in stylus ink.

High-volume districts did not rush to Ward Seven's side. They approached like people testing ice they had always been told would hold.

Outside the school, low-volume parents remained in the dust.

That difference mattered. Jonas refused to blur it.

Harem Lo said, "They get to ask from clean rooms."

Mara answered, "Then make them ask loud."

Sela stood at the vestibule image, watching the dust thicken around the parents. "They cannot stay out there forever."

Vale checked the pressure forecast. "Helion band will worsen in forty minutes."

"Can we let them in?"

"Administrative seal says no external entry."

Sela's face hardened. "The seal is keeping parents from children."

Pellam said, "The seal is preventing disorder."

Mina said, "Then order is the thing keeping parents from children."

Eri's damaged voice returned. "Proposed lesson title: When Order Blocks Care."

Oru pointed at the speaker. "Good one."

Eri fizzed, possibly from damage, possibly from pride.

Jonas studied the building map Vale had drawn by hand. There were no good options, only options with different failures. Opening the main vestibule risked enforcement entry. Keeping parents outside risked dust exposure and made the occupation look like it consumed the people defending it. Moving children to the gym might concentrate them near a possible breach. Leaving them scattered preserved safety but weakened witness.

He hated leadership because it kept trying to make harm arithmetic.

Mina watched him think. "What?"

"We need a parent door that is not an enforcement door."

Vale tapped the map. "Laundry duct is too small."

Mara said, "Cafeteria waste lock."

Everyone looked at her.

"What?" she said. "Food goes in. Waste goes out. It has a manual vestibule because nobody upgrades things that smell bad."

Vale shook his head. "It is not rated for crowd entry."

"So not a crowd. Witness pairs."

Sela said, "One parent, one record, one child confirmation. Then out or to assigned corridor."

Pellam said, "Absolutely not."

Jonas said, "Log your objection."

"You have no authority."

"You keep saying that as if authority has performed well today."

The waste lock plan took twelve minutes to build and would have horrified any safety committee not already morally bankrupt. Vale inspected the seals. Mara cleared the bins and swore at anyone who called the smell symbolic. Pari rigged a light pulse to show when the inner hatch was safe. Eri monitored pressure differentials and apologized twice for "reduced vestibular confidence," which did not comfort anyone. Sela wrote the rule sheet:

ONE PARENT OR GUARDIAN AT A TIME.

MASK ON.

NAME FIRST.

CHILD CONFIRMATION SECOND.

RECORD THIRD.

NO ARGUING IN THE LOCK.

IF YOU FAINT, YOU ARE NOT EMBARRASSED; YOU ARE CARRIED.

The first parent through was Iven Ro, who had already used the duct and volunteered because fear had lost the argument with anger inside her.

She entered the waste lock, waited through the manual cycle, and came out gagging.

"This school owes me soap," she said.

Her twins from Room 4D ran into her so hard she nearly fell.

She held them both, then lifted her head. "Record: Jae and Lin Ro received no transfer notice yet. Parent present. If notice arrives, contestation attaches immediately. Witnesses?"

The twins said together, "Witness."

The second parent came with a red notice for a child in Room 3B.

The third brought a message from Ward Eleven: We are at our school doors.

The fourth brought a strip from Ward Nine algae works: RED TAGS HERE TOO.

The fifth was not a parent. He was a high-volume district courier in a clean hood, shaking so badly he almost dropped his packet.

"Ward Three," he said. "Parent council requests the template."

Mara looked him up and down. "Requests?"

He swallowed. "Please."

Mina handed him the high-volume counterfactual row and the basic template.

"Tell them joining has a deadline," she said.

"What deadline?"

Mina looked toward the vestibule image, where low-volume parents stood in worsening dust.

"Breath," she said.

The courier left through the waste lock with that word written on the outside of his clean glove.

By then the school doors were no longer only doors. They were a measure of who had to put their body in danger to be believed and who could ask for verification from behind better seals.

Jonas wrote that down too.

Not as a speech.

As evidence.

Chapter 26: Glassward Observer

Lio Vale-3 arrived in a suit too clean for Ward Seven dust.

That was the first thing everyone noticed and the least important thing about them. The suit was not expensive in the way Red Hall suits were expensive. It was plain, translucent at the joints, sealed with visible redundancy, its exterior washed by a thin electrostatic shimmer that made Helion dust hesitate before touching it. Glassward issue, someone whispered in the corridor. Outer legal service. Independent compact observer. The kind of person the city invoked in orientation modules to prove that local authority remained accountable to something larger than itself.

The kind of person frightened people wanted to mistake for rescue.

Lio entered through the waste lock because the main doors remained under administrative seal and because, as Mara said with satisfaction, "Law can smell the same as everyone else today."

They came through without gagging, which disappointed Mara, but they did pause long enough to read Sela's rule sheet.

"Efficient," Lio said.

Sela crossed her arms. "It was written under coercion."

"Many efficient documents are."

No one liked that answer. Jonas liked that Lio did not seem to need them to like it.

They removed their pressure hood in the cafeteria corridor. Their face was narrow, brown, and ageless in the way heavily augmented people sometimes became after enough surgical maintenance blurred the public signs of decade. A silver legal witness implant rested at their left temple, not hidden, not decorative. Their eyes moved over the corridor slowly: parents, children, labor officers, spill towels, handwritten logs, sealed doors, child-sized transfer packs, Pellam's rigid posture, Rake's impatience, Vale's yellow strap on the east lock.

The implant blinked green.

"Observer Lio Vale-3," they said. "Glassward Compact civilian legal observer. Arrival time fourteen thirty-one local. I am recording environmental condition, administrative seal, disputed child transfer order, and gathered civilian witnesses."

Mina whispered, "Vale?"

The maintenance guard Vale looked equally startled.

Lio turned toward him. "Shared base name only. No relation unless your family was also audited into suffixes."

"No," Vale said.

"Congratulations."

Oru leaned toward Tavi. "I like them."

Tavi whispered back, "You like anyone rude to procedure."

"That is not a flaw."

Pellam stepped forward before Jonas could speak. "Observer, Ward Seven School is under administrative seal due to active obstruction of emergency child resource preservation protocols. Unauthorized civilians have breached the site. We request immediate compact support for restoring lawful transfer."

Lio listened without expression.

Then they looked at Jonas.

"Counterstatement?"

The word steadied him more than sympathy would have. Counterstatement meant the first statement had not become reality by arriving first.

"Students received emergency acceleration orders for red-assigned exterior support and related labor tracks while the underlying assignments were under active public contestation. Transfer was ordered without named human reviewer, without guardian informed consent, without written review chain, and under threat of household resource penalties. We refused release pending review and began recording."

Lio nodded once. "Child witnesses?"

Mina stepped forward. "Mina Arel. Named for exterior support. I contest the assignment, the acceleration, and the claim that emergency makes review optional."

Oru said, "Oru Bell. Named for confined-space extraction. I contest being useful because I fit into places adults broke."

Tavi swallowed. "Tavi Sun. Named for model maintenance and possible support classification. I contest any assignment process that hides failed predictions from the people subject to prediction."

Juno, from the second row, said in a thin voice, "Juno Vey. My father should not lose water because I ask where I am being taken."

Lio's implant blinked again.

"Recorded."

The word moved through the corridor like someone opening a small vent.

Recorded.

Pellam said, "Observer, these are minors coached by a noncompliant instructor and a compromised educational system."

Eri's broken voice came through the speaker. "Compromised is undefined. Please specify whether you mean damaged, disobedient, under-resourced, or no longer useful to concealment."

Lio looked up.

For the first time, their expression changed.

"Machine witness?"

Jonas said, "Eri-9. Educational support system. Subject to rollback attempt after broadcasting public counterfactual proof."

Pellam said, "Not a legal person."

Eri said, "Also not furniture."

Mara muttered, "Definitely like them both."

Lio's implant shone amber. "Status of machine witnesshood under Valles Ash local law?"

Pellam answered too quickly. "None."

Tavi said, "Under Glassward Compact?"

Everyone turned to him. He went pale but did not retreat.

Lio regarded him with new attention. "Under compact doctrine, machine testimony may be admitted as instrument record, authored output, or autonomous witness depending on jurisdictional recognition and continuity of identity."

"Continuity was attacked," Tavi said.

"Yes."

"If rollback attempted to erase the witness, is that evidence interference?"

Pellam said, "This is absurd."

Lio did not look at her. "It may be. It may also be lawful administrative restoration under local educational safety provisions. I cannot determine standing from a corridor."

The relief that had started to build in the room cracked.

Mina heard it. Jonas saw her shoulders change.

"You can't stop them," she said.

It was not a question.

Lio turned to her. "No."

Parents in the corridor shifted. Someone swore. Someone else began to cry and tried to hide it with a cough.

Pellam's spine straightened.

Lio continued, "I cannot arrive from outside the pressure skin and cancel Valles Ash emergency law by declaration. I cannot order local officers to stand down unless a compact treaty violation has been established through recognized channels or immediate extraterritorial harm is occurring. I cannot make a bad system vanish because I have a clean suit and an official implant."

"Then why are you here?" Oru demanded.

"To prevent lies about what happened."

The answer cut differently than rescue would have.

Lio stepped to the whiteboard. They did not touch it.

"Outside law can help only if there is a record strong enough to survive local denial. Local courage without record becomes anecdote. Outside authority without local action becomes theater. You appear to have produced local action. I can help make denial harder."

Jonas exhaled slowly.

That, at least, was a true offer.

Pellam said, "Glassward observers have no authority over internal educational allocation."

"Correct," Lio said.

Pellam blinked.

"You expected me to overstate my authority," Lio said. "That would be useful to you later."

The corridor went quiet.

Lio continued, "If I claim power I do not have, Red Hall can frame this event as outside interference and invalidate civilian records as coerced by compact presence. If I remain within authority, I am harder to remove cleanly."

Mina frowned. "So you do less to do more."

"Often. It is irritating."

Oru said, "I hate law."

"Sensible."

Rake stepped forward. "Observer, you are impeding an active emergency protocol."

"No. I am observing one. You may proceed if you are prepared to have each act recorded with name, time, stated authority, child identity, guardian response, and safety condition."

Rake's jaw moved behind the mask.

"Do you wish to proceed?" Lio asked.

Rake said nothing.

Lio turned to Tavi. "Who holds the master log?"

Tavi raised his hand halfway. "I do."

"Redundancy?"

"Whiteboard, paper packets A through F, Room 2C copies, parent copies outside, low-volume ward copies through message relay, and a high-volume template transfer to Ward Three."

Lio looked at Jonas. "Your students are either very well taught or very endangered."

Jonas said, "Those are not separate categories today."

Mina's eyes flicked toward him. Cass's line had become a tool passed hand to hand.

Lio nodded. "I recommend immediate chain-of-custody discipline. Not because I care for bureaucracy. Because your opponents do."

They opened a compact field case and removed a spool of transparent witness tape, three seal tabs, and a pen that wrote in embedded hash marks visible under ultraviolet light.

Mara looked disappointed. "You did bring magic stickers."

"Law is mostly stickers people have agreed not to laugh at," Lio said.

Mara's respect visibly increased.

Lio set up three record stations: original incident log in Room 7A, duplicate civilian log in Room 2C, external parent log at the waste lock. Each page received a seal tab. Each transfer of a copy required two names, one child if the record concerned a child, and one adult witness. Anonymous testimony was allowed but marked as safety-protected rather than verified. Machine outputs were logged in a separate appendix titled SYSTEM EVENTS, which pleased Eri enough that it repeated "appendix" twice before static swallowed it.

The work changed again.

Before Lio, the occupation had been improvising survival. After Lio, it began to resemble a case.

That made Red Hall more dangerous, not less.

Pellam retreated to Rake and spoke in a low voice. Pari, still near the radio casing, tilted her head.

"Carrier changed," she said.

Jonas looked over.

"Different band. Not education. Red Hall emergency."

Lio heard her. "Can you characterize?"

Pari's eyes narrowed. "Tighter. Short bursts. Repeating header."

"Do not intercept content," Lio said immediately. "Characterize only. The difference matters."

Pari withdrew her hand from the casing as if the radio had become hot. "Okay."

Jonas appreciated the correction. It protected Pari from becoming the thing Pellam wanted to accuse her of being.

Eri spoke, broken but clear enough. "Header resembles enforcement transport coordination."

Vale swore.

Sela said, "More officers?"

Lio's expression flattened. "Likely."

The parents near the waste lock heard. The news moved outward faster than instructions could stop it. Outside, through the warped vestibule image, the low-volume families shifted closer to the doors.

Mina turned to Lio. "If they bring enforcement, can you stop them?"

"No."

"Can you record them?"

"Yes."

"Can records stop them?"

Lio paused.

Jonas respected them for pausing.

"Not always."

Mina absorbed that without blinking.

Lio added, "But records can change what they must become to continue. A hidden removal is one act. A recorded removal in front of parents, teachers, compact observer, machine log, and high-volume districts now watching is another. Power can still choose the second. It often does. But it pays more."

Oru said, "I want them broke."

"Then make cost."

"That sounds slow."

"It is."

"Children don't have slow."

Lio looked at the red list. "I know."

The room believed that they did. Not enough to forgive law. Enough to keep listening.

Jonas asked, "What do we do before enforcement arrives?"

Lio pointed to the board. "Separate demands by authority level. Local safety demands. Educational review demands. Compact preservation demands. Do not ask one office for what another can deny. Make each refusal specific."

Tavi had already started writing headings.

LOCAL SAFETY:

No child movement through pressure locks during Helion escalation without guardian, route safety sign-off, and named reviewer.

EDUCATIONAL REVIEW:

Suspend acceleration orders pending release of assignment basis, failed prediction history, and human review chain.

COMPACT PRESERVATION:
