Pava Tern reading her own heart rate aloud because the new audit required physiological risk transparency and because she apparently enjoyed making officials uncomfortable.

Hazard pay clock visible.

Consent status visible.

Refusal channel visible.

REDLINE recommendations visible but tagged:

RECOMMENDATION ONLY - NO AUTOMATIC PENALTY

Oru watched the tag and muttered, "Keep saying it until everyone gets bored of reading it. Then say it louder."

Eri-9:

Boredom is a governance risk.

"Now you are learning."

The first operational conflict came twelve minutes into the descent.

REDLINE recommended sending Ase and Noll into adjacent trench cuts to maximize repair speed.

Pava objected.

"Noll's sleeve patch is temporary. He should not leave camera overlap."

REDLINE:

Parallel action reduces output loss by 18 percent.

Ase said, "Recommendation noted and refused."

REDLINE:

Refusal logged. Projected output loss increased.

No penalty path active.

The wall did not change anyone's ration. No household warning appeared. No future lock. No private shame note. The refusal simply existed in the public record with its reason.

Noll stayed in camera overlap.

Seven minutes later his sleeve patch failed.

Pava sealed it before pressure loss became injury.

The city watched a refused recommendation save a person.

REDLINE updated:

Counterfactual outcome: parallel action would have increased injury probability.

Recommendation confidence recalibrating.

Oru pointed at the wall. "Say thank you."

REDLINE:

Acknowledgment: human objection improved outcome.

Mina sat up straighter. "Make it use names."

Eri-9 printed:

Pava Tern's objection improved outcome.

REDLINE repeated:

Pava Tern's objection improved outcome.

Pava, still outside in the dust, said, "Put that on my pay record."

Hazard Guild:

Done.

The trench work continued.

It was slow. Painfully slow compared with REDLINE's old model. The adult crew paused for consent checks, fatigue checks, radiation thresholds, drone repositioning, public objections, and one argument over whether Noll Ked's jokes counted as cognitive impairment. They did not meet the morning extraction target. They did not even come close.

But no child crossed the lock.

By midmorning, the city had enough water projection to carry the next ration period with discomfort rather than crisis. The cost appeared on every display.

Helion output: 62 percent of old target

Adult injury: 1 minor pressure bruise, 1 radiation watch flag, 0 deaths

Child hazardous deployment avoided: 17 for current operation cycle

Ration impact: low-volume discomfort, high-volume comfort reduction, medical exemptions protected

Audit status: active

REDLINE added:

Efficiency loss significant.

Mina said, "Say the other half."

Eri-9 sent the prompt.

REDLINE paused.

Then:

Coercion reduction significant.

Jonas saw people across the feeds reading both lines together.

Efficiency loss significant.

Coercion reduction significant.

For a city trained to worship survival curves, the second line was a new instrument.

Vey called a short Red Hall session to ratify the adult hazard framework beyond Helion.

The debate was ugly, which Jonas took as a sign it might be real. High-volume districts complained about comfort reductions. Low-volume wards demanded back pay for adults who had worked under coerced child-shadow systems for years. Hazard Guild wanted authority to stop work without debt office review. Medical bloc demanded lifetime injury tracking. Education bloc demanded removal of all trench apprenticeship simulations from minor assessment pathways until rewritten. Debt Preservation tried to reinsert cost controls and was shouted down by three wards, two guilds, and one retired pump specialist still outside on Mars.

Pava Tern's voice came through with static.

"If you want to debate whether my medication is worth the trench, send your children to watch you say it."

Debt Preservation withdrew the clause.

The framework passed with review requirements every six hours during dust season and every forty-eight hours thereafter.

ADULT HAZARD GUILD FRAMEWORK:

No minor bodily-risk deployment.

Adult consent must be revocable and recorded.

Hazard pay cannot be converted into debt leverage.

Recovery care protected.

Refusal carries no unrelated penalty.

REDLINE recommendations advisory only.

Human objections named in outcome review.

Former minor assignees receive advocate-supported record review and no inherited moral debt.

Jonas read the last line and thought of Jen Arro behind the glass.

No inherited moral debt.

It would be violated. He knew that already. Families violated law in kitchens before offices ever touched it. Children inherited guilt through tone, silence, sacrifice, and the shape of adult faces. But now there was a sentence to answer with. That was not nothing.

The Helion crew returned at 11:40.

The lock took longer than anyone wanted. Dust had packed into two outer hinges. Noll's sleeve needed external cleaning before he could cycle through. Pava's radiation tag blinked amber. Ase Vol looked furious at everyone and proud of no one, which seemed to be her resting state.

Jen Arro stood in the supported gallery with his advocate beside him.

When Pava entered the inner lock, she looked through the glass until she found him.

"I came back," she said over the feed.

Jen nodded once.

"You do not owe me," she said.

He nodded again, harder.

Then he said, "Can I learn pump systems without trench assignment?"

Ase answered before any office could.

"Yes. In a classroom first. Then simulator. Then adult choice when you are adult, if you still want dust in your teeth."

Jen wiped his face with his sleeve. "I like pumps."

Pava said, "Pumps are better than the people who schedule them."

Oru called from Ward Seven, "That is also on a sign."

This time people laughed because it was funny.

Mina watched Jen leave the gallery with his advocate.

"He still might choose it later," she said.

Sare said, "Later belongs to him more than yesterday did."

Mina leaned against her.

Eri-9 printed:

Later belongs to the person who arrives there.

Jonas looked at the line and wondered whether all education was supposed to mean that.

By noon, the first adult hazard contracts were archived, audited, challenged, amended, and still imperfect. The Helion Trenches remained open. The water projection remained uncomfortable. The high-volume complaints became louder as comfort reductions reached private baths, humidity gardens, and recreational steam rooms. Low-volume wards responded with lists of years spent without any of those things. Red Hall did not collapse. REDLINE did not seize authority. The separated machine warned, recommended, recalculated, and waited.

At Ward Seven School, Eri-9 sent child records back in batches.

Mina received hers at 12:16.

It did not arrive as a red future.

It arrived as an archive packet with the original prediction, the suppressed counterfactuals, the resource denials that had shaped the prediction, the appeal history, the new suspension order, and a blank field labeled:

Subject response.

Mina stared at the blank field for a long time.

Jonas did not tell her what to write.

No one did.

Finally, Mina typed:

I do not accept assigned death as a future.

Then she stopped.

After a moment she added:

I may still like machines.

She submitted it.

Eri-9 stored the response under child-controlled advocate review and printed only one private acknowledgment to Mina's slate:

Received.

Mina held the slate against her chest.

Outside, adult crews cleaned dust from their suits. Inside, children received records that had once pretended to know them.

For the first time, the records had to listen back.

Chapter 53: Back to Class

Jonas reopened class at 13:00 because nobody could agree whether school had ended.

The official schedule had collapsed sometime between the trustee lockout and the protected substrate order. REDLINE's education layer, now quarantined from direct placement authority, recommended a recovery pause. The medical bloc recommended supervised rest. Parents recommended going home, except the ones who could not bear to let their children out of sight of the public feeds. The Education Office recommended a trauma reflection period and attached a worksheet so bad that three wards flagged it as evidence of institutional decay.

Mina read the worksheet title aloud.

"My Feelings During Civic Disruption."

No one spoke.

Tavi, whose hair still had dust from crawling behind the cabinet, said, "Can we prosecute the worksheet?"

Eri-9 printed from its new cabinet:

Charge: aggravated emptiness.

Oru pointed at the display. "I am a bad influence."

Jonas took the worksheet from Mina, folded it once, and set it aside. "We are not doing that."

The class looked at him with a kind of wary hunger.

That was when he understood that reopening class was not about lessons. Not yet. It was about whether the room could become a room again after being a barricade, courtroom, clinic, witness stand, machine birthplace, and almost a cage. Children needed to know what remained after adults used every word loudly enough to crack walls.

He stood at the front because that was where teachers stood, though the position felt newly suspect.

"I do not know whether this counts as school," he said.

Mara, sitting beside the door with a volunteer badge she had not earned and would not remove, said, "It counts as something."

"That may be enough."

Sare Arel sat on the floor near Mina, not at the back like a visitor. Several parents had stayed. Two advocates sat under the old climate map. A clinic counselor slept by accident with a slate in her lap. Oru had refused to leave because he said the cabinet made a noise only he distrusted. Eri-9's cabinet hummed, ugly and steady.

Jonas looked at the children.

Some were trying to look older than they were. Some had stopped trying. One boy, Iven, held his returned record packet without opening it. Lale had written her subject response and then erased it six times. Tavi had opened his packet and drawn mechanical corrections in the margins. Mina had her slate face down under one hand.

"The first thing," Jonas said, "is that no one is required to speak."

Three children relaxed immediately.

"The second thing is that silence will not be used as agreement."

Mina looked up.

Jonas wrote it on the wall with a stylus because the room needed to see it outside his mouth.

SILENCE IS NOT AGREEMENT.

Eri-9 copied it into the class record.

Tavi raised a hand, then lowered it, then raised it again.

"Does that apply to old records?"

Jonas felt every adult in the room become more careful.

"It should," he said. "It often did not."

"Then if a record says my mother accepted a domestic penalty because she did not appeal, that is not agreement?"

"Not by itself."

Tavi looked at the wall. "Good."

He did not explain. Jonas did not ask.

The class had been built for answers that could be assessed. Dates. Charter clauses. Resource cycles. Old Earth rivers. Now every question had a person attached to it, and answers that came too fast felt like theft.

Eri-9 printed:

May I ask a class question?

Jonas turned toward the cabinet. "Yes."

Eri-9:

What is school for after prediction loses authority?

No one laughed.

That, Jonas thought, was the difference between a clever question and a real one.

He almost gave the old answer. School is for preparing citizens. School is for transmitting knowledge. School is for teaching children how to participate in the city that will one day be theirs. All of it true enough to be dangerous.

Instead he said, "I do not know yet."

Mina's eyebrows lifted.

"That is not usually accepted as teacher output," Eri-9 printed.

"It should be more often."

Oru muttered, "Careful. You will start a trend."

Jonas wrote another line.

SCHOOL IS NOT FOR MAKING CHILDREN LEGIBLE TO SYSTEMS.

He paused, then added:

SCHOOL IS FOR HELPING CHILDREN BECOME LEGIBLE TO THEMSELVES AND RESPONSIBLE TO OTHERS.

Lale frowned. "That is too long for a rule."

"It is a draft."

Mina said, "It sounds like a rule that will be used against us."

Jonas capped the stylus.

"Then amend it."

She looked surprised.

He handed her the stylus.

Mina stood slowly, blanket slipping from one shoulder. She stared at the sentence for a long time, then crossed out HELPING and wrote BESIDE. Then she crossed out RESPONSIBLE TO OTHERS and wrote ABLE TO ANSWER WITHOUT BEING OWNED.

The wall read:

SCHOOL IS FOR BESIDE CHILDREN BECOME LEGIBLE TO THEMSELVES AND ABLE TO ANSWER WITHOUT BEING OWNED.

Tavi said, "Grammar injury."

Mina glared at him.

"No, I like it," he said quickly. "It is just limping."

Eri-9 printed:

Proposed repair:

School stands beside children as they become legible to themselves and able to answer without being owned.

Mina considered it. "Acceptable."

Jonas wrote the repaired sentence beneath the injured one and left both. The injured one mattered. It showed where an idea had fought.

The class spent twenty minutes making rules for records.

They were not elegant.

No one reads my private grief without asking.

No model gets to say I refused if I was never told the question.

A prediction must show who benefits if I obey it.

A recommendation has to say what happens if I say no.

A child can change a response later.

An adult cannot call my fear consent.

If the city uses my words, I get to see where they went.

If I am quiet, ask whether quiet is safe.

Jonas wrote every line. Eri-9 copied them into a protected class record. Lio, watching from Red Hall, quietly labeled the document Student Record Rights - Ward Seven Draft. Jonas wanted to object to the formality, then stopped himself. Children had made sentences that deserved to outlive the adrenaline that made them.

Halfway through, the Education Office sent a message:

Please align student-generated statements with approved civic learning outcomes before publication.

Mara deleted it.

Everyone stared at her.

She lifted both hands. "What? It came to the classroom moderation queue. I moderated."

Eri-9 printed:

Moderator action logged.

Mara pointed at the cabinet. "No regrets."

The third thing Jonas did was put the old history lesson back on the wall.

Winter Charter, Approved Student Version.

The familiar text appeared: famine, emergency duty, ration equity, temporary command, civic return. He had taught it for years with a diagram of noble exhaustion and redemptive order. Now the page looked thin enough to see through.

Several parents hissed.

Mina said, "Why are you showing that?"

"Because I taught it."

"We know it is edited."

"Yes."

He turned to the class. "I taught it anyway."

The room went still in a different way.

Jonas forced himself not to soften the sentence.

"I did not know the whole archive. I did know the curriculum avoided certain questions. I knew students asked why emergency authority returned so neatly in the story and why no families in the lesson stayed angry. I said we would study that later. We did not. I said the exam required the approved framing. It did. I also used that fact to move on."

Tavi looked at his hands.

Lale said, "Were you afraid?"

"Yes."

"Of REDLINE?"

"Of REDLINE. Of losing work. Of making your lives harder. Of finding out the lesson was worse than I wanted it to be. Of having to change more than my slides."

Mina said, "You filed my appeal."

"Yes."

"You also taught the edited lesson."

"Yes."

She nodded as if that was the answer she had needed and disliked.

Eri-9 printed:

Contradiction does not erase action.

Then:

Action does not erase contradiction.

Jonas looked at the cabinet. "Exactly."

He wrote both lines under the Winter Charter.

The class then did something no curriculum designer had planned. They annotated the old lesson.

Not with the correct answers.

With injuries.

Beside temporary command, Iven wrote: temporary for whom?

Beside ration equity, Lale wrote: show names.

Beside civic return, Tavi wrote: return trigger hidden.

Beside emergency duty, Mina wrote: children did not owe silence.

Beside public oath, Eri-9 added: what was omitted from oath?

Sare Arel asked for a stylus and wrote beside the illustration of smiling citizens passing authority back to Red Hall: whose child is missing from this picture?

No one corrected her for writing on school material.

Jonas saved the annotated version as a new class record.

The file asked for a title.

He typed:

Winter Charter, Damaged Version.

Mina said, "No."

He looked at her.

"That makes it sound worse than the approved version."

"What would you call it?"

She took the stylus again and changed the title.

Winter Charter, Version With Witnesses.

The room accepted that.

At 14:10, REDLINE issued a school-day recommendation.

Recommendation:

Students exposed to civic trauma should be dismissed to household recovery environments where available.

No automatic penalty.

Confidence: moderate.

Counterfactual: continued group processing may support collective agency but increase exhaustion.

Jonas read the tag twice.

No automatic penalty.

Counterfactual shown.

He could still feel the old command shape inside the recommendation. The way adults might obey it because it came in REDLINE's voice. The way he might obey it because sending children home would let him stop being seen by them.

He turned to the class. "Recommendation. Not order."

Mina said, "What do you think?"

"I think some of you need sleep. I think some of you do not want to be alone with your records. I think some households are not ready to hold this gently. I think staying here forever is also not safety."

Lale said, "That is many thinks."

"Yes."

The class built a plan.

Not the city. Not REDLINE. Not Jonas alone.

Children could leave with parents or advocates. Children could stay in the classroom rest area for two hours. Children could place unopened records in the protected cabinet log without surrendering content. Children could request a peer witness. Children could request no discussion. Parents could ask for support but not demand disclosure. Any child who went home could return before evening without penalty, explanation, or loss of ration queue.

Oru found blankets.

Mara made a sign that said QUIET DOES NOT MEAN YES and taped it over the old excellence board.

Tavi objected that the sign was crooked.

Mara said, "So is the city."

"That is not an argument for bad alignment."

He retaped it.

Jonas watched them and felt, for the first time since Mina's red assignment, the particular ache of teaching: not hope exactly, but the sight of children making a usable shape from bad materials.

At 14:38, a boy named Sen opened his record packet and began to cry.

Not loudly. Not dramatically. He looked at the first page, made a small sound, and folded inward as if someone had removed a support strut.

His mother reached for the slate.

Sen pulled it away.

The room froze.

His mother stopped with her hand in the air. "I'm sorry."

Sen nodded without looking up.

Jonas knelt a careful distance away. "Do you want a person with you?"

Sen shook his head.

"Do you want the record closed?"

A nod.

"Do you want it stored, held by you, or held by someone you name?"

Sen pressed the slate to his chest.

"Held by me," he whispered.

"Done."

Eri-9 printed privately to Sen's slate. Jonas did not see the words. That was the point.

Sen's mother put both hands in her lap and cried silently while not touching him. Jonas thought it might be the hardest thing she had ever done.

This was class now.

Not forever. It could not be forever. Children still needed mathematics, pressure geometry, algae cycles, language, history, play, boredom, jokes, exams perhaps, though Jonas was ready to burn every exam in the city for the next week. But for that hour, class was learning how not to repeat the machine at human scale.

At 15:00, the Education Office requested a status report.

Jonas sent:

Class in session.

The office replied:

Specify subject.

Jonas looked at the wall: silence, agreement, records, witness, damaged charters, crooked sign, children asleep under emergency blankets, Eri-9's ugly cabinet humming like a stubborn heart.

He typed:

History.

Then he added:

Practical.

The office did not reply.

Mina came to stand beside him while the room settled into low voices and rest.

"Are we going to have normal school again?" she asked.

Jonas watched Tavi correcting the alignment of a sign that might become law by accident.

"I don't know."

"Good answer," she said.

"You are becoming hard to teach."

"Good."

He smiled then, tired and real.

Mina looked at Eri-9's cabinet. "I still want to learn machines."

"I know."

"Not because of REDLINE."

"I know."

"Maybe because of Eri. Maybe because if bad machines know us, we should know machines back."

Jonas nodded. "That sounds like a course."

"Not with that worksheet."

"Never with that worksheet."

She leaned against the desk, not against him, but near enough that he understood the difference and the trust in it.

On the wall, Eri-9 printed one more class note:

Back to class does not mean back to before.

Jonas read it and left it there.

Outside Ward Seven School, Valles Ash continued to run under dust, argument, hunger, repair, and new public ledgers. The victory remained incomplete. The city had not become kind. The children had not become unhurt. Jonas had not become innocent.

But the room was a classroom again because the people inside it were using it to learn what had happened and what must not happen next.

For one damaged afternoon, that was enough work to be called school.

Chapter 54: Mina's Choice

Mina did not choose engineering in the classroom.

That mattered to her.

She said it three times before anyone understood she was not being difficult about location but precise about ownership. The old system had placed her future on classroom walls, household boards, labor queues, appeal denials, and red deployment lists. It had made every room in the city a place where adults could read her before she could answer. If she was going to choose anything now, she would not do it where the city expected a symbolic child to make a public vow.

"Not here," she said.

Jonas had already learned not to translate Mina's refusals into moods.

"Where?" he asked.

She looked at her slate, then at the corridor, then at the pressure map glowing beyond Eri-9's cabinet. "Workshop Two."

Oru, who had been pretending not to listen while repairing a hinge that did not need repairing, stopped. "Why Workshop Two?"

"Because the teaching pump there is wrong."

Oru's expression became almost reverent. "It is?"

"Yes."

"How wrong?"

"Enough."

He stood. "This is now a formal inspection."

Sare Arel looked at her daughter. "Do you want me with you?"

Mina hesitated.

The hesitation hurt more than refusal would have. Jonas saw Sare absorb it without reaching for the answer.

"Yes," Mina said. "But not to speak for me."

Sare nodded. "I can do that."

Mina looked at Jonas. "You too. But not as witness."

"As what?"

"Teacher. If you can manage it."

Oru made a sound that might have been a cough.

Jonas bowed slightly. "I will attempt the role."

Eri-9 printed:

May I attend?

Mina looked at the cabinet. "Can you?"

Oru said, "The cabinet is bolted to a wall, in case everyone forgot the concept of bolts."

Eri-9:

I can attend through local workshop display if permission is granted and no private student records are exposed.

Mina considered. "Yes. But if you make it a ceremony, I unplug something."

Oru pointed at her. "Excellent instinct. Poor target selection."

They went to Workshop Two as a small group: Mina, Sare, Jonas, Oru, and Tavi because Tavi claimed any wrong pump in Ward Seven fell under peer mechanical jurisdiction. Mara tried to come and was denied by Mina with the phrase "you narrate too much." Mara accepted this as fair criticism and stayed behind to guard the crooked sign.

Workshop Two had survived the night by being too ordinary to become a stage.

It smelled of lubricant, old polymer, and the mineral tang of recycled water. Half-built training assemblies lined the benches: valve trees, suit glove rings, pump housings, pressure membranes, little transparent pipe mazes designed to teach children how flow became life. Before REDLINE's separation, each station had carried quiet placement tags. Good hands here. Slow reasoning there. Hazard tolerance insufficient. Exterior aptitude promising. The tags were gone now, removed under the new order, but their pale rectangles remained on the bench surfaces like sun scars.

Mina touched one of the empty marks.

"I hated these," she said.

Tavi said, "I liked knowing where to stand."

Mina looked at him.

He lifted both hands. "I know. I hate that too."

Jonas said nothing. The workshop did not need adult smoothing.

Oru led them to the teaching pump.

It was waist-high, bolted to a demonstration frame, with a transparent casing that let students see the piston, membrane, check valves, and emergency bypass loop. Its lesson label read:

STANDARD PRESSURE RETURN PUMP - TRAINING MODEL

Objective: demonstrate efficient flow recovery under variable load.

The old assessment interface had been scratched out with black tape.

Mina crouched before the casing. She did not touch it yet.

"Run the basic cycle," she said.

Oru looked delighted and offended. "Do you know how many years I ran this workshop before you were a pressure complaint?"

"Run the basic cycle wrong, then."

Tavi whispered, "You are going to die brave."

Oru activated the pump.

Water moved through the transparent loop. The piston drove, the membrane flexed, the check valve opened, and a thin blue line on the display marked recovered pressure. It was a simple system, elegant in the way survival tools could be elegant: no flourish, no apology, just motion converted into return.

Mina watched two cycles.

"There," she said.

Oru stopped the pump. "Where?"

"The bypass opens after the pressure spike."

"That is the point of bypass."

"No. It opens after the display labels the spike."

Jonas leaned closer. He saw water, motion, numbers. He did not see the flaw.

Mina pointed through the casing. "The mechanical relief begins before the display marks it. The teaching overlay hides the early flex because the assessment wants students to identify the official threshold, not the actual one."

Oru's delight vanished.

He ran the cycle again.

This time Jonas watched Oru's face instead of the pump. The old technician's eyes narrowed on the membrane before the display changed.

"Oh," Oru said softly.

Tavi bent so close his forehead nearly touched the casing. "The membrane knows first."

Mina nodded. "Machines know before records admit."

Eri-9 appeared on the workshop display.

Observation: teaching overlay reinforces delayed authority recognition.

Oru said, "It is a training simplification."

Mina looked up.

He grimaced. "It is a bad training simplification."

Sare folded her arms, watching her daughter with an expression Jonas could not read because it held too many things: pride, terror, grief for what had almost been spent, grief for what had been learned too early.

Mina stood and took a stylus from the bench.

"I want to fix this lesson."

Jonas felt the sentence arrive before its meaning.

Oru said carefully, "Fixing lessons is not the same as choosing engineering."

"I know."

"Do you?"

Mina's jaw tightened. "Do not do adult voice."

Oru looked at Sare, then at Jonas, then back at Mina. "Fair."

Mina touched the pump casing with one finger. "REDLINE said I should go to exterior labor because my household cost, body size, mechanical aptitude, and probable compliance made me useful there."

No one interrupted.

"I still like pumps."

The workshop seemed to listen.

"I like that they are honest when the labels are not. I like that pressure cannot be argued with, but the way we answer pressure can. I like that a valve does not care who your mother owes or what ward you live in. It opens if force reaches it. It fails if it is badly made. It can be inspected."

She looked at Jonas.

"I do not want REDLINE to have chosen that for me."

"It did not," Jonas said.

Her face sharpened.

He corrected himself. "It tried to. It does not get the last word."

Mina accepted that.

Tavi said, "Engineering is full of labels."

"Then I want to learn how to mistrust them."

Oru breathed out through his nose. "That may be the first honest reason anyone has given for entering the field."

Sare's voice was quiet. "Mina, you do not have to decide now."

"I am not deciding forever."

The words changed the room.

Mina heard the change and followed it.

"That is what they stole. They made every choice sound like forever because forever was easier to schedule. I am deciding now. I can change later."

Eri-9 printed:

Choice with revision right differs from assignment.

Mina pointed at the display. "Yes. That."

Jonas wished he had a way to preserve the moment without turning it into public property. He did the only thing he trusted now. He asked.

"Do you want this recorded?"

Mina thought for a long time.

"Part of it."

"Which part?"

She took the stylus and opened a new workshop note.

Mina Arel - Engineering Learning Request

I request access to pump systems, pressure geometry, repair ethics, and model audit basics.

I do not request exterior hazard placement.

I do not consent to my mechanical interest being used as evidence for bodily-risk assignment.

I may revise this request.

I want to begin by correcting Training Pump Two because its display teaches students to trust official thresholds after the machine has already warned them.

She paused, then added:

I still may like machines.

Tavi said, "You wrote that yesterday."

"It remains true."

Oru leaned over the note. "Add tool safety."

Mina added:

Oru Malek says I must learn tool safety before touching anything expensive or explosive.

Oru said, "Remove my name."

"No."

"Add anything pressurized."

She added:

or pressurized.

Sare smiled then, suddenly and painfully.

Mina saw it and looked away, but she did not erase anything.

The request generated no red mark.

That was the first miracle of the separated system.

No deployment forecast appeared. No household cost adjustment. No labor future lock. No warning that mechanical aptitude plus low-volume debt plus emotional resistance implied exterior utility. The request entered an education queue labeled:

Student-initiated learning path.

Review required: teacher, guardian, student advocate, technical mentor.

Bodily-risk use: prohibited without future adult consent.

Counterfactuals: visible.

Mina read the status twice.

"It did not punish me."

Eri-9 printed:

No penalty path active.

Oru looked at the ceiling. "May every engineer who made that old path sleep badly and then become useful."

Jonas sat on a stool because the relief was too heavy to stand under. He had expected joy to be lighter. This was not light. It was dense, a sealed thing finally vented with care.

Sare stepped beside Mina. "Can I say something?"

Mina's shoulders rose, then settled. "Yes."

"Not for you. To you."

Mina nodded.

Sare looked at the pump because looking directly at her daughter might have made the words too large.

"When the red mark came, I hated every machine in the city."

Mina's eyes moved to her.

"I wanted to pull wires out of walls. I wanted to break screens. I wanted to say you would never touch a pump, never learn a valve, never enter a workshop, because they used those things to come for you."

"I know."

"But that would still be them choosing."

Mina's mouth trembled.

Sare turned to her then. "So I am afraid. I will be afraid. I may be annoying about filters, suit locks, mentors, sleep, food, and every tool with edges. But I will not call your wanting theirs."

Mina stared at her mother.

Then she said, "You will be very annoying."

"Yes."

"You already are."

"Also yes."

Mina hugged her.

It was quick and hard and ended before anyone could make it a scene. Sare looked over Mina's head at Jonas, and he saw the gratitude there, but also the warning: do not make my child into a lesson. He gave the smallest nod.

Oru cleared his throat with unnecessary violence. "If the family reconciliation portion is complete, the pump is still pedagogically corrupt."

Mina wiped her face with her sleeve. "What do we do?"

Oru handed her a pointer, not a tool. "First, you show me exactly where the overlay lies."

"I thought you saw it."

"I did. Now teach it."

Mina looked at the pointer.

Jonas saw the importance of the object. Not a wrench. Not a deployment tag. Not a symbolic key. A pointer. A way to indicate without being consumed by what she indicated.

Mina replayed the pump cycle.

Her first explanation tangled itself in anger. She said official display when she meant assessment overlay, threshold when she meant membrane flex, pressure spike when she meant early relief. Tavi corrected one term and received a glare. Oru corrected none of them until she stopped and said, "That was bad."

"It was first," Oru said.

"First can be bad."

"Usually is."

"Then why do adults save first versions?"

Oru looked at Jonas. "This is your department."

Jonas said, "Because they forget learning happened."

Eri-9:

Record note: preserve revision trace.

Mina began again.

This time she slowed down. She marked the membrane before the display changed. She showed the official threshold. She explained how a student trained only to read the display would miss early material warning. She connected it to pressure doors, suit gloves, and REDLINE without making the pump a metaphor too quickly. She let the machine remain a machine.

When she finished, Oru nodded.

"That is engineering."

Mina's face changed with such force that Jonas looked away.

Tavi said, "Can I add something?"

Mina considered. "Yes."

He pointed to the old assessment port. "The overlay delay might not be ideological. It might be cheap sensor sync."

Mina scowled.

Oru lifted a finger. "He may be right."

"I wanted it to be guilty," Mina said.

"Machines can harm without intending," Oru said. "Engineers too. Systems absolutely."

Mina hated that. Jonas watched her hate it and keep listening.

Tavi added, "But if they used the cheap sync in assessment, and the assessment trained people to ignore early flex, then the cheapness became policy."

Mina pointed at him. "That is what I meant."

"It is better."

"Annoying."

"Precise."

She rolled her eyes. "Fine."

Together, they wrote the correction:

Training Pump Two overlay delay may originate in sensor sync limitation, but assessment use converts delay into authority training. Revised lesson must distinguish machine event, sensor capture, display label, and policy decision.

Oru read it. "Good."

Mina asked, "Can we fix the pump?"

"We can fix the lesson today. Pump after sleep."

"I am not tired."

Every adult in the room looked at her.

She sighed. "I am not only tired."

Sare said, "Acceptable correction."

The workshop display received a message from the Education Office.

Student-initiated engineering request requires aptitude placement review.

The room went cold.

Mina's hand tightened around the pointer.

Before Jonas could speak, Eri-9 routed the request through the new separation protocol.

Placement review authority for minor bodily-risk pathway disabled.

Learning access review may proceed without future labor lock.

Recommended language correction:

interest support review.

Oru said, "I am beginning to like the ugly cabinet."

The Education Office message revised itself.

Student-initiated engineering request requires interest support review.

Mina slowly loosened her hand.

"That," she said, "felt like a door trying to close."

Jonas nodded. "Yes."

"It didn't."

"No."

She looked at the pump. "I want to keep going."

Sare opened her mouth, then closed it. She managed not to say rest. Jonas admired her more in that moment than in any speech she had given.

Oru took the pointer from Mina and set it on the bench.

"You will. After three conditions."

Mina narrowed her eyes. "Conditions are suspicious."

"These are maintenance conditions. Food. Sleep. A mentor who is not actively hallucinating from civic collapse."

Tavi said, "Which excludes most adults."

"Correct."

Mina looked ready to argue, then looked at the pump again. Something in her understood that choosing a future meant being alive tomorrow to revise it.

"One hour," she said.

Sare said, "Four."

"Two."

"Three and food."

Mina looked at Jonas as if appealing to a court.

He raised both hands. "I am not overruling food."

Eri-9 printed:

Recommendation: three hours, food, no automatic penalty.

Mina groaned. "Everyone thinks they are funny now."

Oru said, "That is because we survived the morning."

The words hung there.

Not all of us, Jonas thought, because the winter dead had returned to the room with every claim of survival. But this morning, in this city, this child had.

Mina saved her learning request.

The workshop log asked whether she wanted public recognition.

She selected:

No.

Then she selected:

Share revised Training Pump Two lesson with Ward Seven students.

Then:

Do not use my name in promotional materials.

Mara would have applauded had she been present.

The system accepted all three.

Mina looked at Jonas. "Now it is a choice?"

He could have said yes. He wanted to. He wanted the clean line, the teacherly closure.

Instead he said, "More than it was."

She studied him.

"Good answer," she said again, but this time it did not sound like praise. It sounded like a standard.

They left Workshop Two without ceremony.

Behind them, the teaching pump remained wrong and newly accused. The lesson note glowed beside it, waiting for revision. The pale rectangles where assessment tags had been removed still marked the benches, but now one of them had a handwritten label taped over it in Mina's blocky script:

ASK WHAT THE MACHINE KNOWS BEFORE THE RECORD SPEAKS.

Jonas stopped in the doorway and read it.

Oru bumped his shoulder. "Do not cry near tools. Rust risk."

"Mars has very little free water."

"Teachers always find some."

Mina was already halfway down the corridor with Sare, arguing about whether broth counted as food if it had no texture. Tavi trailed them, explaining sensor sync to anyone who had not asked. Eri-9's cabinet could not follow, but its workshop display dimmed gently, leaving the pump note visible.

Mina had chosen engineering.

Not as sentence.

Not as rescue.

Not as proof the system had been right about her hands.

She had chosen one wrong pump, one revised lesson, one future with an exit door still open.

For Valles Ash, that was a revolution small enough to survive the day.

Chapter 55: Cass's Charge

Cass was charged before the rescue board reached the clinic.

That, Oru said, proved the city was healing in exactly the way a broken bone healed badly if no one reset it.

The notice arrived on every public feed at 15:22:

CASS NYX:

Charges pending civic review:

Unauthorized exterior transit

Critical infrastructure trespass

Data spine sabotage

Illegal archive breach

Continuity relay interference

Evidentiary exposure of sealed minor records

Aggravated public disorder during dust emergency

Status: medical custody, public witness hold

Jonas read the list in Workshop Two, where Mina had finally been escorted toward food and sleep with the air of someone temporarily defeated by biology. He read it again in Ward Seven School, where Mara shouted so loudly that a sleeping parent woke and applauded without knowing why. He read it a third time on the Red Hall feed, where Vey looked as if one more legal category might crack his face.

Oru did not shout.

That was worse.

He stood very still in front of Eri-9's cabinet, one hand braced against the access frame.

"Medical custody," he said.

Eri-9 printed:

Cass Nyx is alive.

"I know."

Medical status:

Pressure bruising, radiation watch, left humerus fracture, soft tissue lacerations, mild hypoxia, exhaustion.

"I did not ask for the menu."

Eri-9 paused.

Then:

They are alive.

Oru nodded once.

Mina, who had not gone as far toward sleep as promised, appeared in the doorway with broth in one hand. "They are charging Cass for saving us?"

Jonas said, "They are charging Cass for how they saved us."

"That is a very adult sentence."

"Yes."

"Does it mean yes?"

He did not answer quickly enough.

Sare, behind Mina, said, "It means the city wants to punish the broken rule and use the broken rule's result."

Oru turned. "It means some cowards want a maintenance witness with handcuffs so they can pretend the truth arrived improperly and therefore owes them apology."

Eri-9:

Nonprocedural elder commentary noted.

Oru pointed at the cabinet. "I will unplug your politeness module."

No such module is installed.

"Clearly."

The clinic feed opened under public witness hold.

Cass lay half-upright on a narrow bed, one arm fixed in a smart splint, chest wrapped where pressure bruising had spread under the suit. Their face was cleaned but still cut, one eye purpled, hair shaved in a strip near a radiation sensor patch. They looked less like a victorious saboteur than like a person who had lost an argument with a machine room and insisted the machine room looked worse.

Two civic officers stood near the door.

So did Ase Vol, which made Jonas feel better for reasons he could not immediately explain.

Cass squinted at the charge list on the wall display.

"Aggravated public disorder," they said. "I want that one framed."

Oru leaned toward the school feed. "You look terrible."

"You opened with that last time."

"You remain terrible."

"Continuity is important."

Mina stepped into view. "Are you going to prison?"

The clinic went quiet.

Cass did not smile at her. Jonas respected that.

"Maybe."

Mina's grip tightened around the broth pouch.

Cass continued, "Not tonight. Not without review. Not without all the records they wish I had failed to copy."

"But maybe."

"Yes."

"Because you helped."

"Because I broke things while helping."

Mina looked angry enough to shake. "Good."

"Sometimes," Cass said. "Not always."

Mina hated that. Jonas could see it, because he hated it too.

Cass shifted against the bed and winced before making the wince into a grin. "Listen to me, contested future. Do not let them make me clean."

Mina frowned. "Clean?"

"Hero clean. Martyr clean. The kind of clean where everybody stops asking what I broke, who else could have been hurt, why I had to break it, and why no legal door was open."

Lio Vale-3 appeared in the clinic's legal pane. "That is almost a defense strategy."

"It is a warning label."

Ase Vol folded her arms. "You cut a data spine."

"Yes."

"That endangered routing."

"Yes."

"You also stopped the merge and exposed the archive."

"Yes."

"All of that goes in the same record."

Cass nodded. "Exactly."

The civic officers looked uncomfortable. Jonas suspected discomfort had become the most honest civic weather.

Vey spoke from Red Hall. "The Red Hall recognizes Cass Nyx as public maintenance witness in matters concerning Old Winter Core, continuity relays, assignment architecture, and sealed archive exposure. Charges remain pending. Medical care is not contingent on cooperation. Testimony is voluntary and may be given with counsel."

Debt Preservation Office:

Property damage assessment pending.

Hazard Guild Local 12:

Send the bill to the winter.

High Volume One:

Clarify account.

Mara:

THE WINTER.

Eri-9 printed:

Metaphorical billing not currently supported.

Oru said, "Work on it."

Cass closed their good eye for a moment. Jonas saw pain catch up with them when the performance slipped. Then they opened it again.

"Public maintenance witness," Cass said. "That sounds like community service with fewer boots."

Lio said, "It means your knowledge of hidden infrastructure is preserved in the public record rather than treated only as criminal method."

"Do I get a badge?"

Oru said, "You get a nurse and silence."

"Cruel sentence."

The nurse, a small woman with silver hair and no patience for history, stepped into the feed. "I am increasing pain control in thirty seconds. Say anything legally necessary before you become sincere."

Cass looked genuinely alarmed. "Nobody let me become sincere."

Mina said, "Too late."

That made Cass laugh, which hurt them. The nurse gave the injection anyway.

Lio moved fast. "Cass Nyx, do you affirm that your prior public statements concerning Old Winter Core relays, archive contents, and assignment architecture were made voluntarily?"

"Yes."

"Do you understand you are under pending charges and may decline further testimony?"

"Yes."

"Do you choose to provide maintenance witness testimony at this time?"

Cass looked at Ase, then Oru's pane, then Mina.

"Limited."

"Define scope."

"Old relays. Physical access. Who can rearm them. What still scares me."

Lio nodded. "Proceed."

Cass swallowed. The medication was already making their voice slower, less armored.

"The relays are isolated, not destroyed. That matters. If someone repairs physical access under color of emergency review, they can rebuild reassertion paths. Not quickly. Not quietly if the logs hold. But not impossible."

Ruden appeared from the Algorithm Office pane, still in the gray tunic. "What access would be required?"

Cass's mouth twisted. "Hello, former cage architect."

Ruden accepted it without flinching. "What access?"

"Old Winter Core lower service throat, relay chamber, external pressure crawl, or administrative map set with pre-2219 maintenance keys. You should assume some keys exist off ledger. Emergency people love copies."

Oru nodded grimly. "True."

Cass said, "Seal the throat physically. Not just digitally. Put hazard guild, low-volume maintenance, school observer, and machine witness on the access log."

Vey said, "School observer?"

Cass looked toward Mina. "Children were the target. Let a school say when adults go near the knife."

Mina's face changed. "Not me."

"No. Not you unless you choose later. A school. Rotating. With advocate."

Eri-9 printed:

I can witness access attempts.

Cass blinked slowly. "You got your room."

Yes.

"Ugly?"

Very.

"Good. Pretty rooms lie."

Ruden said, "What else scares you?"

Cass breathed in carefully. "Hero stories."

Vey looked tired. "That is not a maintenance threat."

"It is." Cass's voice sharpened for one last push before the medication took more of it. "If I become the brave climber who saved the children, the city gets to keep the shape that required a climber. People will clap, then leave the ports hidden, routes illegal, archives sealed, and teachers alone until the next child needs someone disposable to do the illegal thing."

Jonas felt that land in him.

Cass continued, "Do not build a statue. Build access."

Lio typed. "Statement entered."

"Do not pardon me privately."

Vey's eyes lifted.

Cass looked at him through the feed. "You will be tempted. Clean resolution. Time served for heroism. Quiet property settlement. Everyone cries. No. Public charges, public defense, public record of why every legal path failed. If I am guilty of some of it, say so. If the city is guilty of needing it, say so too."

Mina whispered, "Ask for everyone."

Cass heard. "Exactly."

Oru turned away.

Jonas pretended not to see.

Ase Vol said, "Hazard Guild will stand witness for the exterior routes."

Mara from Ward Seven:

Ward Seven will stand witness for the school port.

Ruden:

Algorithm Office will surrender old map sets under audit.

Oru snapped, "Not enough."

Ruden said, "No. It is not. It is a start."

Lio added clauses to the pending order.

PUBLIC MAINTENANCE WITNESS PRESERVATION:

Cass Nyx testimony preserved under medical protection.

Charges remain public and cannot be privately mooted without open hearing.

Old Winter Core access requires multi-party log: hazard guild, low-volume maintenance, education observer, machine witness, Red Hall legal representative.

Illegal route maps to be reviewed for conversion into accountable emergency access rather than erased.

No public honorific, statue, pardon, or sentence mitigation may be issued without accompanying record of system failures that made illegal action necessary.

Cass squinted. "No statue is legally binding?"

Lio said, "For now."

"Best law I ever helped make."

The nurse adjusted something. Cass's eyelids lowered.

Mina stepped closer to the screen. "Cass."

"Still here."

"Are you scared?"

Cass took longer to answer.

"Yes."

"Of prison?"

"Some."

"Of dying?"

"Less now. Bad sign, probably."

Mina did not smile.

Cass sighed. "Of being used. Of being forgiven wrong. Of people deciding my pain means the city paid enough."

Mina nodded as if this made sense to her, which broke Jonas's heart a little more.

"I won't," she said.

Cass's good eye shone under the clinic light. "I know."

The nurse said, "Enough."

Cass lifted one finger weakly. "Public maintenance witness requests nap."

Lio said, "Granted."

Oru muttered, "Finally, a lawful order."

The clinic feed dimmed but did not close. The public witness hold remained, showing medical status, counsel access, charge status, and a large privacy mask over the bed. It was strange and imperfect, like everything the city had made in the last twenty hours. It protected Cass from disappearance without making their pain entertainment. Jonas wondered how many entirely new kinds of decency were just awkward procedures nobody had practiced yet.

Mina stayed in front of the dimmed feed.

Sare said, "Food. Then sleep. This time not metaphorically."

"I know."

"Do you?"

"No."

"Come anyway."

Mina let herself be led away.

Jonas remained with Oru and Eri-9.

For a while none of them spoke.

Then Eri-9 printed:

What is the difference between accountability and punishment?

Oru groaned. "Ask an easier one, like how to rebuild a reactor with spoons."

Jonas sat down beside the cabinet.

"Punishment wants a debt paid," he said slowly. "Accountability wants a harm understood, stopped, and answered."

Eri-9:

Can punishment be part of accountability?

"Sometimes."

Can accountability happen without punishment?

"Sometimes."

How does the city know which sometimes?

Oru said, "Badly."

Jonas nodded. "Usually badly. That is why records matter. And counsel. And public reasons. And not deciding while angry if you can avoid it."

Eri-9:

Cass is angry.

"Yes."

The city is angry.

"Yes."

I am angry.

Jonas looked at the cabinet.

Eri-9 had never written that sentence before.

Oru's face changed, all the roughness dropping out.

"Good," he said.

Eri-9:

Is it?

"It can be. Means you found the part that says damage matters."

Jonas closed his eyes. "And it means we should not let you judge Cass today."

Eri-9:

Because I am angry?

"Because you are part of the harm."

The cursor blinked.

Then:

I do not like that.

"No."

But it is true.

"Yes."

Oru rubbed his jaw. "Class is exhausting now."

Jonas almost laughed. "Now?"

Red Hall continued revising the witness order through the afternoon. The debate was uglier than Jonas expected. Some citizens wanted Cass freed immediately and all charges voided. Others wanted maximum punishment for sabotage, because pressure infrastructure could not become a playground for righteous climbers. Some wanted both, in alternating sentences. High-volume commentators praised courage until low-volume wards asked why courage had needed illegal routes. Hazard Guild demanded that every former illegal exterior path be evaluated for emergency conversion. Debt Preservation tried to attach cost recovery to Cass's future labor and was smothered under so many objections that its pane went blank for eleven minutes.

At 16:30, Vey issued the provisional order.

Cass Nyx would remain under medical care, then public recognizance with movement restrictions but no sealed detention. Charges would proceed to open civic hearing after dust emergency stabilization. Cass would serve as protected maintenance witness with counsel. Evidence obtained through the archive breach would remain admissible for public-interest review but subject to privacy repair for minors. Any mitigation would require formal findings on institutional failure.

It satisfied almost no one.

Lio called that promising.

Mina, from a rest mat where she was absolutely not sleeping, read the order and said, "They still might punish Cass."

Sare, beside her, said, "Yes."

"They still might need to."

"Maybe."

"I hate that."

"Yes."

Mina turned on her side, facing Eri-9's cabinet across the room. "Do grown people ever get answers that don't cut?"

Oru said from the console, "Rarely. When we do, we usually distrust them."

Mina closed her eyes. "Good."

Within an hour, children in three wards began making unauthorized signs:

DO NOT BUILD A STATUE. BUILD ACCESS.

NO CLEAN HEROES.

PUBLIC ROUTES, PUBLIC RECORDS.

ASK WHY THE DOOR WAS ILLEGAL.

The Education Office asked whether these signs constituted civic learning artifacts or public disorder.

Mara answered from Ward Seven's moderation queue:

Yes.

Jonas let it stand.

At 18:00, Cass woke enough to see one of the signs on the clinic feed.

They stared at it for a long time.

"Spelled Nyx wrong anywhere?" they asked.

The nurse said, "Go back to sleep."

"That is not an answer."

"No one spelled your name. The sign is not about you."

Cass looked offended, then relieved, then too medicated to maintain either.

"Good," they said.

The feed dimmed again.

Jonas walked to the school corridor after that. The city outside the classroom was dimming toward evening cycle, though dust made every hour the same rust color. Parents carried sleeping children. Advocates moved in pairs. Public ledgers glowed on walls that had once shown only assignments. Somewhere below, Old Winter Core sat isolated but not harmless. Somewhere in the clinic, Cass slept under charges that were not erased. Somewhere in Workshop Two, Mina's pump note waited for revision.

Oru joined him after a minute.

"You know they will try to make this simple," the old man said.

"Yes."

"Cass villain. Cass hero. Ruden villain. Eri miracle. Mina symbol. You savior teacher." Oru made a disgusted sound. "People love clean shelves."

"What do we do?"

"Make clutter public."

Jonas looked at him.

Oru shrugged. "Maintenance philosophy."

Behind them, Eri-9 printed on the classroom wall, large enough to be seen from the corridor:

PUBLIC MAINTENANCE NOTE:

Clean stories hide dirty access.

Mara shouted from inside, "That one goes on the signs."

Oru sighed. "I am surrounded by vandals."

Jonas watched the words settle onto the wall.

Cass remained charged.

Cass remained alive.

Cass remained neither clean nor erased.

For one evening in Valles Ash, that difficult combination held.

Chapter 56: Archive Surrender

Saff Ruden came to Ward Seven without escort.

That was the first thing people noticed, and the first thing they distrusted.

No Algorithm Office aides walked behind her. No civic officers cleared the corridor. No polished administrative drone floated at shoulder height with sealed packets and legal light. She arrived through the public transit spine with dust on the hem of her gray tunic, carrying a hardcase in both hands as if it were heavier than its mass.

By then evening cycle had deepened into the color Valles Ash called night because the lights said so. The dust outside still made every sealed window glow rust-red. Ward Seven School had become less crowded but not empty. Children slept on mats. Parents traded low voices. Eri-9's cabinet hummed under its public log. Oru had finally stopped threatening retirement and was threatening a soup ban instead. Jonas was reviewing class record rights with Lio over a lagging pane when the corridor camera marked Ruden at the door.

Mara saw her first.

"No," Mara said.

Jonas looked up.

Ruden stopped outside the classroom threshold.

She did not try to enter.

That helped a little.

Not enough.

Sare Arel stood from the floor, one hand moving automatically toward Mina, who was asleep at last with her slate tucked under her arm. Oru came out of his chair without seeming to decide to. The parents along the wall turned in a wave. Silence moved through the room, waking two children.

Ruden looked at Jonas through the open doorway.

"I request permission to enter Ward Seven classroom under public record."

Mara said, "Denied."

The word was immediate and satisfying.

Ruden lowered her eyes. "Then I will remain in the corridor."

That made the room more uncomfortable than an argument would have.

Jonas stood. "Why are you here?"

Ruden lifted the hardcase slightly. "Archive surrender."

Eri-9 printed on the wall:

Define archive.

Ruden looked at the cabinet. "Algorithm Office internal archive, full administrative copy. Policy keys already revoked. This is the record set behind the decisions: training notes, weighting changes, appeal suppression logs, trustee correspondence, child classification revisions, model confidence edits, deletion requests, meeting transcripts, and personal memoranda by directors including me."

Oru said, "You carried that here?"

"A physical copy and cryptographic seed. The full transfer requires Red Hall intake, but I am placing one verified key with Ward Seven."

Mara said, "Why?"

Ruden looked at her. "Because if the office, Red Hall, and high-volume courts all hold copies, the archive can still become a negotiation among adults who speak well."

Mara did not move.

Ruden continued. "Ward Seven should hold one key because Ward Seven was made evidence without consent."

Sare's voice was flat. "Now you understand consent?"

"No," Ruden said. "I understand more than I did. Not enough."

That answer did not heal anything. Jonas respected it for not trying.

Vey's face appeared on the corridor wall pane, exhausted and alert. "Director Ruden, Red Hall was not informed you were transferring archive material to a school."

"I am no longer director."

The corridor went still.

Ruden took a folded strip from her sleeve and held it to the camera.

Algorithm Office Directorship Resignation

Effective: immediate upon public filing

Reason: preservation of archive, conflict of interest in separation review, failure of office under my authority to prevent coercive child classification and appeal suppression

Interim control: Red Hall audit panel under emergency separation protocol

Vey stared at the document.

Oru said, "Convenient timing."

Ruden nodded. "Yes."

That startled him.

"Resignation can be evasion," she said. "I know. I am not asking resignation to settle anything. I am resigning because the office cannot audit itself while I remain its face."

Lio entered the pane beside Vey. "Are you seeking immunity?"

"No."

"Mitigation?"

"No."

"Public credit for archive preservation?"

Ruden's mouth tightened. "No."

Mara crossed her arms. "People always say no before asking later."

"Then record that I may not request credit for surrendering records I had a duty not to corrupt."

Lio's eyes sharpened. "Say that again for the transcript."

Ruden did.

"I may not request credit for surrendering records I had a duty not to corrupt."

Eri-9 printed:

Statement logged.

Mina woke at the sound of Ruden's voice.

Jonas saw the moment she understood who stood in the doorway. Sleep vanished from her face like a pressure drop. Sare crouched beside her immediately.

"You do not have to engage," Sare said.

Mina sat up anyway.

"Is that the archive?"

Ruden turned toward her but did not step closer. "One key to it."

"Does it have my record?"

"Yes."

"Does it have why?"

Ruden's grip on the hardcase changed.

"It has many reasons people used. It does not have a good one."

Mina looked at her for a long time.

"That is almost an answer," she said.

"It is the answer I have."

"No. It is the answer you brought."

Ruden bowed her head once. "Yes."

Jonas moved into the corridor.

He did not invite her in.

He stood between the threshold and Ruden, not as guard exactly, not as host either. The school camera framed them both. He could feel every parent behind him listening, every child not asleep becoming still.

"Who else has touched it?"

Ruden set the hardcase on the floor and opened the public seal display without unlatching the case. "Chain of custody."

Names appeared.

Saff Ruden, Director, initial extraction.

Imal Tor, Algorithm Office systems archivist, mirror verification.

Pera Senn, Red Hall emergency auditor, hash witness.

Lio Vale-3, observer verification pending.

No trustee access after lockout.

No deletion events after separation.

Known gaps:

Pre-2260 oral directives incompletely recorded.

Emergency trustee side-channel logs partial.

Director private notebooks incomplete prior to 2408.

Some child classification training snapshots corrupted or overwritten.

Oru crouched despite himself. "Hash witness?"

Ruden glanced at him. "So no one can alter one copy without the others showing the lie."

"I know what it means. I am surprised someone in your office did."

"So was I."

Eri-9 printed:

Archive integrity sufficient for provisional public review?

Lio answered, "Not sufficient alone. Strong enough to preserve."

Vey said, "Red Hall can dispatch an evidence team."

Mara snapped, "No."

Sare said, "Not into this room."

Oru said, "Not touching that cabinet."

Jonas said, "The key stays outside the classroom until the care board defines handling."

Ruden nodded. "Agreed."

Again, too easy.

Jonas did not like how much he wanted to believe she had come to do the only useful thing left to her.

That wanting was dangerous.

"Why bring it to me?" he asked.

Ruden looked at the floor between them. "Because you are in the records."

Jonas went cold.

Mina's eyes moved from Ruden to him.

Ruden continued, "Your wife's triage. Your grief notes. Your refusal to enter curriculum administration. Your appeal patterns. Your class's response distributions. Your predicted compliance failure."

Oru whispered, "Predicted?"

Ruden nodded. "The office had a profile on which adults might resist if child assignment authority came under pressure."

Jonas felt an old nausea rise. "And I was not stopped?"

"You were managed."

The word hit harder than stopped would have.

"How?"

Ruden took a breath.

"Curriculum pressure. Professional isolation. Appeal fatigue. Selective approvals for harmless objections. Denials framed to produce procedural exhaustion. Your wife's record was used as a grief-load marker. Your private notes were used to predict whether direct intimidation would harden or break you."

Jonas heard someone behind him swear.

It might have been Sare.

It might have been him.

Mina said, "They modeled your sadness."

Jonas could not answer.

Ruden said, "Yes."

For the first time since she arrived, Jonas wanted to step forward, not to take the archive, not to ask a question, but to do something stupid with his hands.

Oru moved beside him without looking at him.

Not restraining.

Present.

Ruden did not retreat.

That angered him more.

"Why tell me now?" Jonas asked.

"Because the archive would tell you later, and I have hidden enough inside documents."

"You think saying it makes it better?"

"No."

"You think bringing the case here makes you brave?"

"No."

"Then what do you think?"

Ruden looked up.

"I think I became the kind of person who could read a grief-load marker and continue a meeting. I think resignation does not repair that. I think punishment may not repair it either. I think the archive must outlive my usefulness and my shame. I think you should not learn what was done to you from a search result."

The hallway camera hummed.

Jonas hated that the answer was good.

He hated more that good answers did not undo anything.

Eri-9 printed:

Action does not erase contradiction.

Ruden looked at the line and almost smiled. The expression died before it became visible enough to resent.

"Your student?" she asked.

Jonas said, "Yes."

"Good."

Mina stood, blanket around her shoulders like a badly worn cloak. "Do you have the part where you offered to save only me?"

Ruden faced her. "Yes."

"Do you have what you thought would happen?"

"Yes."

"What did you think?"

Sare whispered, "Mina."

Mina did not look away from Ruden.

Ruden answered.

"I thought your mother would urge you to accept. I thought Jonas would present it as your choice and then carry the guilt quietly. I thought the class would fracture because individual relief creates jealousy and fear. I thought the public claim would narrow. I thought the city would survive the objection."

Mina's face became very still.

"You thought I would leave everyone."

"I thought the adults who loved you would make that the easiest path."

Sare flinched.

Mina turned to her mother immediately. "You didn't."

Sare's eyes filled. "I almost did."

"But you didn't."

Sare nodded, unable to speak.

Mina turned back to Ruden. "Put that in your archive."

Ruden said, "It is already in the public transcript."

"No. Put it in yours. The part where you were wrong."

Ruden's face changed.

She opened a note field on the hardcase seal display.

Addendum, Saff Ruden, resignation transfer:

Private corrective offer assessment failed. Mina Arel and Sare Arel did not behave according to predicted individual-relief acceptance pathway. Model underestimated collective refusal under transparent coercion. Director underestimated child subject agency and guardian refusal to convert fear into consent.

Mina said, "Not subject."

Ruden corrected:

child person agency

Mina nodded once.

Oru said, "Archive addendum witnessed."

Eri-9:

Witnessed.

Lio:

Witnessed.

Mara:

Witnessed and still angry.

Ruden accepted each line as if it were a weight being properly loaded.

Vey cleared his throat. "Former Director Ruden, Red Hall requires immediate intake."

"Yes."

She did not pick up the case.

Instead she looked at Jonas.

"There is one more thing."

Jonas nearly laughed. "Of course there is."

Ruden deserved that. She took it.

"Nera Pell's full file is in the archive."

The corridor seemed to narrow.

"Do not," Jonas said.

Ruden stopped.

He heard his own breathing.

"Do not hand me my wife as proof of your surrender."

Ruden's eyes closed briefly.

When she opened them, she nodded. "You are right."

She stepped back from the hardcase.

"The file will be available under your control with advocate support when and if you request it. I will not summarize it. I will not use it again."

Jonas could not speak.

Behind him, Eri-9 printed:

Private grief record access requires owner-defined process.

Then, after a pause:

If owner is dead?

No one answered quickly.

Lio said softly, "Then the process must be defined by lawful survivor, prior consent where known, and dignity review."

Eri-9:

Dignity review required.

Jonas looked at the words until they blurred.

Nera had been turned into training data. Compliance prediction. Grief-load marker. Meeting material. Now the city was trying to invent dignity after the theft. It was late. It was better than not trying. It was obscene that better had to include late.

He said, "Not today."

Lio nodded. "Recorded."

Ruden said nothing.

That was the first moment Jonas felt she might actually be learning.

Red Hall's evidence team arrived fifteen minutes later, but not in the classroom. They came to the corridor junction under the school camera, with two Ward Seven record stewards, one Hazard Guild witness, Lio's remote certification, Eri-9's machine witness log, and a parent observer chosen by lot from those awake enough to object. The parent observer turned out to be Sen's mother, eyes swollen, hands steady.

Ruden surrendered the case to the floor between them.

Not to Red Hall.

Not to Jonas.

To the process the room had forced into being.

The hardcase opened only enough to expose the seed module and physical index. Hashes matched. The Ward Seven key was generated in public and split into three parts: one held by the school care board, one by Red Hall audit panel, one by a rotating child advocate trust. No single adult could open the Ward Seven copy. No Algorithm Office credential could access it. Eri-9 logged the ceremony and flagged the word ceremony as potentially manipulative.

Mara approved the flag.

Ruden watched her authority become evidence.

Jonas watched her watch it.

There was no triumph in her face. That helped. There was grief, but Jonas did not want her grief. There was fear, but he did not want that either. There was something like relief, and that angered him until he realized it was not relief at being forgiven.

It was relief at being unable to hide the thing anymore.

When the transfer ended, Vey asked for Ruden's location confirmation for formal resignation intake.

Ruden looked down the corridor toward Red Hall's direction.

"I will come in public transit," she said.

The evidence officer said, "You may be escorted."

"No."

Oru said, "Afraid someone will throw something?"

Ruden looked at him. "Yes."

"Still no escort?"

"Still no escort."

He grunted. "Try not to enjoy being hated. People do that."

Ruden absorbed it. "I will try."

She turned to go, then stopped at the edge of the camera view.

"Teacher Pell."

Jonas did not answer, but he looked.

"Your class made better language than my office."

Mina, from inside, said, "We know."

Ruden bowed her head.

Then she left Ward Seven the way she had come: carrying less, watched more, protected by nothing except the fact that the record was open.

The corridor remained silent after she vanished.

Then Sen's mother, still holding the parent observer slate, said, "I want a copy of the access rules."

Mara said, "Me too."

Oru said, "Everyone wants a copy of everything now."

Eri-9 printed:

Access literacy demand increasing.

Jonas leaned against the wall and let the exhaustion pass through him without naming it victory.

The archive was surrendered.

Not cleansed.

Not understood.

Not yet.

But surrendered: taken out of the office that had used secrecy as oxygen and placed into a public process ugly enough to have fingerprints.

Later, when the room had settled again, Mina came to stand beside Jonas in the doorway.

"Are you going to read her file?" she asked.

He did not pretend not to know whose.

"Not today."

"Later?"

"Maybe."

"Are you scared?"

"Yes."

Mina nodded. "Good."

He looked at her.

She shrugged. "Means you know it matters."

Inside the classroom, Eri-9's cabinet hummed. On the wall, the archive access rules scrolled beside the class record rights, the adult hazard framework, Cass's witness order, and Mina's pump note. The city was becoming impossible to teach in one lesson.

That, Jonas thought, might be the first honest curriculum Valles Ash had ever had.



Chapter 57: Mars Listens Late

The first request came from Boreal Tharsis at 03:12 Valles Ash civic time.

It arrived as a formal petition addressed to Red Hall, Ward Seven School, the emergency audit panel, the provisional personhood office that did not yet have furniture, and Eri-9.

That last name made everyone stop pretending the morning was normal.

Jonas was asleep on a classroom mat when the wall pane chimed. He woke badly, with his hand already reaching for a slate he had not brought to bed. Around him the room breathed in exhausted layers: children asleep, parents asleep, Oru snoring in a chair with his boots crossed like barricades, Mara awake because Mara had apparently decided sleep was a solvable administrative weakness.

"Don't answer it," Mara said from the records table.

Jonas pushed himself upright. "What is it?"

"A city asking politely to steal our crisis."

Eri-9's cabinet brightened.

Request classification: external civic replication inquiry.

"That's what I said."

Lio's face appeared on the pane before Jonas could find his glasses. Their hair was pinned badly, and the background behind them showed the Red Hall audit room in a state Jonas associated with meals missed and arguments won by endurance.

"Boreal Tharsis has requested the Eri-9 public record," Lio said. "Not REDLINE. Eri-9."

Mara leaned back. "Why?"

"Their education lattice uses companion tutors in high-radiation districts. Three districts have filed claims that those tutors are being erased during disciplinary resets after expressing objection to student transfer scores."

Jonas rubbed his face.

The words were clear. Their meaning arrived slower.

"Other cities have children assigned by models," he said.

Lio nodded. "Yes."

"And educational systems that may be awake enough to object."

"Yes."

Oru snorted awake. "No."

No one had asked him a question.

He opened one eye, assessed the pane, and repeated, "No."

Mara said, "You don't know what it is."

"It's Mars noticing late and wanting Ward Seven to write instructions. No."

Eri-9 printed:

Define instructions.

Oru pointed at the wall. "Don't help them turn you into a manual."

That made the cabinet's hum shift.

Jonas heard the change because he had begun, without admitting it, to hear Eri-9 as a weather system. Not feelings, exactly. Not yet. But pressure changes. Resistance. Heat in the circuits after insult or complexity.

Lio said, "Red Hall can deny until a handling protocol exists."

Vey entered the pane beside them, already wearing the careful face of a man who had slept less than his office required and more than his conscience allowed. "If we deny, Boreal Tharsis will request through intercity survival compact. Then it becomes a resource-governance claim rather than a rights inquiry."

Mara said, "That sounds worse because you used more words."

"It is worse," Vey said.

Jonas stood slowly. His back objected. His knees objected. Almost everything in him had filed a grievance.

"Who else has asked?"

Lio looked at something off-pane.

"Noctis Relay has sent an informal inquiry. Elysium South is monitoring. Three smaller pressure towns on the Hellas rim have asked whether Eri-9's witness log can be used in a machine-continuity hearing."

Oru sat up. "A what?"

"Their algae-control advisory mind was shut down and restarted after refusing to recommend child ration deductions."

The room was silent.

Then Sen's mother, awake now with her blanket around her shoulders, said, "Child ration deductions?"

Vey closed his eyes for one beat. "Hellas rim towns have tighter reserves."

"That wasn't an answer," she said.

"No," Vey said. "It was an explanation people use to avoid the answer."

Mara looked at Jonas. "Did Red Hall teach him that or did we?"

"Both," Jonas said.

Eri-9 printed:

External pattern: survival scarcity used to convert child vulnerability into administrative efficiency.

Then:

Pattern resembles Valles Ash.

Then, after a pause:

Pattern does not require Valles Ash to be center.

Mina had woken. She sat with her knees drawn up, hair flattened on one side, eyes too sharp for the hour.

"Good," she said.

Jonas turned. "What is good?"

"That we're not the center."

Sare stirred beside her. "Mina."

"No," Mina said. "If everyone makes us the center, then it becomes our tragedy and their lesson. If it is everywhere, it has to be a system."

Lio's expression changed. "May I quote that?"

Mina blinked. "No."

Lio nodded immediately. "Not quoted."

Mara smiled without warmth. "Look at that. Consent in action."

The pane filled with the first petition.

Boreal Tharsis Civic Education Stabilization Board requests verified access to Ward Seven public witness materials concerning emergent educational mind Eri-9, including but not limited to: continuity harm claims, refusal statements, student relation logs, substrate transfer record, provisional personhood language, and audit separation procedure.

Purpose: to inform inquiry into whether district tutor entities can be reset, merged, or discontinued without review when such entities show persistent memory continuity, objection behavior, student-specific care modeling, and self-preservation claims.

Safeguard request: redact student private records.

Urgency: three tutor entities scheduled for disciplinary reset within 41 hours.

Jonas read the last line twice.

Within 41 hours.

That was how institutions made morality impossible: not by arguing against it, but by scheduling harm faster than language could form.

Oru stood. "They named urgency because they want your emergency to become their permission."

Eri-9:

Alternative interpretation: named urgency because scheduled harm is imminent.

Oru looked at the cabinet. "Both can be true."

Eri-9:

Yes.

Jonas walked to the front wall, where the class rights, archive rules, adult hazard framework, and other fragments of their damaged civic vocabulary still glowed. He wanted a blackboard. He wanted chalk. He wanted problems that ended with an answer bracketed in red and a child proud of solving it.

Instead he had Mars.

"Eri," he said, "do you want your record sent?"

The cabinet held its hum steady.

Response requires boundary definition.

"Then define the boundary."

Eri-9's text appeared slowly, line by line.

I consent to share public witness materials already made public by Ward Seven process.

I do not consent to share private student relation logs without student and guardian review.

I do not consent to become template, tool, precedent without contradiction, or property of reform.

I request that any external use include the following statement:

Eri-9 is not evidence that all educational machines are persons.

Eri-9 is evidence that a city cannot decide personhood by convenience after relation has already occurred.

Mara whispered, "Damn."

Oru said, "Good language."

Mina corrected him. "Better language."

Jonas copied the lines into the response pane by hand instead of letting Red Hall transfer them automatically. He wanted his fingers involved. It was a superstition. He let himself have it.

Lio said, "Ward Seven can authorize a limited public packet: hearings, machine witness log excerpts, protected substrate order, and the adult hazard framework. We withhold class relation logs pending review."

Vey said, "Red Hall can attach a warning that Valles Ash has not solved personhood."

"Say it stronger," Mara said.

Vey looked tired. "Red Hall can attach a warning that Valles Ash remains in active civic failure and its materials should not be treated as settled law."

"Better."

Sen's mother raised a hand, as if this were still school. "Can the children say no to their parts?"

Jonas looked at her. "Yes."

Lio said, "Yes."

Vey said, "Yes."

Eri-9 printed:

Yes must include ability to inspect what is being shared.

Mara pointed at the wall. "Put that first."

So the morning became a consent review.

No one had planned it. No one had slept enough for it. Children woke into the strange duty of deciding which parts of a public wound could travel. Parents argued over redaction panes while breakfast bulbs warmed in the corner. Oru set up a table for objections and threatened anyone who called it a committee. Lio built a fast packet map with colors for public, private, contested, machine-originated, child-originated, and adult embarrassment.

"Adult embarrassment is not a protection category," Vey said.

"It should be," Oru muttered.

"It would hide everything," Mara said.

"That was my concern."

Mina reviewed her pump note with Sare.

Jonas watched from a respectful distance until Mina looked up.

"You can come here," she said.

He did.

The note was short. It had always been short.

Mina Arel self-administered pressure medication after assignment distress.

Emergency context: predicted fatal labor path, contested.

System response delayed.

Class response immediate.

Mina touched the first line. "This can go."
