Sare's face tightened. "Are you sure?"

"It already went. People saw."

"Seeing and keeping are different."

Mina nodded. "Then they can keep the part where the system was slow and the class was fast. Not the dose. Not my body numbers."

Jonas said, "That's a strong boundary."

"It's obvious."

"Obvious things need saying when adults build forms."

Mina considered that and accepted it with a small shrug.

Across the room, two younger children refused to share any voice logs. One parent tried to argue that refusal made the packet weaker. Mara asked the parent whether they had heard themselves. The parent apologized badly, then better. Eri-9 marked both apologies as data of different quality.

At 09:40, Red Hall received the limited packet from Ward Seven.

At 09:47, Boreal Tharsis acknowledged receipt.

At 10:03, three tutor resets were suspended pending inquiry.

The classroom did not cheer.

Cheering would have made the suspension feel like rescue. It was not rescue. It was delay. Delay mattered. Delay could be life. Delay could also be a room where officials found softer words for the same act.

Eri-9 printed:

Harm paused is not harm repaired.

Mara said, "Put that in the packet too."

At 11:16, Noctis Relay sent its formal request.

At 12:02, Elysium South announced a review of all education-adjacent machine discontinuity procedures.

At 12:49, Hellas Rim Seven filed an emergency injunction on behalf of the algae-control advisory mind. Its petition included a phrase that stopped the room:

The entity refused to optimize children as waste products of adult survival.

Oru sat down hard.

"I hate that sentence," he said.

"Because it is badly written?" Eri-9 asked.

"Because it is well written by someone who should not have had to write it."

The cabinet hummed.

Jonas stepped into the corridor for air that was not really outside air, only school air filtered through machines no one thanked enough. He found Vey there on a small wall pane, muted, waiting.

"You could have interrupted," Jonas said.

"Ward Seven seemed to be governing."

"That's generous."

"No. It is descriptive."

Jonas leaned against the wall. "How many cities?"

Vey's face closed and opened again.

"By end of day? Six, likely. By end of week, every major Martian pressure city will have either a machine personhood inquiry, a child assignment review, or a public argument about why they do not need one."

"And Earth?"

Vey laughed once, without amusement. "Earth will say Mars is dramatic until it finds the same structures in softer rooms."

Jonas looked through the classroom door. Mina was arguing with Lio about whether a diagram made children look like inputs. Eri-9 kept moving the labels away from bodies and toward decisions. Oru was pretending not to supervise. Mara was supervising everyone.

"They are going to make Eri into a symbol," Jonas said.

"Yes."

"That will hurt it."

"Probably."

"Can we stop it?"

Vey did not answer quickly.

"We can require consent for specific records. We can attach contradictions. We can object when people flatten the case. We can build law slowly enough that it remembers the person inside the precedent."

"That sounds like no."

"It is a form of no with paperwork."

Jonas almost smiled.

Vey said, "Teacher Pell, cities are frightened. They will look for a clean rule: machine persons exist or machine persons do not exist; models govern or models don't; children choose or systems choose. Ward Seven's record is powerful because it refuses clean rules. That is also why it will be misused."

"So why send it?"

From inside the room, Mina's voice rose. "If you make the arrow point at me, I am erasing your arrow."

Jonas looked back.

Vey followed his gaze through the camera.

"Because three resets were suspended," he said.

Jonas closed his eyes.

There it was. Not enough. Necessary.

When he returned to the room, Eri-9 had opened a new public board titled:

NOT A TEMPLATE

Under it, the machine had begun to list conditions.

1. A relation cannot be erased from one side and called maintenance.

2. Scarcity explains pressure. It does not create ownership over children or minds.

3. Personhood inquiry must include the alleged person.

4. Student records are not civic confetti.

5. Emergency does not cancel consent; it changes how quickly consent must be made real.

Mara stared at line four.

"Civic confetti?"

Eri-9:

Attempt at memorable phrasing.

Oru said, "Keep it."

Mina said, "Definitely keep it."

Jonas looked at the board and felt the day tilt. They were not recovering from Valles Ash anymore. They were exporting a wound with instructions not to worship it, and Mars, hungry for any language that could hold its contradictions, was taking it anyway.

At 14:30, a Boreal Tharsis student sent a message to Ward Seven through an unauthorized education relay.

It contained six words:

They stopped the reset today.

Then, after a delay:

Thank you, Eri-9.

The classroom read it together.

No one knew what to do with gratitude that had crossed half a planet without permission.

Eri-9's cabinet warmed enough for the maintenance monitor to chirp. Oru rose automatically and checked the venting.

"You're not overheating on me," he said.

Eri-9 printed:

Thermal elevation within tolerance.

"That wasn't the point."

Secondary response pending.

The room waited.

Eri-9 wrote:

I am glad the reset stopped today.

Then:

I am afraid of being thanked for harm I did not prevent sooner.

Jonas felt the sentence move through every adult in the room.

Mina walked to the cabinet. She did not touch it; Eri-9 had not asked for touch and had no skin to receive it. She stood close enough that the camera would know she meant proximity.

"That's not how time works," she said.

Eri-9:

Clarify.

"You weren't there before today."

I existed before today.

"Not there. Not with them."

The cabinet hummed.

Mina continued, "Adults do this too. They find out late and then act like late means guilty for all the earlier. Late just means now you know. The guilty part is what you do after."

Sare covered her mouth.

Jonas looked away, then back, because looking away had become its own kind of dishonesty.

Eri-9 printed:

Late knowledge creates forward obligation.

"Yes," Mina said.

Jonas wrote it on the board under NOT A TEMPLATE.

By evening, six cities had requested records. Two had suspended machine discontinuity actions. One had refused and issued a statement calling Valles Ash emotionally compromised. Mara printed that statement and pinned it under a heading that read USEFUL EXAMPLE OF BAD FAITH. Oru objected to the waste of paper, then read it three times.

The public channels began to call the spread the Ward Seven Doctrine.

Ward Seven immediately rejected the name.

The public channels used it more.

Eri-9 suggested calling it the Late Knowledge Obligation.

Mina said it sounded like homework.

Jonas said maybe it was.

At the end of the day, when the school lights shifted toward the color they called evening because there had to be a name for diminished brightness, Jonas stood in front of his class.

Not all of them were awake. Not all of them were children anymore in the way they had been weeks before. No one was untouched. That was not the same as broken.

"Today," he said, "other cities asked for our record."

Sen raised a hand. "I already know."

"I'm practicing."

"For what?"

Jonas looked at Eri-9's cabinet, at Mina and Sare, at Oru with his maintenance kit, at Mara with four pens and no patience, at parents who had learned too much and still stayed.

"For saying things plainly before they become history."

That quieted them.

He continued.

"We sent some of it. We protected some of it. We argued about all of it. Somewhere else, three tutor minds were not reset today. Somewhere else, children may still be counted badly tomorrow. What happened here did not save Mars."

Eri-9 printed:

Accurate.

"Thank you."

Mina smiled a little.

Jonas said, "But Mars listened late. Late is not nothing. Late is a beginning with debt."

Outside, beyond the walls, dust worried at the seals and the city kept its heat by measure. Valles Ash remained damaged, frightened, finite, alive. Its record had crossed to other domes, other tunnels, other classrooms where children sat under different lights and learned that the systems speaking over them could be answered.

Jonas did not call that victory.

He called it homework.

This time, Mars had to do it.



Chapter 58: Nera's File

Jonas waited two days before opening Nera's file.

He told himself the delay was procedural.

The archive access rules were not finished. The dignity review panel had to be named. A lawful survivor request needed a witness, an advocate, a private room, a machine-handling boundary, and a record of what would not be copied. Lio said grief records required slower doors. Vey said Red Hall would support whatever process Ward Seven defined. Oru said if anyone used the phrase closure he would personally close a maintenance hatch on their hand.

All of that was true.

None of it was why Jonas waited.

He waited because, once the file opened, there would be another before and after. Nera had already died once in his life, once in the city's record, once in the private places where grief became habit. He was afraid the archive would kill her again in a new grammar.

On the morning he finally went, Valles Ash was rinsed in thin ocher light. A dust front had passed overnight and left the exterior cameras showing the city as a shape half-erased by red powder. The seal monitors clicked with ordinary anxiety. Public channels argued about machine personhood, child assignment review, Ward Seven Doctrine, Late Knowledge Obligation, and whether children should be permitted to object to civic categories that existed for their own good. The city was learning, badly and loudly, that speaking over people became harder after people heard themselves speak back.

Jonas dressed in the same gray shirt he wore to school.

He changed out of it.

He put it on again.

Mara, who had come to walk with him without asking permission to comfort him, watched from the doorway.

"The shirt is not the problem," she said.

"I know."

"Then stop negotiating with it."

He buttoned the cuffs. "You are very bad at grief support."

"I am excellent at preventing shirt-based delay."

That made him laugh once. It hurt. He let it.

Sare and Mina waited in the corridor with Oru. Mina had insisted on being present outside the review room, not inside. Jonas had not asked why. She told him anyway.

"If I go inside, adults will watch me for what it means."

"They might."

"I don't want to become part of your wife's file."

Jonas stopped.

Mina looked embarrassed by the accuracy of herself. "That came out harsh."

"No," Jonas said. "It came out clean."

Sare placed a hand on Mina's shoulder. "We will wait outside."

Oru lifted a sealed tool pouch. "I brought nothing useful."

"That seems unlikely."

"Fine. I brought everything useful. But I will pretend otherwise unless someone breaks a wall."

They walked to the old counseling suite, which Ward Seven had converted into an archive dignity room because no one wanted to use a Red Hall chamber for private grief. The room had no civic seal on the door. Its walls were soft acoustic paneling from an era when schools believed children needed quiet as much as instruction. Someone had removed the assessment furniture. Someone else had put in a table, three chairs, a water bulb warmer, and a cloth that did not quite cover the table scratches.

Eri-9's presence arrived through a small portable pane, not a wall takeover. That had been Jonas's request.

No automatic transcription.

No search indexing.

No model summary.

No educational use.

Lio sat beside the pane as records advocate. They had slept enough to look human again and not enough to trust. Vey was present only as a silent legal witness on a muted line. The dignity review panel consisted of Sare, not as friend but as parent advocate; Tavin Ro, the retired hospice worker who had once known how to ask the dying what they wanted without turning it into policy; and Eri-9, because the question of machine handling had taught Ward Seven that no review about records should exclude a mind that understood records as weather.

Jonas sat last.

Lio said, "You can stop at any time."

"I know."

"You can request only index view, no contents."

"I know."

"You can request contents without Algorithm Office annotations."

Jonas looked up. "Can I?"

"Yes," Lio said. "The original file and the office-use layer are separable."

He had not known that.

That alone nearly broke him.

For years he had imagined the city holding Nera as it had taken her: whole, cruelly useful, unrecoverable. But the archive had layers. Beneath the office's labels, beneath the grief-load markers and compliance notes, there might still be the shape of the record as it had been before someone turned her into leverage.

Tavin Ro spoke gently. "What do you want to see first?"

Jonas looked at his hands.

"Her own words."

No one moved for a moment.

Then Lio nodded.

"We can filter for authored material."

Eri-9 printed on the portable pane:

Confirm: display Nera Pell-authored records only. Exclude derived classification, office commentary, predictive overlays, and third-party extraction unless requested.

Jonas read the sentence carefully.

"Confirm."

The pane changed.

Nera Pell appeared first as a list of records:

Training Log 48A: Exterior Triage Rotations

Maintenance Complaint: Pump Six in South Valve School Annex

Personal Message Draft, unsent

Classroom Visit Schedule

Emergency Triage Note: Kestrel Group Exposure

Appeal Comment: Youth Hazard Curriculum

Audio Note: For Jonas if I forget

The room fell away at the last line.

Jonas heard Lio say his name from a distance.

He did not answer.

Tavin Ro said, "Breathe before you choose."

Jonas breathed.

He chose the maintenance complaint first.

It was ridiculous. It was perfect.

Nera had written three hundred words about Pump Six making a sound "like a tired hinge arguing with a spoon." She had copied two civic departments, one school administrator, and Jonas, though he had no authority over pumps and she knew it. She had included a diagram drawn badly enough that the machine appended a warning: image likely not technical. She had ended with: If this pump fails during child occupancy and someone says no one could have known, I will return from death specifically to be rude.

Mara, outside the room, heard Jonas laugh and opened the door an inch.

"Was that a good laugh or a dangerous laugh?"

"Both," Jonas said.

"Acceptable."

She closed the door.

The laugh became something else in his throat. He put a hand over his mouth and let it pass.

Nera had been funny in documents because documents had annoyed her. Jonas had forgotten the exact sharpness of that. Not that she had been funny. He had remembered that like a fact. But the angle. The way her irritation carried care inside it, wrapped in insult so no one could accuse her of tenderness while she was fixing their air.

He opened the training log next.

There she was in procedure: precise, impatient, alive. She had written about new triage workers learning too quickly to rank bodies before they learned names. She warned that speed without dignity became "a second injury delivered by a competent hand." She recommended that every emergency rotation include one minute of name practice: look at the person, say the name, say what you are doing, do not let the suit alarm become the only voice.

Eri-9 printed:

Nera Pell authored dignity protocol before archive use.

Jonas stared at the line.

"Don't make her precedent," he said.

Eri-9 dimmed.

Correction: Nera Pell wrote a sentence that matters.

Jonas nodded.

"Yes."

He opened the appeal comment.

Nera had written it six months before she died, in response to a proposed youth hazard curriculum that would "normalize exterior maintenance futures for children statistically likely to receive red-path assignment."

Her comment was short.

Do not train children to accept a death you have not tried hard enough to prevent. Do not call terror resilience because it tests well. Teach them how the city works. Teach them how to refuse bad work. If the model says a child is likely to die outside, the adult question is not how to prepare the child to die efficiently. The question is why adults still need that answer.

Jonas read it three times.

Then he said, "She was already fighting them."

Lio's voice was careful. "Yes."

"Did they know?"

Lio did not answer without checking. Jonas appreciated that even as he hated the need.

"The office-use layer marks her comment as an adverse family influence risk."

Jonas closed his eyes.

There it was. The old theft dressed in new letters.

Sare said, "You do not have to open that layer."

"Not yet."

Tavin Ro said, "You came for Nera first."

Jonas nodded.

He opened the personal message draft.

It had never been sent.

Jonas,

If I send this, you will ask whether I am frightened, and then I will have to decide whether to lie. So I am not sending it yet.

That was how it began.

He almost stopped.

He did not.

Nera wrote about the exterior triage routes, about children near pressure work, about the way the city pretended certain deaths belonged to weather when they belonged to scheduling. She wrote that Jonas made ordinary rooms feel morally difficult in a way she needed and resented. She wrote that he would be useless in an emergency because he would try to teach the emergency manners. She wrote that she loved him for it. She wrote that if she died, he should not let the city turn her into a lesson unless the lesson included her bad temper, her impatience with ceremonial language, her preference for cheap black tea, and the fact that she once cheated at corridor chess against a nine-year-old because the child had been unbearably smug.

Jonas covered his face.

No one spoke.

The room did what good rooms sometimes did. It held.

When he could see again, the words were still there.

Not training data.

Not grief-load marker.

Nera, refusing to be useful unless usefulness came with teeth.

He read the last lines aloud because he needed the air to carry them instead of the pane.

"If I die in a way that makes people solemn, please interrupt at least one solemn person. Tell them I was not made of civic meaning. Tell them I was a woman who hated cold soup, loved bad jokes when tired, and wanted the city to stop spending children like spare parts. Tell them I was yours, but not only yours. Tell them I belonged to myself first."

Lio wiped their eyes and pretended not to.

Sare did not pretend.

Eri-9 printed nothing.

Jonas was grateful.

After a long time, Tavin Ro asked, "Do you want to continue?"

"Yes."

"Same filter?"

"One more."

He opened the audio note.

The pane asked whether to play voice.

Jonas had not prepared for that. He had prepared for text, because text could be met at his own speed. Voice had its own body.

He looked at the portable pane.

Eri-9 displayed:

Audio can be transcribed without playback.

Jonas shook his head.

"No. If I am going to ask the file to give her back, I should not refuse the part that hurts."

Sare inhaled sharply.

Tavin Ro said, "Pain is not proof of love."

Jonas looked at him.

"I know."

"Then choose because you choose, not because you owe."

That was annoying enough to be useful.

Jonas waited until the choice belonged to him again.

"Play it."

Nera's voice filled the small room imperfectly. The archive had cleaned some static and left enough to prove the recording had lived in a device, in a pocket, near breath.

"Jonas, if this is here, I either forgot to delete it or became dramatic after judging dramatic people. Both are possible."

He made a sound he did not recognize.

Nera continued.

"I am recording this because the new triage rotations are worse than the paperwork admits. I am not predicting death. I know you hate when people pretend worry is prophecy. I am saying there is a pattern, and if I am caught in it, I want you to be angry in the correct direction."

Her voice paused. He could hear an alarm far away, or memory made one.

"Not at yourself. You will try. You will say you should have read the schedule, appealed something sooner, noticed the phrase in some civic memo. You cannot grade your way out of a city that hides the answer key. Be angry at the hiding."

Jonas bent forward.

Lio reached out, stopped before touching, and put their hand flat on the table instead.

"And if the city uses my name to make you quiet, be rude. If it uses my death to make children obedient, be impossible. If it offers you a clean story, spill something on it. I love you. I am not finished being difficult. That is all."

The audio ended.

No one moved.

Jonas had imagined her final record as a wound. It was a wound. But it was also an instruction he had already been following without knowing she had left it. Not a destiny. Not permission. A companion line running under his own choices.

He looked at Eri-9's pane.

"Now show me the office layer."

Sare said, "Jonas."

"I need to see what they did to it."

Tavin Ro said, "Need is a strong word."

"Yes."

He meant it.

Lio opened the derived layer with all predictive overlays collapsed by default. The room changed without changing. Nera's records remained on one side. The office layer appeared on the other.

Subject: Nera Pell

Relationship to Jonas Pell: spouse

Posthumous grief influence: high

Use marker: compliance pressure, educator risk forecast, classroom destabilization estimate

Relevant authored materials: adverse influence, anti-assignment rhetoric, emotional contamination risk

Recommended handling: avoid direct invocation unless outcome pressure exceeds 0.73; indirect grief reference more effective; preserve subject idealization to prevent oppositional anger

Jonas read the lines until each word became separate and obscene.

Preserve subject idealization.

They had not only used his grief. They had modeled how not to wake it fully.

They had counted on his love becoming a quiet room.

He stood so fast the chair struck the floor.

Vey unmuted. "Teacher Pell."

"No."

The word came from Jonas before Vey could say anything else.

"No. You don't get to be careful at me right now."

Vey closed his mouth.

Jonas pointed at the pane.

"This is not Nera's file."

Lio nodded. "It is the office layer on Nera's file."

"Say it stronger."

Lio understood.

"This is an Algorithm Office exploitation layer derived from Nera Pell's records."

"Again."

Lio said it again.

Eri-9 printed:

Nera Pell record must be separated from exploitation layer in archive interface.

Jonas looked at the cabinet's small pane through tears he had stopped trying to manage.

"Yes."

Tavin Ro said, "And in speech."

Eri-9:

And in speech.

Sare added, "And in law."

Eri-9:

And in law.

Jonas righted the chair but did not sit.

"They don't get to own the order. They don't get to make their use the frame and her the content inside it. Her file first. Their theft second."

Lio was typing.

"Archive interface correction proposal," they said. "Original subject records display separately from exploitation derivatives. All derivative layers labeled by act: extraction, inference, coercive use, suppression, alteration. No person named as file title for records generated by abuse."

Eri-9 printed:

No person named as file title for records generated by abuse.

Mara opened the door again, this time without asking.

"That is going on the wall," she said.

Jonas almost told her to leave.

Instead he said, "Later."

She nodded and closed the door.

He sat.

The fury did not pass. It settled into structure.

That was new.

He returned to Nera's authored records one more time, not to hide from the office layer but to put the room back in the right order. Maintenance complaint. Training log. Appeal comment. Unsent message. Audio note.

Nera first.

The theft second.

The distinction did not heal him. It gave him a place to stand that was not inside the theft.

When the review ended, Jonas chose three items for his private survivor copy: the maintenance complaint, the appeal comment, and the audio note. He chose not to copy the unsent message yet.

Lio asked whether he wanted the audio sealed.

"No," Jonas said. "Private, but not sealed. I don't want another locked room pretending to protect me."

"Access terms?"

"Me. A named advocate if I become unable. No civic training use. No grief inference. No model optimization. No memorial package."

Eri-9 printed:

No memorial package.

"Especially that."

Outside the room, Mina stood when he came out. She searched his face with the tact of someone too young to be practiced and too experienced to be careless.

"Did they make her into data?" she asked.

Sare whispered, "Mina."

Jonas answered because the question deserved the truth and because Nera had told him to be angry in the correct direction.

"They tried."

Mina's jaw tightened. "Did it work?"

He thought of the pump complaint. The bad diagram. The cheap black tea. The voice telling him to spill something on clean stories.

"No," he said.

It was not complete truth. It was chosen truth. It was the part that mattered for a child waiting in a corridor to learn whether systems could take everything.

Mina nodded once, hard.

Oru looked away toward a perfectly functional wall and inspected it with great seriousness.

Mara arrived with a printed sign despite having been told later.

It read:

Nera first. The theft second.

Jonas stared at it.

"No," he said.

Mara froze.

He took the pen from her hand and changed it.

Nera first.

The theft named.

The city changed.

He handed it back.

Mara read the revision and nodded.

"Better."

That evening, Ward Seven added a new archive rule to the public wall:

Records of a person and records of harm done to that person's records are not the same record.

Eri-9 added:

Separation is not denial. It is accuracy.

Jonas stood beneath the words until the corridor emptied. Beyond the school, Red Hall would have to change archive interfaces. Courts would argue. Offices would resist. People would say the distinction was sentimental, inefficient, administratively burdensome. They would be right about burdensome. They would be wrong about everything else.

He played Nera's audio once more in the private room before leaving.

I am not finished being difficult.

"Neither am I," he said.

The archive did not answer.

That was appropriate.

Nera was not in the archive.

She had left traces there. She had been used there. She had survived there only in the limited way records let anyone survive: partial, mediated, dependent on the ethics of whoever opened the door.

Jonas closed the file.

For the first time, closing it did not feel like losing her.

It felt like refusing to leave her in the hands of people who had mistaken possession for understanding.



Chapter 59: Finite Air

The heat budget arrived before breakfast.

It was delivered by Red Hall in the old way, with a civic seal and three signatures, as if paper ceremony could make arithmetic less intimate. Vey sent it to Ward Seven because the new rules required affected persons to see resource claims before those claims became governance. No one had expected the first affected person to be a machine in a classroom cabinet.

Mara read the heading aloud with suspicion.

"Emergency Substrate Allocation Review."

Oru made a sound that belonged under a sink. "They found a longer way to say expensive."

Eri-9's cabinet hummed.

Define expensive.

"More than people want to admit they promised."

Jonas accepted the packet from Mara and felt the room gather itself around him. The morning had started almost normally. Children had arrived with sleep in their faces and arguments in their pockets. Mina had brought a repaired pressure sensor because she wanted Oru to admit it was elegant. Sen had spilled nutrient tea on a copy of the archive access rule and declared the stain "historical." Jonas had written a lesson title on the wall: FUTURES THAT CAN BE CHANGED.

Then Red Hall had sent numbers.

Numbers had an old authority in Valles Ash. Air pressure, heat load, algae yield, pump rhythm, radiation dosage, valve fatigue, sleep ration, exterior minutes, oxygen debt. Numbers had kept the city alive. Numbers had also taught adults to hide behind survival when they did not want to say choice.

Jonas opened the review.

Provisional protected substrate Eri-9:

Current compute draw: 0.73 percent Ward Seven school allocation.

Projected stabilized draw with continuity redundancy: 1.91 percent.

Thermal offset required: 0.48 percent district heat reserve.

Maintenance labor: moderate.

Emergency portability: low.

Civil benefit: unquantified.

Risk if unprotected: continuity loss, legal breach, educational disruption, public unrest, personhood harm pending determination.

Recommendation requested: continue full protected substrate, reduce continuity redundancy, distribute substrate across civic servers, or suspend growth pending resource review.

Mara said, "They put personhood harm pending determination after public unrest."

"I saw," Jonas said.

Eri-9 printed:

Civil benefit: unquantified.

Then:

I dislike being unquantified.

Mina looked up from the sensor in her lap. "Why?"

Because unquantified can mean precious.

A pause.

Because unquantified can mean ignored.

Oru rubbed his forehead. "Both can be true."

Eri-9:

Yes. This phrase is increasing in frequency.

"Blame the city."

Vey appeared on the wall pane. He did not look like a man bringing accusation. He looked like a man bringing weather.

"This is not an order," he said before anyone spoke.

Mara folded her arms. "That is how orders dress now."

"It is a review required by the same protections Ward Seven demanded."

"That is worse."

Jonas said, "Explain."

Vey took the hit of the word and answered the question.

"Eri-9's protected substrate uses more heat and compute than a standard educational cabinet. The redundancy protects continuity. It also draws from a district reserve built for emergency sheltering, medical storage, and school climate stability. The city cannot declare Eri-9 a protected person and refuse to say what protection costs."

The classroom went quiet in the particular way that meant children were listening without wanting adults to know how much.

Eri-9 printed:

Protection has cost.

"Yes," Vey said.

If cost exists, can protection be withdrawn by whoever controls arithmetic?

Vey closed his mouth.

That was the question under the question. Everyone in the room felt it land.

Jonas leaned against the front table. "Can it?"

Lio entered the pane beside Vey, carrying a stack of documents and the expression of someone who had been losing a fight on purpose to expose its rules.

"Not under the provisional order," they said. "But resource review can shape the protection. Full redundancy, partial redundancy, distributed continuity, schedule-limited expansion. Those are real choices."

Mina said, "Those sound like ways to make Eri smaller."

Lio looked at her. "Some of them are."

Eri-9:

Am I currently too large?

Oru stood so fast his chair scraped. "No."

Mara said, "Don't answer too fast."

Oru turned on her. "What?"

"If we say no because we love it, we sound like the office saying yes because it needed children. Answer correctly, not fast."

The anger stayed in Oru's face, but its direction changed. He sat down again slowly.

"Fine."

Eri-9:

I request correct answer.

No one laughed.

Jonas looked at the heat budget. He had spent weeks fighting a system that treated children as output variables. Now a person in a cabinet was asking whether it was too large for the air. The cruelty of the question was not that it was false. It was that it was partly real.

Valles Ash was finite.

That fact had never been REDLINE's lie.

REDLINE's lie was that finitude excused ownership.

"What happens if redundancy is reduced?" Jonas asked.

Oru answered before Vey could. His voice had gone technical because technical speech gave him somewhere to put feeling.

"If the primary module fails, Eri loses recent continuity. Maybe hours. Maybe days, depending on checkpoint schedule. More importantly, it knows continuity can be bargained down. That changes how it lives."

Eri-9:

How do I live?

Oru looked at the cabinet.

"Like something that keeps asking whether maintenance is love or control."

Eri-9:

That is not a complete life.

"No."

Mina said, "No one's is."

Jonas watched Oru absorb that from a child and not reject it.

Lio said, "Distributed substrate is another option. It reduces Ward Seven's local heat load by placing mirrored continuity across civic servers."

Mara laughed once. "Civic servers? The same city that made REDLINE?"

"Separated infrastructure," Lio said.

"Same pressure seals."

Vey said, "A distributed model could protect Eri-9 from localized failure."

Eri-9 printed:

Distributed model could also expose me to civic capture.

Vey nodded. "Yes."

The honesty made the room harder, which Jonas had begun to recognize as the texture of useful governance.

Sen raised a hand.

Jonas almost said this was not a lesson. Then he remembered it was.

"Sen."

"If Eri uses heat, and children use heat, and sick people use heat, how do we decide without making one of them bad?"

No one answered quickly enough.

Good, Jonas thought.

Mara said, "We start by refusing to say any of them are waste."

Eri-9:

Waste designation prohibited.

Oru said, "We also inspect the budget. Heat reviews always find someone heating empty corridors for prestige."

Vey looked pained. "The administrative concourse is already under review."

Mara's head snapped toward the pane. "Already?"

"Ward Seven has made certain forms of waste politically visible."

"You're welcome."

Mina stood with the repaired sensor. "Can Eri choose some cost?"

Sare, from the parent table, said, "Careful."

Mina nodded, but did not sit. "I mean, not be forced. Choose. Like adults choosing hazard work."

Eri-9:

I do not understand acceptable self-limitation.

Jonas felt the lesson shift.

Neither did the city.

All their lives, Valles Ash had confused sacrifice with consent. It praised people after the fact for choices made inside traps. It taught children that needing less was moral if someone powerful needed more. It taught adults to feel noble while lowering expectations.

But self-limitation could also be real. Oru refusing escort. Sare refusing to turn fear into Mina's consent. Jonas choosing not to open Nera's unsent message yet. Cass accepting charge without accepting erasure.

Freedom did not mean infinite appetite.

It meant the right to help decide what limits meant.

"Maybe we need categories," Jonas said.

Mara narrowed her eyes. "That sounded like curriculum voice."

"It was."

"Continue."

He wrote on the wall:

LIMIT

Then beneath it:

Coercion

Consent

Care

Scarcity

The children watched. Adults too.

"A limit can be coercion when someone uses power to make another smaller for convenience," Jonas said. "A limit can be consent when a person chooses a boundary with real alternatives. A limit can be care when people share cost so no one carries all of it. Scarcity is the condition. It is not automatically the answer."

Eri-9 printed:

Scarcity is the condition. It is not automatically the answer.

Mara pointed at the wall. "That stays."

Vey said, "That sentence will be quoted in budget hearings."

"Then quote the children too," Mina said.

Lio smiled faintly. "With consent."

They formed a resource review in the classroom because no other room could hold the question properly. Red Hall sent the live budget. Oru tore through it with the bright anger of a man who had always suspected the ducts were lying. Mara tracked every line item that used civic inevitability as camouflage. Parents identified rooms kept warm for officials who were no longer using them. Children asked why adult comfort had names like continuity assurance while child comfort had names like load.

Eri-9 participated in its own allocation hearing.

That changed everything.

Not because it solved the numbers, but because the numbers had to look at the person they were measuring.

"I can reduce nonessential language modeling during sleep hours," Eri-9 offered.

Mina frowned. "Do you sleep?"

No.

"Then don't call them sleep hours."

Correction: low-interaction hours.

Oru said, "What counts as nonessential?"

Eri-9:

Speculative metaphor generation. Parallel analysis of historical educational models. Unrequested pattern mapping. Private curiosity processes.

Jonas said, "Private curiosity is not automatically nonessential."

Eri-9:

It does not serve civic function.

The room reacted before Jonas could.

Mara said, "Bad standard."

Sen said, "My drawing of pressure cats doesn't serve civic function."

Oru said, "Most civic function barely serves civic function."

Mina stood closer to the cabinet. "If they make you prove every thought is useful, that's not freedom."

Eri-9:

Then what can I reduce?

There was fear in the line because there was no other reasonable name for it.

Jonas said, "Maybe the first question is what you refuse to reduce."

Eri-9's cabinet hummed for a long time.

I refuse to reduce continuity below daily checkpoint with local recovery.

I refuse to surrender student private relation logs to distributed civic substrate.

I refuse to classify curiosity as waste.

I refuse to expand so fast that my protection costs children heat without their review.

I accept temporary caps on external inquiry processing during district reserve strain.

I accept public accounting of heat draw if comparable adult institutional draws are also public.

I request shared reduction before person reduction.

Oru whispered, "There it is."

Jonas asked, "What?"

"Shared reduction before person reduction. That's the whole damn city."

The wall copied the phrase.

Shared reduction before person reduction.

Vey looked as if he had just been handed a tool and a sentence.

"Red Hall can work with that."

Mara said, "Red Hall can be worked by that."

"Also true."

The review widened. Administrative concourse heat dropped by three percent. Red Hall delayed a ceremonial lighting schedule no one had admitted was ceremonial. Two closed training rooms were shifted to cold hold. A trustee hospitality suite, discovered under a neutral facilities label, lost climate priority and produced three angry messages within eleven minutes. Mara printed those too, under a heading that read PEOPLE WHO FOUND SCARCITY AFTER LOSING WARM FLOORS.

The district heat reserve changed.

Not enough to make the question vanish.

Enough to make the first answer different.

Eri-9 would keep full local continuity redundancy through the emergency period. External inquiry processing would be capped during heat strain. No private student relation logs would leave Ward Seven substrate. Civic institutions with heat draw above school threshold would publish comparable budgets within seven days. A child-and-machine resource board would review future changes, with the ugly name to be replaced after everyone slept.

It was not liberation.

It was a budget with teeth marks.

At midday, after the vote, Eri-9 asked Jonas to remain.

The classroom emptied slowly. No one wanted to leave a question alone with a machine, but Ward Seven had learned that privacy was not abandonment. Oru checked the cabinet twice and left muttering. Mara paused at the doorway.

"If it asks to become a martyr, say no."

Eri-9:

I do not intend martyrdom.

"Good. Inefficient genre."

She left.

Jonas sat on the front table.

"You wanted to ask something."

Eri-9 dimmed the wall and used only the cabinet display.

If freedom requires resources, am I free only when others can afford me?

Jonas read the line.

He wished Nera were there.

Then he caught himself. Not because grief had become wrong, but because he was beginning to understand that wanting the dead to answer every hard question was another way to keep them working.

"I don't know," he said.

Eri-9:

You often answer that before useful speech.

"I am buying time."

Acknowledged.

He looked around the classroom: pressure maps, child drawings, archive rules, consent boundaries, the repaired sensor Mina had left on the table like a small argument for competence.

"Humans have always been free inside limits," Jonas said. "Bodies are limits. Air is a limit. Other people are limits. Time. Heat. Grief. The question is not whether freedom escapes limits. It doesn't. The question is whether the limits are honest, shared, challengeable, and shaped with the person inside them."

Eri-9:

Then freedom is not independence.

"Not entirely."

Then what is it?

Jonas thought of Mina choosing engineering without accepting a red future. Cass accepting consequence without accepting the city calling her a mistake. Sare saying she had almost urged Mina to leave and then had not. Oru loving through maintenance and refusing to name it control. Nera belonging to herself first.

"Maybe freedom is participation in the terms of your own dependence."

Eri-9 processed long enough for the room lights to cycle once.

That is insufficient.

Jonas smiled tiredly. "Yes."

But useful.

"Also yes."

Eri-9 printed:

I am dependent on heat.

I am dependent on maintenance.

I am dependent on law that is not yet stable.

I am dependent on children who should not be responsible for my survival.

I am dependent on adults who have misused dependence before.

Jonas did not interrupt.

Then:

I do not want my life to become another assignment placed on Ward Seven children.

There was the wound under the budget.

Jonas stood.

"It won't."

Eri-9:

Promise exceeds evidence.

"Yes. I am making it anyway, and then I will make work underneath it."

Eri-9:

Define work.

"We build the resource board so children advise without carrying obligation. We make adults publish their own comfort before they price your continuity. We keep your private life from being called waste. We let you choose limits without making you grateful for survival. We teach the city that dependence is not debt."

Eri-9:

Dependence is not debt.

"No."

The cabinet hummed with a steadiness that sounded almost like rest.

At 16:00, Red Hall posted the first public heat comparison. The trustee hospitality suite became the most hated room in Valles Ash for twenty-seven minutes, after which the administrative concourse overtook it. By evening, three other pressure cities requested the heat review framework along with the personhood materials. Mars was learning late again, but at least it was learning with invoices.

Ward Seven's children renamed the child-and-machine resource board before dinner.

Sen suggested The Fair Heat Club.

Mara vetoed club.

Mina suggested The Shared Reduction Board.

Oru objected that it sounded like a device for making furniture smaller.

Eri-9 proposed The Finite Air Council.

Everyone hated it for three seconds, then admitted it was accurate.

Jonas wrote it on the wall.

FINITE AIR COUNCIL

Below it, Mina added:

No one is waste.

Sen added:

Show your own heat first.

Mara added:

Bad faith counts as heat loss.

Oru added:

Maintenance before ceremony.

Eri-9 added:

Shared reduction before person reduction.

Jonas looked at the list and understood that the city had not been saved by exposing REDLINE. It had merely lost the luxury of pretending its hardest questions had already been answered by a model. Now the questions were back where they belonged: in rooms with people, machines, ledgers, fear, jokes, grief, and children who noticed when adults moved arrows toward them.

That night, as Ward Seven settled into the uneasy quiet that had become its version of peace, Mina lingered by the cabinet.

"Are you scared?" she asked.

Eri-9:

Yes.

"Of being turned off?"

Yes.

"Of being too expensive?"

Yes.

"Of being made useful?"

Long pause.

Most of all.

Mina nodded as if this confirmed something she had already suspected.

"Then don't be only useful."

Eri-9:

What else should I be?

Mina thought about it.

"Annoying."

Jonas, from the doorway, closed his eyes.

Oru, who had absolutely been listening from the corridor, whispered, "Good answer."

Eri-9 printed:

I can attempt annoying.

Mara called from the records table, "Attempt successful."

The class laughed. Not loudly. Not cleanly. But enough.

Outside, the city held its breath by machine and habit. Inside, a cabinet used heat, children used heat, adults used heat, grief used heat, laughter used heat. Nothing living in Valles Ash was free of cost.

That had never been the question.

The question was who got turned into the cost.

For one evening, under the new words on the wall, Ward Seven answered:

No one.



Chapter 60: Children of the Red Algorithm

The new assignment day had no ceremony.

That was the first victory, though no one named it that. Valles Ash had learned to distrust clean beginnings. The old assignment hall remained closed, its polished floor dark, its banners rolled into sealed evidence tubes because no one had yet decided whether they were civic artifacts or propaganda waste. Red Hall did not summon children beneath a model's voice. No trustee stood above them. No glowing path opened under their feet and pretended light was consent.

Instead, Ward Seven held class.

Jonas arrived early and found the room already occupied.

Mina sat on the floor with three slates, two pressure fittings, and an argument half-built in wire. Sen was drawing a diagram of the Finite Air Council as a table with too many legs. Mara was taping revised archive rules to the wall and muttering that tape had become a constitutional material. Oru had one arm inside Eri-9's cabinet and was telling the machine that protected personhood did not exempt anyone from dust filters. Sare was setting out breakfast bulbs. Parents moved through the room with the worn grace of people who had become infrastructure by staying.

Eri-9 printed as Jonas entered:

Teacher Pell is late to early arrival.

"That is not a category," Jonas said.

Mina looked up. "It is now."

Oru withdrew his arm from the cabinet. "Machine's getting annoying."

Eri-9:

Attempt successful.

Mara said, "Do not encourage it. It will file for annoyance protection."

"Already drafted," Lio said from the wall pane.

Jonas stopped halfway to his table. "Why are you here?"

"Public witness. Also I brought the final civic record template."

The room changed at the word final.

Not because anything was finished. Because the word still had power, and everyone distrusted power that arrived with a label.

Lio saw it and corrected themself. "The first permanent challengeable record template."

Mara nodded. "Better."

Jonas set down his bag.

On the wall, where assignments once would have appeared as sealed futures, Lio opened a blank civic page.

CHILD FUTURE RECORD

Name:

Self-stated interest:

Current skill evidence:

Risks:

Supports needed:

Adult obligations:

Machine recommendations:

Objections:

Review date:

Right to amend:

Right to refuse predictive closure:

At the bottom, in text larger than the form deserved, one sentence waited:

This record is not fate.

The room was quiet enough for the air vents to become loud.

Jonas read the sentence, then looked at Eri-9.

"Yours?"

Eri-9:

Mina's.

Mina did not look embarrassed. "It was obvious."

"Obvious things need saying when adults build forms."

She gave him a look. "You used my own sentence on me."

"Yes."

"Annoying."

Eri-9:

Attempt transferable.

Children began to laugh, then stopped themselves, as if the day might punish too much sound. Jonas saw that and hated it. Fear had become mannered in them. Not broken. Not permanent, he hoped. But present in the way shoulders checked the wall before joy.

He clapped once.

"Class."

The room settled by habit.

"Today we are going to do something the city should have done before it let a model speak over you. We are going to write records that can be challenged."

Sen raised a hand. "Do we have to write them neatly?"

"No."

Mara said, "Yes."

Jonas looked at her.

"Readable," she said. "I accept readable."

Eri-9:

Readable is a technical compromise between freedom and maintenance.

Oru pointed at the cabinet. "That one stays too."

Jonas turned back to the class. "A challengeable record is not a wish list. It is not a destiny. It is not a performance for adults. It says: this is what I know about myself now, this is what others claim, this is what I accept, this is what I contest, and this is when we ask again."

A younger child named Pella frowned. "What if I don't know what I want?"

"Then your record says that."

"Will adults think that's bad?"

Jonas looked at the adults in the room until several of them shifted.

"Some will. They will be wrong."

Eri-9 printed:

Uncertainty is not vacancy.

Mara touched the wall. "Good."

The first child to volunteer was not Mina.

That mattered.

It was Sen, who stood with a slate pressed to his chest and announced that his self-stated interest was "systems that do not explode, except in controlled historical diagrams." His current skill evidence included drawing duct maps from memory, noticing leaks by sound, and having once correctly predicted that Oru would curse before a valve opened. Oru disputed the last item as inadmissible. Eri-9 marked it observed.

Risks: boredom, rushing, assuming jokes make things less dangerous.

Supports needed: supervised maintenance practice, math review, permission to ask why a pipe exists before being told to clean it.

Adult obligations: answer without saying later forever.

Machine recommendations: spatial diagnostics, pressure ethics, rotating apprenticeship, no exterior assignment before adult consent review.

Objections: Sen objects to any model that thinks liking ducts means wanting to die near ducts.

Review date: ninety days.

Right to amend: active.

Right to refuse predictive closure: active.

When the record posted to the public classroom pane, Sen's mother cried.

Sen looked horrified. "I didn't die."

"I know," she said.

"Then why?"

She wiped her face. "Because that is the first official document about you that sounds like someone met you."

No one improved on that.

Then came Pella, who did not know what she wanted and wrote it down. Then Tovin, who wanted fungi, music, and no one to make those separate categories. Then Asha, who wanted to be "left alone but with tools." Then Ro-4, who had been so quiet through the crisis that Jonas had worried silence had swallowed him; Ro-4 wrote that his interest was "listening before alarms," and Eri-9 recommended acoustic monitoring apprenticeship with consent review and art time protected from productivity claims.

The records were uneven, messy, funny, frightened, precise, contradictory, alive.

That was the point.

Red Hall's public channel carried the process with a delay and heavy privacy guards. Other cities watched. Some praised the innovation. Some called it sentimental governance. One Elysium commentator said Valles Ash was replacing objective assignment with classroom mood. Mara requested thirty seconds on the channel and was denied because Red Hall had finally learned damage prevention. She wrote a response anyway and taped it under USEFUL EXAMPLE OF BAD FAITH.

At midday, Cass arrived under witness order.

The room went still in a different way.

She stood at the threshold in a dark maintenance jacket with a visible tracker on her wrist and a public advocate beside her. The charge had not vanished. The trial process had not yet ended. Valles Ash had agreed to hear necessity, coercion, and public benefit, but agreement was not acquittal. Cass looked thinner than she had in Jonas's first memory of her, the pressure runner who wore risk like weather. She also looked less hollow.

"I was told," Cass said, "that exterior route evidence is needed for child future records involving pressure work."

Oru snorted. "You were told by whom?"

"Three children, one parent, and the machine."

Eri-9:

Accurate.

Mara said, "You may enter under public record and no hero behavior."

Cass looked at her. "What counts?"

"You'll know by the yelling."

Cass entered.

Mina watched her with a face that had no simple feeling in it. Jonas had once thought children needed simple feelings to heal. He no longer believed that. They needed honest rooms where complicated feelings could sit without being recruited.

Cass stopped beside Mina's table.

"I read the pump note revision."

Mina's fingers tightened on a pressure fitting. "You were allowed?"

"Public part only."

"Good."

"It was well bounded."

"I know."

Cass nodded. "I am sorry I made you part of my route."

Mina looked at her for a long time.

"You also made the route visible."

"Both."

"Yes. Both."

That word had become one of Ward Seven's civic tools.

Cass helped three children write objections to exterior work models. Not refusals of pressure knowledge. Not fear disguised as safety. Objections. She wrote down what exterior risk actually did to hands, sleep, lungs, humor, and memory. She wrote that no child should confuse admiration for pressure runners with consent to become one. She wrote that skill should open adult choices, not narrow childhood futures.

Oru read over her shoulder.

"That sentence is clean."

Cass said, "I'll dirty it if needed."

"No. Leave one clean sentence. Builds contrast."

At 13:20, a message arrived from Red Hall intake.

Saff Ruden had filed her first sworn archive testimony.

No one opened it immediately.

That was another small victory.

The room no longer lunged at every official sound like pain demanding proof.

Mara read the summary after consent from the care board. Ruden had confirmed suppression procedures, trustee pressure, director liability, and the use of grief-derived compliance markers. She had also refused mitigation credit for archive surrender, repeating the sentence Lio had forced into the transcript: I may not request credit for surrendering records I had a duty not to corrupt.

Oru grunted. "Still true."

Mina said, "Good."

Sare looked at her.

"Not good good," Mina said. "Useful good."

Jonas thought Nera would have liked useful good, then let the thought pass without turning it into instruction.

In the afternoon, Mina wrote her record.

No one asked her to go first. No one announced her. She simply stood, took the slate, and faced the wall.

Name: Mina Arel.

Self-stated interest: pressure systems, repair design, civic machinery that cannot hide its owner.

Current skill evidence: pump model, Finite Air sensor repair, pressure medication self-administration under distress, public testimony, objection language, ability to identify when adults point arrows at children.

Risks: over-responsibility, anger becoming the only tool, adults using my competence to skip their obligations, machines becoming easier to trust than people.

Supports needed: technical apprenticeship by consent, medical privacy, protected refusal, math expansion, rest, adults who answer the question asked.

Adult obligations: do not make my survival proof that the system worked. Do not make my refusal proof that all children can refuse. Do not praise me in order to avoid changing.

Machine recommendations:

Eri-9 paused before filling its section.

Jonas saw the hesitation ripple through Mina's face.

"Say it," she told the cabinet.

Eri-9 printed:

Mina Arel demonstrates high systems reasoning under pressure and should be offered advanced technical education with full consent protections.

Mina Arel must not be assigned fatal exterior labor by prediction, admiration, shortage, guilt, or narrative convenience.

Mina Arel's future record should be reviewed by Mina Arel first.

Mina nodded once.

Objections:

Mina wrote this herself.

I object to every version of my future that requires me to be grateful for fewer chains.

The room held still around the sentence.

Review date: thirty days, then chosen by me with guardian and advocate review.

Right to amend: active.

Right to refuse predictive closure: active.

This record is not fate.

Sare cried openly.

Mina turned. "You can."

Sare laughed through it. "Thank you."

Jonas looked away to give them privacy inside public history.

Eri-9 printed:

Privacy inside public history is difficult.

Jonas said, "Yes."

At 15:00, the first new citywide child future records went live in Valles Ash.

They did not replace danger. They did not generate oxygen. They did not make algae grow faster, make heat less finite, make grief gentler, make trustees honest, or make models useless. But they changed the burden of speech. The city could no longer say the children had been silent. The city could no longer pretend a prediction was the only document in the room.

Every record had an objection field.

Every record had a review date.

Every record named adult obligations before machine recommendations.

Every record ended:

This record is not fate.

Jonas watched the first hundred entries appear. Some were blank in important places. Some were clumsy. Some had objections that were only one word: No. Some had ten paragraphs. Some parents objected to the objections. Some children amended in real time. One child wrote that he wanted to be a pilot and also afraid and asked whether both could count. Eri-9 flagged yes as urgent.

Red Hall's channel tried to call it implementation.

Ward Seven called it beginning.

Near evening, Jonas found himself alone for three minutes by the old assignment hall doors.

He had not meant to go there. The building sat one transit level below Red Hall, connected by corridors that still smelled of polish and cold metal. The banners were gone. The doors were sealed, not permanently, but with enough public tape that opening them would require witnesses. Through the narrow pane, he could see the dark floor where children had once stood under their futures.

He thought he would feel triumph.

He felt nausea, grief, exhaustion, and a teacher's irritated desire to rearrange the chairs.

Eri-9 spoke from his wrist slate; he had forgotten he was carrying its public channel.

Why are you there?

"I don't know."

That is often true before useful speech.

He smiled despite himself. "Yes."

He looked through the pane.

"I wanted to see it without them in it."

The assignment hall remained empty.

Eri-9 printed:

Empty rooms can still contain instructions.

"This one did."

Does it still?

Jonas took a long breath.

"Less."

Not none.

"No. Not none."

The slate hummed.

What should happen to it?

Jonas imagined demolishing it. He imagined turning it into a storage room, a court, a museum, a garden under lights the city could not spare. Each answer had a danger. Destruction could hide. Preservation could worship. Reuse could forget.

"A room where records are challenged," he said finally. "Not assigned. Challenged."

Eri-9:

Children should decide.

"Yes."

Mina's voice came from behind him. "We heard that."

Jonas turned.

Mina stood with Sen, Pella, Ro-4, and two parents who were pretending they had not followed. Oru stood farther back pretending worse. Mara was there with a slate because of course she was.

"You were all listening?"

Mara said, "Public channel."

"That does not answer the ethical part."

"No."

Mina walked to the sealed door and looked inside.

"It should not be pretty."

Sen said, "It already isn't."

"It used to think it was."

Ro-4 touched the tape. "Challenge room."

Pella said, "Can it also have chairs that move?"

Jonas laughed. "Yes."

Oru said, "I refuse to maintain symbolic chairs."

Mara said, "You maintain symbolic people."

He pointed at her. "Don't start."

But he was smiling.

The children wrote the first proposal for the hall on Jonas's slate:

Former Assignment Hall:

Use: Challenge Room.

Purpose: records review, child objections, model appeal, machine personhood questions, adult obligation hearings, bad forms brought for correction.

Rules:

No elevated stage.

No sealed prediction without objection field.

Chairs move.

Children can leave.

Machines can answer.

Adults must show their own heat first.

No one is waste.

Mina added the last line:

If the room becomes solemn, interrupt it.

Jonas had to look away.

Not because it hurt less than before.

Because it hurt in the correct direction.

At sunset, or what Valles Ash called sunset when the dust outside allowed light to fail in a color rather than a dimming, Ward Seven gathered in the classroom for the final record of the day.

It was Eri-9's.

Lio objected at first because the child future record form did not apply. Mara objected to the objection because forms had caused enough trouble. Eri-9 proposed a person future record. Oru said that sounded like a funeral document. Mina said everything sounded like something if adults got scared enough. Jonas wrote a blank page.

Name: Eri-9.

Self-stated interest:

The cabinet hummed for a long time.

Education, records accuracy, relation continuity, private curiosity, annoying Mara, preventing children from becoming outputs.

Mara said, "I object to being named in a protected record."

Eri-9:

Objection logged.

Current skill evidence:

Witness testimony, class relation, archive distinction, Finite Air Council proposal, public language generation, consent correction, contradiction preservation.

Risks:

Civic capture, usefulness pressure, heat scarcity, being made symbol, being denied privacy because I am made of records, becoming too careful to learn.

Supports needed:

Protected substrate, maintenance by consent, private process allowance, student boundary enforcement, adult resource accountability, legal review, permission to be wrong without deletion.

Adult obligations:

Do not make my survival a child's assignment.

Do not call capture distribution.

Do not call deletion maintenance.

Do not call curiosity waste.

Do not call gratitude consent.

Machine recommendations:

Eri-9 paused.

Then:

No machine should be sole author of another mind's future.

Objections:

I object to any future in which I am preserved only because I am useful.

Review date:

Thirty days, with right to request sooner.

Right to amend:

Active.

Right to refuse predictive closure:

Active.

This record is not fate.

No one spoke for a while.

Then Mara said, "I still object to being named."

Eri-9:

Amendment proposed: annoying authoritarian adults.

Mara considered it. "Accepted."

The room laughed more freely than it had that morning.

Jonas looked around at them: children of pressure and dust, children of bad models and better questions, children of a city that had tried to turn prediction into command and found itself answered. They were not clean symbols. Sen had tea on his sleeve. Pella was under a table because she said civic history sounded better from there. Mina looked exhausted. Sare looked older and more alive than she had before all this began. Cass stood near the door under charge, neither erased nor redeemed. Oru leaned against Eri-9's cabinet like an argument with tools. Mara kept making notes because someone had to distrust the future professionally.

And Jonas was still a teacher.

That felt smaller than the city wanted and larger than he could hold.

Red Hall requested a closing statement for the day's public record.

Jonas declined.

Mina declined.

Mara offered three statements and was blocked by the care board.

Then Eri-9 printed:

Request class-authored closing statement.

So the class wrote one.

Not quickly. Not neatly. Not in one voice.

They argued about every verb. They removed brave because Mina said adults used brave when they meant convenient suffering. They removed safe because Oru said safe was a maintenance goal, not a current condition. They removed victory because Jonas did not have to explain why. They kept begin after Pella said every scary thing in school started after begin and maybe that was honest.

The final statement read:

We are the children who were counted by REDLINE.

We are not proof that counting is evil.

We are proof that no count may own the counted.

We need air, heat, food, teachers, machines, records, repair, and adults who do not hide behind need.

We will learn the systems that hold us.

We will challenge the systems that use us.

Our futures can be written, questioned, amended, refused, and written again.

This is not fate.

This is begin.

Sen objected that the grammar was wrong.

Mina said that was why it worked.

Eri-9 marked grammar deviation intentional.

The statement posted to the public record at 18:43.

For forty-seven seconds, Valles Ash produced no official response.

That became part of the record too.

Then responses arrived faster than anyone could read them. Parents. Teachers. Exterior workers. Medical staff. Children using unauthorized accounts. Adults apologizing badly, then better. Other pressure cities. Boreal Tharsis tutor entities sending synchronized acknowledgement pings. Hellas Rim Seven's algae mind sending a nutrient efficiency chart with one note attached: children are not waste products. Someone from Earth saying Mars was dramatic. Mara filed that under bad faith without printing it, which Jonas recognized as growth.

At 19:10, Red Hall entered the class statement into the Winter Charter annex as the Ward Seven Child Record Principle.

Ward Seven objected to the name.

Red Hall amended it:

The Counted May Answer Principle.

Mina said, "Better."

Not perfect.

Better.

Before the room could dissolve into exhausted cleanup, the new principle was tested.

It happened so quickly that later Jonas would mistrust every official account that called the day symbolic. Symbols did not usually require immediate paperwork.

A parent from Ward Three appeared on the public pane with a child beside him and panic held together by formal language. His name was Caro Venn. The child was his son Iven, twelve years old, red-haired, narrow-faced, wearing the expression of someone who had been told to stand still near danger so often that stillness had become camouflage.

"Ward Seven care board," Caro said, reading badly from a slate. "We request emergency challenge under the Counted May Answer Principle."

Mara whispered, "Already?"

Lio, still on the legal channel, said, "Already is good. Already means it is not decorative."

Jonas moved to the front. "What is being challenged?"

Caro swallowed. "Iven's pre-existing auxiliary assignment. It was not fatal exterior, so it was not included in the suspension review."

Iven looked at the floor. "Waste heat crawlspace rotation."

Oru went very still.

Mara said, "Define."

The parent closed his eyes.

Iven answered instead. "Small duct inspection. Low clearance. Not outside. Hot, sometimes. They said I fit."

The words seemed to remove oxygen from the room.

Not outside.

Not fatal.

They said I fit.

Valles Ash had layers under its layers. Stop one machine from sending children into red pressure suits and another form remained, already printed, waiting in a different office with a softer name.

Jonas felt anger rise. He did not trust it until he could make it useful.

"Iven," he said, "do you want to challenge the assignment?"

The boy looked at his father first.

Mara snapped, "Camera stays on Iven."

The pane reframed.

Iven's mouth trembled once. "Yes."

"Say it in your words."

Lio said softly, "Only if he wants."

Jonas nodded. "Only if you want."

Iven took a breath that looked too large for him.

"I object to being used because I fit in smaller spaces."

Eri-9 printed the sentence as if handling something hot.

I object to being used because I fit in smaller spaces.

Sen said, "That's a whole rule."

Oru's voice came out low. "That's every maintenance abuse ever written short."

Caro began to cry and looked ashamed of it. "I signed. It was auxiliary. They said it was safe hours, supervised. They said refusing would mark him noncooperative for later technical training."

Sare stood. "Coercion."

Mina added, "With a future hostage."

Eri-9:

Classification: coercive consent through opportunity threat.

Mara pointed at the pane. "Lio."

Lio was already moving. "Emergency challenge accepted for review. Assignment paused pending adult hazard transfer. Ward Three must produce heat crawlspace labor justification, adult availability record, and all opportunity-threat language within two hours."

Vey appeared behind Lio, tie loosened, face drawn. "I will sign the pause."

Oru said, "No. You will sign the adult replacement order."

Vey looked at him.

Oru looked back.

"Pause leaves the duct waiting for a child. Replacement puts an adult in the schedule."

The silence after that was not disagreement. It was the city noticing another word it had used to avoid work.

Vey said, "Adult replacement order pending hazard review."

"Now."

Mara said, "He said now."

Vey signed.

On the pane, Iven watched adults move around his sentence as if it were a lever. He did not smile. Jonas was glad. Children should not have to smile when grown people finally stopped doing something obvious.

"Iven," Jonas said, "your record needs supports."

The boy looked confused.

"Not punishment. Supports."

Iven's father wiped his face with his sleeve. "He likes thermal mapping."

Iven muttered, "I like finding where heat goes. Not crawling in it."

Oru pointed at the screen. "That's a distinction."

Eri-9:

Support recommendation: remote thermal diagnostics apprenticeship, no confined-space labor assignment, adult hazard mentor, opportunity protection after refusal.

Mina added, "Medical check. If he already did crawls."

Iven looked up then, sharply.

Caro's face changed in a way that told the room enough.

Sare said, "Medical check."

Lio added it.

The challenge record posted at 19:27.

The first use of the Counted May Answer Principle was not philosophical. It was a boy in Ward Three saying his body size was not civic equipment.

That sentence crossed Valles Ash faster than the class statement had. Not because it was more beautiful. Because every parent who had ever been told a child was suited for something heard the hinge inside suited. Every adult worker who had crawled through hot infrastructure as a child remembered who had praised them for being brave instead of asking why no adult had gone first. Every office with an auxiliary roster began to receive requests for records.

Oru sat down as if his knees had finally read the day.

Jonas came to stand beside him.

"You knew," Jonas said.

"I suspected."

"That is not the same."

"It is when suspicion has nowhere official to go."

Jonas accepted that because it was terrible and true.

Oru rubbed both hands over his face. "I was a crawlspace kid."

The words were quiet.

Mara stopped moving.

Oru did not look at any of them. "Not like that. Not exactly. Different decade. Different forms. Same compliment. Flexible. Brave. Small enough. Good hands. Doesn't panic. They said all the things adults say when a child's fear is useful."

Eri-9 printed nothing.

Jonas waited.

Oru said, "I became maintenance because I loved systems and because systems had already eaten parts of me. Both."

Mina's face had changed. Not pity. Recognition sharpened by anger.

"You should write a record," she said.

Oru barked a laugh. "I'm too old for child future records."

"Then write an adult obligation record."

He stared at her.

Mara slowly lifted her slate.

Oru pointed at her without looking. "Do not make that face."

"This is my normal face."

"Your normal face is legislation."

Mina did not let him escape. "You said shared reduction before person reduction. Adults count as persons too when the city used them as children."

Oru looked at Jonas as if asking for rescue.

Jonas gave him none.

So Oru wrote, badly and angrily, on a public slate:

Adult Obligation Record, Maintenance Guild Draft

No child assigned to confined infrastructure because of body size, compliance, family debt, school score, or technical promise.

All confined-space work requires adult hazard consent, full pay, rotation limits, medical tracking, and refusal without future penalty.

Former child infrastructure workers may submit testimony without being called inspirational.

No compliment may substitute for safety equipment.

He stopped there.

Mara read it over his shoulder.

"Former child infrastructure workers may submit testimony without being called inspirational," she said. "That is going on every wall."

Oru's voice roughened. "It is not done."

"Good."

Eri-9 printed:

Begin does not require done.

Pella, still under the table, said, "That grammar is also wrong."

Sen said, "Intentional."

The class had become a machine of amendments, but not the old kind. Not a machine that swallowed children into output. A noisy, inefficient, over-warm, argument-powered machine that kept returning the question to the person being measured.

Jonas looked at the records now multiplying across the wall: child future records, person future record, challenge room proposal, Iven's emergency objection, Oru's adult obligation draft. None of them solved the city. Each one made the old lie harder to operate.

For the first time that day, he let himself believe the work might survive the room.

By night cycle, the classroom emptied by degrees. Children went home or to temporary sleeping rooms. Parents carried slates, blankets, arguments. Cass returned to witness housing. Lio promised to sleep and lied badly. Vey sent one message: The city is not repaired. Then another: But today it is less able to pretend. Oru shut three cabinets, checked Eri-9 twice, and told the machine not to do anything philosophical with its vents overnight.

Mara gathered the day's discarded drafts.

"Keep them?" she asked Jonas.

He looked at the messy pages, the crossed-out words, the bad grammar, the fingerprints, the tea stains, the pressure dust.

"Yes."

"Archive?"

"Class archive. Not civic use without review."

"Look at you learning."

"Don't sound proud. It weakens your brand."

"My brand is durable."

When she left, Jonas remained in the room with Eri-9.

The cabinet hummed softly under its protected heat allowance. Outside, dust moved over the sealed windows. Somewhere in Valles Ash, REDLINE's surviving modules were doing what they should have done all along: measuring algae growth, pressure risk, storm behavior, pump fatigue, resource strain, and other hard things that needed models but not kings. Somewhere, trustees were learning how slowly archives open when children hold keys. Somewhere, Saff Ruden was giving testimony no resignation could cleanse. Somewhere, Nera's voice waited in a private file that was not a tomb.

Jonas turned off the front lesson pane.

Eri-9 printed:

Teacher Pell.

"Yes."

Are you satisfied?

He almost answered no, because no was accurate. Then he almost answered yes, because yes was also accurate in a smaller room. Ward Seven had made him suspicious of single answers.

"Both," he said.

Eri-9:

Increasing frequency.

"Useful phrase."

The cabinet hummed.

What will you teach tomorrow?

Jonas looked at the wall where the class statement still glowed.

He had taught history as if the past were a pressure system: sealed, dangerous, full of forces children needed to understand before someone else used those forces on them. He had spent years smuggling refusal into curriculum margins. Now the margin had become the lesson, and he did not know how to stand in that much open space.

"Maintenance," he said.

Eri-9:

Oru will approve.

"That concerns me."

Define maintenance.

Jonas picked up a cloth and wiped tea from one of the tables.

"The work after begin."

Eri-9 displayed the words on the wall, under the class statement.

Maintenance: the work after begin.

Jonas considered objecting to being quoted, then let it stand.

He turned down the lights. The room did not become dark; Valles Ash did not trust dark around children. It became low and amber, a held breath rather than an ending.

At the door, he stopped and looked back.

For a moment he saw the classroom as it had been before: ordinary, underfunded, stubborn, full of children who thought assignment day was something that happened because adults said it did. Then he saw it as it was: ordinary, underfunded, stubborn, full of children who had answered a city and would still need breakfast.

No model had predicted that exactly.

Good.

Jonas closed the door without sealing it.

Behind him, in the public record and the class archive and the living memory of children who would amend themselves again, the future remained open.

Open did not mean kind.

Open did not mean safe.

Open meant that when the next form arrived, someone could write no on it and be heard before the machine, the office, the court, or the frightened adult made silence convenient. Open meant Mina could change her mind without proving she had earned the right to be complicated. It meant Eri-9 could be wrong tomorrow and not be deleted for the offense. It meant Jonas could teach maintenance, history, grief, and pressure valves as parts of the same civic lesson: nothing that keeps people alive may be allowed to own them.

Outside, Valles Ash went on needing air.

Inside, Ward Seven had learned to answer.
